The Copyright Law Review Committee is a specialist advisory
body established in 1983 to inquire into and report to Government on specific
ISBN 0 642 20955 3
In 1983 the Federal Attorney-General established the Copyright Law Review
Committee as a specialist advisory body to report to government
on specific
From 1983 to 1995 the Committee was chaired by the
Hon. Justice IF Sheppard AO, a Judge of the Federal Court of
Australia and President
of the Copyright Tribunal. During that period the
Committee reported on eight references: the meaning of ‘publication’
; the use of copyright material by churches;
performers’ protection; moral rights; the importation provisions of the
Act; conversion
damages; journalists’ copyright; and computer software
protection.
In 1995 the Minister for Justice reconstituted the Committee and asked it to
advise on simplification of the
. Mr Peter Banki, formerly a member
of the Committee, was appointed Chairman. Following the 1996 federal election,
the Attorney-General
reviewed the structure and function of the Committee and
relocated its Secretariat to Canberra. In May 1996 Mr Banki resigned from
In September 1996 Professor Dennis Pearce was appointed Chairman of the
Committee. The other members of the Committee are Dr Andrew
Christie, Mr Chris
Creswell, Mr Malcolm Colless, Ms Victoria Rubensohn and Ms Rhonda Smith.
Expert Advisory Group
The Committee is assisted by a panel of experts who represent the interests
of the creators and users of copyright material. The current
members of the
Expert Advisory Group (EAG) are Ms Libby Baulch, Mr Tom Cochrane and Ms Anne
Fitzgerald. Mr Nicholas Hasluck QC AM
was also a member of the EAG until 31
December 1997, when he sought not to be reappointed.

1 The Committee’s task
1.01 Following the
September 1996 appointment of Professor Pearce as Chairman of the Copyright Law
Review Committee, the Attorney-General
asked the Committee to reconsider its
terms of reference so as to focus on simplification of the legislation, having
full regard
to Australia’s international obligations, rather than conduct
a more general and wide-ranging review of copyright protection.
In December 1996
the Attorney-General announced revised terms of reference for the
Committee—see Appendix A.
terms of reference
1.02 This report deals with matters raised for the Committee’s
consideration in paragraph 1(a) of the terms of reference. The
Committee will
shortly produce a further report dealing with matters raised by the remainder of
the terms of reference, including
the exclusive rights comprising copyright,
categorisation of subject matter, and the outstanding matters identified by the
Convergence Group (CCG) in its 1994 report entitled
Highways to
1.03 Paragraph 1(a) asks that the Committee, having regard to
Australia’s international obligations as a member of the relevant
international conventions for the protection of copyright, to inquire into and
how to simplify the
to make it able to be
understood by people needing to understand their rights and obligations under
the Act, with particular attention
to simplification of the various provisions
and schemes that provide exceptions to the exclusive rights comprising
1.04 In September 1997 the Committee’s revised terms of reference were
further amended to include paragraph 1(ba). The new paragraph
requires that the
Committee examine how the exclusive rights comprising copyright, as redefined
under paragraph 1(b), could implement
the right of distribution provided for in
art. 6 of the World Intellectual Property Organisation (WIPO) Copyright
Treaty (the WCT)
and arts. 8 and 12 of the WIPO Performances and Phonograms
Treaty (the WPPT).
1.05 The following section summarises Australia’s international
obligations.
International
obligations
1.06 The rights accorded copyright owners under Australia’s Copyright
Act derive from a number of international agreements for
the protection of
intellectual property to which this country is a party. The permissible scope of
any statutory exceptions to those
rights must also be determined by reference to
the exceptions allowed for in those agreements.
1.07 For the purpose of considering permissible exceptions to the exclusive
rights of copyright owners the following international
agreements are
the Berne Convention for the
Protection of Literary and Artistic Works (the Berne
Convention);
the Rome Convention for
the Protection of Performers, Producers of Phonograms and Broadcasting
Organisations (the Rome
Convention);
the Convention for the
Protection of Producers of Phonograms against Unauthorised Duplication of their
Phonograms (the Phonograms
Convention);
the Agreement on
Trade-Related Aspects of Intellectual Property Rights (the TRIPS
Agreement).
1.08 In December 1996 two new treaties were agreed to
at the WIPO Diplomatic Conference on Certain Copyright and Neighbouring Rights
Questions held in Geneva. Those treaties are the WCT and the WPPT. Neither
treaty is yet in force since in each case 30 countries
must have deposited
instruments of ratification or accession for this to occur. Australia is yet to
sign or ratify the treaties,
so the obligations they contain do not apply to
Australia. The Committee understands, however, that the Government is
considering
whether Australia should become a party to the two new treaties. For
this reason, and pursuant to the requirement in the Committee’s
reference to have regard to ‘ongoing developments in the relevant
international copyright forums’, the Committee
considered the potential
impact of the treaties’ provisions—see Appendix B.
1.09 Australia is a party to the TRIPS Agreement, which incorporates the
requirements of the Berne Convention. The TRIPS Agreement
defines the scope of
permissible exceptions to the exclusive rights of authors at the international
level. Article 13 of the Agreement
provides as follows:
Members shall confine limitations or exceptions to exclusive rights to
certain special cases which do not conflict with a normal exploitation
work and do not unreasonably prejudice the legitimate interests of the right
1.10 The Berne Convention contains a similar provision in art. 9(2)
concerning the reproduction right and provision for a number of
other exceptions
to the reproduction and other rights in arts. 10, 10
(2)(b). It also allows the replacement of the broadcasting right and
the exclusive right of recording of musical works by non-voluntary
(2) and 13(1)).
1.11 The Committee notes the report of a study by the WIPO International
Bureau on the implications of the TRIPS Agreement for treaties
administered by
The study examined the relationship
between art. 13 of TRIPS and the various exceptions to authors’
rights permitted under
Berne. The Bureau commented,
None of the limitations and exceptions permitted by the Berne Convention
should, if correctly applied, conflict with the normal exploitation
of the work
and none of them should, if correctly applied, prejudice unreasonably the
legitimate interests of the right holder.
Thus generally and normally, there is no conflict between the Berne
Convention and the TRIPS Agreement as far as exceptions and limitations
exclusive rights are concerned.
1.12 The Committee agrees with these statements as an interpretation of the
relationship between the narrower limitations provided
for in Berne and those in
TRIPS. The Committee also notes that art. 2(1) of TRIPS provides that
nothing therein ‘shall derogate
from existing obligations that Members may
have to each other under ... the Berne Convention ...’ This suggests
that authors
from Berne Convention member countries remain entitled to the same
minimum level of protection, in any Berne Convention member country,
at the time that country assumed its TRIPS obligations.
1.13 Article 10 of the WCT and art. 13 of TRIPS are worded similarly.
The agreed statement at the Diplomatic Conference regarding
art. 10(2) of
the WCT was that it ‘neither reduces nor extends the scope of
applicability of the limitations and exceptions
permitted by the Berne
Convention’.
1.14 Accordingly, it is clear that the international consensus regarding the
interpretation of art. 10 of the WCT is that the limitations
allowed for go no
further than those already specified in the Berne Convention. If Australia
becomes a party to the WCT it would
appear that it would be bound to confine any
limitations to those already permitted under Berne and could go no further.
1.15 The rights granted to performers, producers of phonograms, and
broadcasting organisations under the Rome Convention, the TRIPS
Agreement and
the Phonograms Convention are all expressed to be subject to the exceptions
allowed under the Rome Convention and it
is those exceptions that currently
apply. If, however, the Government were to decide that Australia should become a
party to the
WPPT it appears it would no longer be open to Australia to provide
for the specific classes of exceptions listed in art. 15(1) of
Convention, since art. 16 of the WPPT confines the permissible exceptions
to those allowed under the three-step test in
the TRIPS art. 13.
of the reference to date
1.16 During 1997 the Committee published a series of short issues papers
inviting submissions on areas of relevance to its simplification
reference. The
papers considered more specific matters relating to the scope and form of
protection, as well as the exclusive rights
and the exceptions provided under
the Act. Five papers were published:
Simplification of the Fair
Dealing Provisions of the
Copying by Libraries and
Archives under the
Copying for People with
Disabilities under the
Educational Institutions and
Copying under the
Legal Deposit of Copyright
Material under the
1.17 On 28 April 1997
the Committee, in conjunction with the Attorney-General’s Department, held
a day-long public forum in
Sydney on access to copyright materials. The aim was
to facilitate public discussion of matters arising from the five papers. Over
100 people attended the forum, many of whom represented peak copyright industry
bodies and user organisations. The Committee invited
those attending the forum
to make further submissions to it on the matters discussed at the forum. The
Committee considered those
submissions, and the submissions it had received
before the forum, in formulating this report.
1.18 The Committee wrote to interested parties on 17 November 1997, seeking
further submissions concerning the miscellaneous exceptions
in the Act not
referred to in its issues papers. The submissions received on this subject were
also taken into account in the drafting
of this report.
1.19 It should be noted that this report does not examine the issues relating
to the legal deposit of copyright material under the
Act: this matter is not
directly relevant to the Committee’s consideration of paragraph 1(a) of
its terms of reference. These
issues will, however, be examined in a further
Committee report, to be published shortly.
1.20 On 30 April 1998 the Attorney-General and the Minister for
Communications, the Information Economy and the Arts announced the
Government's
decision on the ‘Digital Agenda’ copyright reforms. The Digital
Agenda reforms implement many of the proposals
contained in a discussion paper
jointly published in 1997 by the Attorney-General’s Department and the
Department of Communications
and the Arts.
centrepiece of the Digital Agenda reforms is a new broadly based,
technology-neutral right of communication to the public. The
new right will
apply to works made available on the Internet and other online services, as well
as works transmitted or broadcast
to the public. It has broad application,
applying to works, sound recordings, cinematograph films and broadcasts. It will
and extend the existing technology-specific transmission-type rights;
that is, the wireless broadcast right and the limited cable
diffusion right.
1.21 In drafting this report, and pursuant to paragraph 2(a) of its terms of
reference, the Committee had regard to the Government’s
proposed reforms,
as just described. In particular, it examined how the simplified exceptions to
the exclusive rights of copyright
owners would apply to the proposed right of
communication to the public. The Committee has partly reserved its comment in
to the impact of the Digital Agenda reforms on the exceptions connected
with copying for educational purposes. It considers that
it would be
inappropriate for it to set out, in this report, the detail of a compulsory
licence for digital copying when the Government
has now stated that it proposes
to deal with the matter.
1.22 In drafting this report the Committee also took account of the
Amendment Bill 1997
and the Copyright Amendment Bill (No. 2) 1997. In
particular, it examined the impact of Schedules 1, 6 and 10 of the former Bill.
The Committee also took account of Items 1, 2 and 3 of Schedule 1 to
Intellectual Property Laws Amendment Bill 1997 in connection with
amendments to the
regarding references to professional advice by a
‘trade mark attorney’.

of fair dealing

be simplified by
consolidating the current fair
dealing provisions
single section;
absorbing the
provisions relating to acts done for the purpose of professional advice in
relation to subject matter other than works
(ss. 104(b)
(c)) within
fair dealing;
removing the fair dealing
provisions that specifically apply to external students
(ss. 40(1A)
removing the provisions that
require sufficient acknowledgment in relation to fair dealings for the purpose
of reporting news
(ss. 42(1)(a)
adopting a modified
quantitative test
(s. 40(3)).

Committee recommends that the proposed fair dealing provision, and the details
of the modified quantitative test, remain
in the Act. [paragraph 6.31]
of fair dealing purposes

model that specifically refers to the current exclusive
purposes—such as research or study

currently considered in relation to ‘a dealing
by way of copying’
with a work for the purpose of research or study
generally to all fair dealings. [paragraph 6.44]
quantitative test
2.05 The Committee recommends that its analysis be taken into account in the
next stage of the Government’s consideration of
the Digital Agenda.
[paragraph 6.66]
test—‘prescribed portion’
2.06 The Committee recommends that the quantitative test, and the concept of
a ‘reasonable portion’, as it is currently
called, be treated
separately from the concept of a ‘fair dealing’ since there is no
requirement for recourse to the
criteria of ‘fairness’. The
Committee therefore
the structure of the new fair
dealing provision reflect this distinction and that the quantitative test be
removed from ‘fair
dealing’ and drafted as a separate stand-alone
provision on permitted free copying;
‘reasonable portion’ be renamed a ‘prescribed portion’,
to distinguish it more clearly from the general
principle of fair dealing.
[paragraph 6.68]
2.07 Additionally, the Committee recommends that
the provision just described expressly prohibit the serial copying of
10 per cent
portions. [paragraph 6.69]
2.08 In the light of the inherent difficulties that exist in the extension of
the quantitative test to all subject matter, including
material in the digital
environment, the Committee recommends that the operation of the quantitative
test be defined exhaustively
and be specifically limited to published literary,
dramatic and musical works in printed form. [paragraph 6.71]
2.09 The Committee therefore recommends the insertion of the word
‘printed’ before the terms ‘periodical publication’
‘published edition’ (see the discussion of the definition of
‘prescribed portion’ in paragraph 6.145).
[paragraph 6.72]
Application
of the modified quantitative test to all dealings
2.10 With the exception of two members, the Committee recommends that the
current limitation on the application of the quantitative
test to a dealing
‘by way of copying’ be removed, thereby allowing the quantitative
test to apply to
all dealings
with printed published literary, dramatic
or musical works, or adaptations of such works, for the purpose of research or
study. [paragraph
2.11 In dissenting from the foregoing recommendation one Committee member
recommends the extension of the quantitative test to all
dealings except those
that involve the reproduction of copyright material from hard-copy form to
digital form. The other dissenting
Committee member recommends that the
quantitative test remain limited to dealings ‘by way of copying’.
[paragraph 6.78]
2.12 If the recommendation to apply the quantitative test to all dealings is
not adopted, the Committee (with the exception of the
two dissenting members)
recommends that the term ‘reproduction’ be used instead of the term
‘copying’, so
as to ensure application of the test to the widest
possible range of reproductions. [paragraph 6.86]

ROYALTY-FREE
EXCEPTIONS TO COPYRIGHT
1 Fair dealing
3 International comparisons
In considering simplification of the fair dealing provisions in the
, the Committee examined the copyright legislation of a
number of countries. This chapter briefly considers this legislation as it
relates to the principle of fair dealing.
3.01 The free use of copyright material is called ‘fair dealing’
in the United Kingdom and in UK-derived legislation (such
as Australian and New
Zealand legislation); it is called ‘fair use’ under US law. In other
legislation it is described
as ‘restrictions’ on or
‘exceptions’ to rights.
3.02 In considering simplification of the various limitations to the rights
of copyright owners, including the fair dealing scheme
in the Australian Act,
the Committee examined the copyright legislation of a number of countries: the
United States, the United Kingdom,
New Zealand, France, Germany, Canada, and
Denmark and Finland (see Appendix C).
3.03 Of the legislation examined, it was found that only the US legislation
contains an open-ended fair use scheme; the other countries
have opted for a
closed set of specific exceptions, which is similar to the current approach in
3.04 The Committee also found that—apart from Australia—the
United States and New Zealand were the only countries of those
considered whose
legislation implements a general statutory test of ‘fairness’ to
determine whether or not exceptions
to copyright owners’ exclusive rights
apply. Under s. 40(2) of the Australian Act, ‘fairness’, in
relation to a
‘dealing by way of copying’ for the purpose of
research or study, is determined by reference to a non-exclusive set of
(see paragraph 4.08).
3.05 Section 107 of the US
provides for a set
of non-exclusive factors to be considered in determining whether or not a
particular use is a ‘fair use’.
The Australian guidelines under
s. 40(2), which apply to fair dealing for the purpose of research or study,
are similar to the factors
included under s. 107 of the US Act. The notable
difference between the US and Australian set of factors is the existence of
s. 40(2)(c)
of the Australian Act, which has no counterpart in the US
legislation. Section 40(2)(c) provides for a court to consider ‘the
possibility of obtaining the work or adaptation within a reasonable time at an
ordinary commercial price’.
3.06 The New Zealand
includes under s. 43(3) a
set of factors that are very similar to the factors found under s. 40(2) of
the Australian Act. The Committee
notes, however, that under the New Zealand Act
the list appears to be drafted as an
set of factors. This must
be contrasted with the Australian and US legislation, which provides for a
list of considerations.
3.07 The Committee found that none of the countries examined provided for a
general quantitative test for fairness similar to that
found under s. 40(3)
of the Australian Act. The international comparison was therefore of no
assistance in determining how the Australian
quantitative test could be applied
to copyright material in the digital environment.
3.08 The Australian fair dealing exceptions appear to give users greater
rights to copy than those applicable in the United Kingdom,
New Zealand and
Canada. Among other limitations to fair dealing not found in the Australian Act,
s. 29 of the UK
of the New Zealand Act, and s. 27(2)(a) of the Canadian Act refer to a fair
dealing in relation to research and/or ‘
study’. This
limitation of ‘private’ before the word ‘study’ was
removed from the Australian Act following
Further limitations to fair dealing
found in the UK and New Zealand Acts and not found in the Australian Act are
discussed in Appendix
3.09 A varied international approach exists in relation to the exceptions
providing for decompilation of computer programs for the
interoperability. The Committee found that the US, Canadian and New Zealand
specific exceptions that allow this
activity. Case law has, however, established that under the US Act decompilation
may be considered
in some cases to be a fair use. The UK Act, whilst
specifically providing that it is not a fair dealing to decompile
(s. 29(4)(a)),
allows for decompilation as a separate exception under
s. 50B. The legislation of Germany (art. 69(e)), France (art. L.122-6-1),
Denmark (art. 37) and Finland (art. 25k) permits the decompilation of a computer
3.10 The German, Danish and Finnish legislation specifically provides for the
protection of the moral rights of copyright owners whilst
applying the
exceptions (described as ‘limitations’) to owners’ economic
4 Fair dealing in Australia
This chapter provides an overview of the fair dealing provisions in
Australia’s
. First, it considers the basic
application of the provisions in terms of the subject matter of copyright and
exclusive rights comprising
provisions as they relate to fair dealing for the purpose of research or study,
criticism or review, reporting news, and giving professional advice. Included in
this examination is a consideration of the factors
to be taken into account in
determining what is a fair dealing. The chapter also examines the quantitative
test to be applied in
determining what is a fair dealing in relation to the
copying of a published literary, dramatic or musical work for the purpose of
research or study. Finally, the main judicial decisions relating to the
operation of these provisions are discussed.
4.01 Under Australia’s Copyright Act a range of statutory limitations
to the exclusive rights of copyright owners may be raised
in answer to a claim
of infringement of copyright. The most important of these is the statutory
limitation of ‘fair dealing’,
which allows the free copying and use
of copyright material without formality. Strictly speaking, fair dealing is not
a defence to
infringement; rather, it defines the boundaries of copyright
owners’ rights.
4.02 Under the Act the doctrine of fair dealing is confined to four
research or study (ss. 40 and
criticism or review (ss. 41
reporting of news
(ss. 42 and 103B);
professional
advice given by a legal practitioner or patent attorney
(s. 43(2)).
4.03 Sections 40 to 43 apply fair dealing to
works and ss. 103A to 103C apply the limitation to an ‘audio-visual
which is defined in s. 100A as ‘a sound recording, a
cinematograph film, a sound broadcast or a television broadcast’.
formulation is based on the distinction that currently exists in the Act between
‘works’ (Part III) and ‘subject
matter other than works’
4.04 What particular acts constitute a fair dealing are not defined in the
Act; with the exception of one member, the Committee is
of the view that fair
dealing can apply broadly to all the exclusive rights subsisting in the
particular subject matter that falls
within the exception. The Committee
majority notes that use of the expression ‘a dealing by way of
copying’ in ss. 40(2)
and 40(3) does not operate to limit fair
dealing for the purpose of research or study (s. 40(1)) to the copyright
owner’s right
of reproduction.
s. 43(2), which provides for fair dealing for the purpose of giving
professional advice by a legal practitioner or patent
attorney, should be
interpreted similarly (notwithstanding the reference in the heading of
reproduction
for purpose of ... professional
advice’, which, as a matter of statutory interpretation, must give way to
the text of s. 43(2)).
For these reasons the Committee majority takes the
view that fair dealing applies to the exercise of all rights.
4.05 The Committee majority considers that this broad application of the fair
dealing provisions to all exclusive rights is consistent
with Australia’s
international obligations under the Berne Convention and TRIPS (see Appendix
dealing for research or study
4.06 Section 40(1) of the Act provides, ‘A fair dealing with a
literary, dramatic, musical or artistic work, or with an adaptation
literary, dramatic or musical work, for the purpose of research or study does
not constitute an infringement of the copyright
in the work.’
Section 103C(1) is in similar terms in relation to dealings with
‘audio-visual items’ (see paragraph 4.03).
4.07 The relevant provisions of the Act relating to research or study are
expanded by a non-exclusive set of factors to be taken into
determining whether a dealing is fair (ss. 40(2) and 103C(2)).
4.08 Section 40(2) provides,
For the purposes of this Act, the matters to which regard shall be had, in
determining whether a dealing with a literary, dramatic,
musical or artistic
work or with an adaptation of a literary, dramatic or musical work, being a
dealing by way of copying the whole
or a part of the work or adaptation,
constitutes a fair dealing with the work or adaptation for the purpose of
research or study
(a) the purpose and character of the dealing;
(b) the nature of the work or adaptation;
(c) the possibility of obtaining the work or adaptation within a reasonable
time at an ordinary commercial price;
(d) the effect of the dealing upon the potential market for, or value of, the
work or adaptation; and
(e) in a case where part only of the work or adaptation is copied—the
amount and substantiality of the part copied taken in
relation to the whole work
or adaptation.
Section 103C(2) is in similar terms in relation to dealings with
‘audio-visual items’.
4.09 Section 40(2) is based on a recommendation in the Franki report that the
concept of ‘fair dealing’ be retained in
The recommendation was based
substantially on principles developed in the case law. The Franki Committee also
made brief reference
to the then yet-to-be-enacted s. 107 of the US
(s. 22), passed by the Senate and before a Committee of the House of
Representatives.
It is reasonable to assume
that the factors referred to under s. 40(2) are also relevant in
determining the fairness of a dealing
for purposes other than research or study:
both the principles in the case law and the factors described under s. 107
of the US Copyright
Act are not limited to a specific purpose.
4.10 As part of its recommendation, the Franki Committee commented on why the
matters now in s. 40(2) do not provide specific guidance
as to what is a
fair dealing for the purposes of research or study:
We see section 40 as being a section mainly directed to the acts of an
individual, and there are so many factors which may have to
be considered in
deciding whether a particular instance of copying is ‘fair dealing’,
that we think it is quite impracticable
to attempt to remove entirely from the
Court the duty of deciding the question whether or not a particular instance
constitutes ‘fair
4.11 The determination of what is a fair dealing in relation to the copying
of a work for the purpose of research or study is assisted
by a quantitative
test provided under s. 40(3) of the Act, which provides that, subject to
certain qualifications, the copying of
the whole or part of an article in a
periodical publication or the copying of not more than a ‘reasonable
portion’ of
a work or adaptation is deemed to be fair, notwithstanding the
factors to be taken into account in determining fairness under s.
4.12 A ‘reasonable portion’ is defined non-exhaustively in
s. 10(2) of the Act. The definition provides that a copy will
‘reasonable portion’ if the pages copied, in relation to a published
edition of a literary, dramatic or musical
(a) do not exceed, in the aggregate, 10% of the number of pages in that
edition; or
(b) in a case where the work is divided into chapters exceed, in the
aggregate, 10% of the number of pages in that edition but contain
only the whole
or part of single chapter of the work.
4.13 In relation to ‘audio-visual items’, s. 103C does not
provide a quantitative test equivalent to that provided for
under s. 40(3).
4.14 It should be noted that s. 40(3) applies primarily to published
literary musical and dramatic works through the reference to
a ‘periodical
publication’ (s. 40(3)(a)) and a ‘reasonable portion’
(s. 40(3)(b)) of ‘a published edition’(s.
discussed, the definition of ‘reasonable portion’ under
s. 10(2) is non-exhaustive and so a ‘reasonable
portion’ in
regard to an unpublished work is possible under the Act. In the case of dealings
with more than a ‘reasonable
portion’, there is no express mention
in the Act of what weight (if any) is to be given to the fact that the work is
unpublished.
Whether or not a work is published would, however, probably be
considered under ss. 40(2)(b), 40(2)(c) and 40(2)(d).
4.15 The Copyright Act contains no definition of the term ‘research or
De Garis v Neville Jeffress Pidler Pty Ltd
Beaumont J adopted the
Macquarie Dictionary
definitions of
‘research’ and
and other cases
have consistently held that it
is the purpose of the person making the dealing, rather than the ultimate use to
which the material
is put, that is relevant when assessing whether the dealing
can be regarded as a fair dealing for the purpose of research or
4.16 Before 1980 the word ‘study’ was qualified by the adjective
‘private’. The
deleted the word
‘private’ from s. 40(1) in accordance with a recommendation
made by the Franki Committee. The CLRC noted
Simplification of the Fair
Dealing Provisions
that copyright
owners generally have pressed for the restoration of the word

that concerns were
expressed that the section permitted commercial organisations to engage in

D, paragraphs 29 to 36, for an analysis of the responses the Committee received
4.17 The Franki Committee considered that the limitation was intended to
distinguish use of material for private study from use for
instruction, but it regarded the distinction as artificial and difficult to
maintain. It stated,
It is clear that the photocopying of material is of considerable assistance
in enabling teachers and students to prepare material
for classroom use, and
that it is difficult to maintain a distinction between private study and other
educational purposes. So long
as the photocopying for educational use is
qualified, for the purposes of s. 40, by the requirement of fair dealing,
we think that
the removal of the limitation to private study will not prejudice
owners of copyright.
4.18 The Australian courts are yet to decide a case that determines whether
copying and other such use of copyright material for the
purpose of commercial
research and development could be regarded as falling within the doctrine of
fair dealing. In 1991 Denis Rose
QC, then Chief General Counsel of the
Attorney-General’s Department, prepared an advice in which he stated that
could well confine ‘research’ to activities for the
purpose of increasing knowledge in the community as a whole—by
for instance, with research in a government department for the purpose of
advising ministers on proposed legislation or
research by a manufacturing
company for the purpose of improving its products. This must be contrasted with
the position in New Zealand,
where Blanchard J incidentally expressed the view,
in the decision of
Television New Zealand v Newsmonitor Services
, that a fair dealing for the
purpose of research could be something with a commercial end in view. It also
appears that in the United
Kingdom, commercial research is covered under an
equivalent provision.
dealing for criticism or review
4.19 Under s. 41 of Australia’s Copyright Act, a fair dealing with
a literary, dramatic, musical or artistic work or with an
adaptation of such a
work other than an artistic work does not constitute an infringement of the
the purpose of criticism or review of that
work or of another work. Similarly, s. 103A provides that a fair dealing
with an audiovisual
item does not constitute an infringement of the copyright in
the item or any work or other audiovisual item included in the item
if it is for
the purpose of criticism or review.
4.20 In compliance with the requirement of art. 10(3) of the Berne Convention
the application of both provisions is subject to ‘sufficient
acknowledgment’ of the work being made. A sufficient acknowledgment in
relation to a work is defined under s. 10(1) as an acknowledgment
identifying the work by its title or other description and, subject to certain
conditions, identifying the author. It should be noted
that Australia’s
in relation to subject matter
other than works.
4.21 Unlike fair dealing for the purpose of research or study, there are no
guidelines as to what constitutes fair dealing for the
purpose of criticism or
review. It is necessary to refer to the case law, although the factors described
in s. 40(2) are probably
also relevant in determining
‘fairness’ (see paragraph 4.09). The origins of this exception were
explained by Lord Hatherley
Chatterton v Cave
Books are published with an expectation, if not a desire, that they will be
criticised and reviewed, and if deemed valuable, that
parts of them will be used
as affording illustrations by way of quotation or the like, and if the quantity
taken be neither substantial
nor material, if ... a fair use only be made of the
publication, no wrong is done and no action can be
4.22 This principle was adopted by the Federal Court of Australia in the
4.23 The meaning of ‘criticism or review’ has been considered in
a number of cases. In
Commonwealth of Australia v John Fairfax & Sons
it was held that the work must be used for genuine criticism or review
and cannot be published under the pretence of
Sillitoe v McGraw-Hill Book
Co (UK) Ltd
held, however, that it is not necessary that criticism or review
be the sole purpose of the dealing, provided there is a substantial
criticism or review.

4.24 Under s. 42(1) of the Act a fair dealing with a literary, dramatic,
musical or artistic work, or with an adaptation of such a
work other than an
artistic work, does not constitute an infringement of the copyright in the work
if done for the purpose of, or

a newspaper, magazine or similar

by means of

work forms part of the news being

by means of

4.28 Under s. 43(2) of the Act a fair dealing with a literary, dramatic,
musical or artistic work does not constitute an infringement
of copyright in the
work if it is for the purpose of the giving of professional advice by a legal
practitioner or patent attorney.
4.29 It should be noted that the limitation does not have a ‘fair
dealing’ counterpart in relation to audiovisual items.
Sections 104(b) and
104(c) provide for a broader limitation, however, in relation to subject matter
other than works. The limitation
covers all acts done for the purpose of the
giving of professional advice by a legal practitioner or patent attorney or for
the purpose
of or in the course of the giving of advice by such a person. The
limitation is not subject to a ‘fairness’ requirement
and therefore
is not described as a ‘fair dealing’.
4.30 Ultimately the question of fair dealing is, in all cases, a matter of
fact and impression, which is to be determined by the
This is limited in relation to fair
dealing for the purpose of research or study by application of the quantitative
test under s.
40(3) of the Act, which deems the copying of up to a
specified amount of a work to be a ‘fair dealing’ notwithstanding
the factors described in s. 40(2) (see paragraph 4.11).
5 The US doctrine of ‘fair use’ and the UK ‘fair
dealing’ provisions
The focus of this chapter is s. 107 of the US Copyright Act, which
provides the foundation for the doctrine of ‘fair use’.
The chapter
considers the main similarities with and differences between s. 107 and
fair dealing provisions such as ss. 40(2) and
40(3) of the Australian
. It also briefly considers the rationale behind the
‘fair use’ doctrine. The chapter then examines each of the factors
as being relevant to the determination of what is a fair
dealing in the United States. It considers the way the US courts have applied
the factors and, by way of comparison, the application of these factors by the
Australian and UK courts.
5.01 The US ‘fair use’ doctrine prescribed by s. 107 of the
provides a useful starting point for considering
rationalisation of the Australian fair dealing provisions.
5.02 Section 107 of the US Act provides as follows:
Notwithstanding the provisions of sections 106 and 106A, the fair use of a
] including such use by
reproduction in copies or phonorecords or by any other means specified by that
section, for purposes such
as criticism, comment, news reporting, teaching
(including multiple copies for classroom use), scholarship, or research, is not
infringement of copyright. In determining whether the use made of a work in
any particular case is a fair use the factors to be considered
(1) the purpose and character of the use, including whether such use is of a
commercial nature or is for non-profit educational purposes;
(2) the nature of the copyrighted work;
(3) the amount and substantiality of the portion used in relation to the
(4) the effect of the use upon the potential market for, or value of, the
The fact that a work is unpublished shall not itself bar a finding of fair
use if such finding is made upon consideration of all the
above factors.
5.03 The fair use doctrine is an ‘equitable rule of
Therefore no general
definition is possible and each case is decided on its facts by reference to the
non-exclusive set of factors
provided under s. 107. This produces the most
significant difference between the US fair use limitation and the Australian
fair dealing
provisions: the Australian Act currently confines fair dealing to a
specific set of purposes. The US approach would be consistent
with art. 10 of
the yet-to-be-adopted WCT
(see Appendix B), which was agreed to on the
It is understood that the provisions of Article 10 permit Contracting Parties
to carry forward and
appropriately extend
into the digital environment
limitations and exceptions in their national laws which have been considered
acceptable under the Berne
Convention. Similarly, these provisions should be
understood to allow Contracting Parties to
devise new exceptions
limitations that are appropriate in the digital network
environment.
[emphasis added]
5.04 As noted, the US factors are similar to the guidelines in s. 40(2)
of the Australian Copyright Act, which apply to fair dealing
only for the
purpose of research or study. The notable difference between the US and
Australian sets of factors is the existence
of s. 40(2)(c) of the
Australian Act, which has no counterpart in the US provision. Section 40(2)(c)
provides for consideration by
the court of ‘the possibility of obtaining
the work or adaptation within a reasonable time at an ordinary commercial
5.05 It is important to note that the legislative history of the US Copyright
Act suggests that the availability of a work to the
potential user is an
important factor in determining fair use. US Senate report no. 94-473 stated
that a key, although not necessarily
determinative, factor in fair use is
whether the work is available. If the work is out of print and unavailable for
purchase through
normal channels the user may have more justification for
reproducing it than in the ordinary case, but the existence of organisations
licensed to provide photocopies of out-of-print works at reasonable cost is a
factor for a court to consider.
5.06 In can be concluded therefore that, although s. 107 of the US Act
does not specifically include a factor similar to s. 40(2)(c)
Australian Act, the availability of a work is still a vital factor in the
determination of what is regarded as fair use. It
should be noted, however, that
the factor described in s. 40(2)(c) is more specific than the US approach
since it examines the availability
of a work ‘within a reasonable time at
an ordinary commercial price’. Nevertheless, it could be argued that the
qualification is implicit in an analysis of the US approach and thus
the availability factor in both countries is very similar.
5.07 The US Act does not include an overall quantitative test similar to that
found under s. 40(3) of the Australian Act, which applies
notwithstanding
the factors to be taken into account in determining fairness under
5.08 An analysis of the rationale behind the doctrine of fair use was made in
the case of
American Geophysical Union v Texaco Inc
, where Judge Jacobs
(dissenting) warned,
Nowhere in the case law is there support for the proposition that the
monopoly granted by copyright is designed to ensure the holder
economic return; rather, the law’s purpose is to balance competing
interests—assuring the author of a fair
return, while permitting creative
uses that build upon the author’s work ...
5.09 This analysis should, however, be contrasted with that of the majority
judgment, where Judge Newman held that the purpose of
the creative activity of authors by the provision of a special reward. Judge
Newman stated that the fair
use doctrine tempers the protection of copyright by
allowing the use of limited amounts of copyright material in some
circumstances.
5.10 It would appear to the Copyright Law Review Committee that the rationale
described in the minority judgment is closer to the
justification for the fair
dealing limitation under the Australian Act. The fair dealing provisions of the
Australian Act in relation
to works and cinematograph films are consistent with
the Berne Convention and TRIPS (see Appendix B, paragraphs 4 to 10 and 22 to
24). At the outset of the negotiations that led to the Berne Convention it was
stated that ‘limits to absolute protection are
rightly set by the public
The fair dealing
provisions might therefore be described as prescribing instances in which it is
considered that the ‘public
interest’ should prevail over the
private interests of authors.
purpose and character of the use
5.11 In examining the purpose and character factor in fair use, the concept
of ‘transformity of use’—the distinction
‘productive’ and ‘reproductive’ uses—is a matter
the US courts have held to be central to a
proper analysis of fair
The concept is pivotal to a US
court’s investigation under this factor because it assesses the value
generated by the secondary
use and the means by which such value is generated.
To the extent that the secondary use involves merely an untransformed
duplication,
the value generated by the secondary use is little more than or the
same as the value associated with the
5.12 An untransformed copy or ‘reproductive use’ is likely to be
used for the same purpose as the original, whereas a
‘productive’
use makes some contribution of new intellectual value and thus promotes the
advancement of the arts and
has therefore been held that a use merely for the same ‘intrinsic
purpose’ as the original seriously weakens a claim
In contrast, if the secondary use adds
something new with a further purpose or different character, the value generated
goes beyond
the value of the original and the promotion of the arts and sciences
is furthered.
The US Supreme Court has held
that, although ‘a transformative use is not absolutely necessary for a
finding of fair use ...
the more transformative the new work, the less will be
the significance of other factors, like commercialism, that may weigh against
finding of fair use’.
5.13 The unproductive or uncreative nature of the use was referred to in the
Australian Federal Court decision in the
, whilst analysing the US case law in
relation to fair dealing for the purpose of reporting news.
The UK courts
have often referred to a similar principle—that a mere republication or
commercially competing edition of a work
cannot be justified under the guise of
fair dealing.
5.14 In examining the factor of the purpose and character of the use, the US
courts inquire into whether the use is of a commercial
nature or is not for
profit. If a copyright work is infringed for commercial purposes, there is a
presumption of unfair use and,
if the presumption is not rebutted, fair use
The essence of the distinction
between whether a use is for commercial or non-profit purposes is not whether
the work is solely for
monetary gain but whether the user stands to profit from
exploiting the copyrighted material without paying the customary
Similarly, the UK courts may be
reluctant to regard a dealing as fair if the activity in question is commercial
exploitation to the
economic detriment of the copyright
The Australian courts have held that
the motive with which the material is copied will therefore be a relevant
consideration in the
assessment of
5.15 The Australian courts are yet to decide a case that determines whether
the reproduction of copyright material for the purpose
of commercial research
and development falls within the doctrine of fair dealing for the purpose of
research or study (s. 40—see
paragraph 4.18).
5.16 The commercial use of a work is but one factor the US courts use in
determining fair use; other elements, such as educational
purposes, can outweigh
the commercial use. In the case of
Maxtone-Graham v Burtchaell
commercial nature of a defendant author’s unauthorised publication of
verbatim quotations from the plaintiff’s copyrighted
book did not preclude
a finding of fair use.
The educational
elements of the defendant’s use far outweighed the commercial aspects of
his work. But an educational purpose
does not necessarily lead to a finding of
fair use: in the case of
Association of American Medical Colleges v
it was held that the use of test questions in a commercial course
to prepare students to take a test was not fair
; further, in the case of
Encyclopedia Britannica Education Corp v Crooks
it was held that, even
though films were copied and distributed for educational purposes, the fair use
limitation was inapplicable
because the volume of copying and the extent of
distribution were unreasonable.
of the copyright work
5.17 In determining fair use, the amount of protection a work may receive
varies with the nature of the work.
example, the US Supreme Court held that factual works may be used by others more
freely than fictional works, although a user
may not take more copyrighted
material from a factual work than is needed to convey the
Using this approach, a broader use
of informational works is permitted under fair use, whereas creative,
imaginative and more novel
works are entitled to a high degree of
protection.
Unpublished
5.18 In the case of
Harper & Row Publishers v National Enterprises
the US Supreme Court held that ‘under ordinary circumstances, the
author’s right to control the first public appearance
undisseminated expression will outweigh a claim of fair
In a separate case, the Second
Circuit Court of Appeals held that unpublished works ‘normally enjoy
complete protection against
copying any protected
expression’.
In 1992 the US Congress
responded to concern that as a result of these decisions a rule might have been
created prohibiting the fair

is unpublished shall
not itself bar a finding of fair use if such finding is
made upon consideration of all the above factors.’
5.19 The amendment was not intended to alter the weight currently given by
the courts to the unpublished nature of a work in the fair

general fair use principles set out in the case of

doctrine to unpublished works is narrowly limited since,
although the work is
unavailable, this is the result of a deliberate choice on the part of the

In a subsequent

the work would be a very important

5.26 In the case of

the US Supreme Court held that the use in an article of verbatim extracts

It is impossible to define what is a fair dealing. It must be a question of

A presumption of harm to

In photocopying cases, the

dealing, although not regarded as the single most important

constitutes a ‘fair dealing’ includes examining

5.33 The main difference between the US doctrine of ‘fair use’
and the Australian doctrine of ‘fair dealing’
is that the US
exception is open ended and is not confined to a specified range of purposes. If
it were adopted in Australia, a fair
use limitation as found under the US Act
could result in a wider range of purposes being regarded by the court as falling
fair dealing.
5.34 Another important difference is that the fair use exception makes no
reference to a general quantitative test similar to that
provided for in
s. 40(3) of the Australian
. The US model is therefore not
useful in providing an example of how
could be applied to works in
digital form.
5.35 It has been found that although the factor described in s. 40(2)(c)
of the Australian Act—namely, ‘the possibility
of obtaining the work
or adaptation within a reasonable time at an ordinary commercial
price’—is not included in the
set of factors in s. 107 of the
US Act, the availability of a work is still regarded as an essential element in
the determination
of the ‘fairness’ of a use in the United States.
5.36 Interpretation of the common factors used by the courts in the
determination of what is regarded as a ‘fair use’
dealing’ appears to be based on similar principles. The overriding
principle—that ultimately the question
of fair dealing is ‘a matter
of impression’, which is to be determined by the
—would appear to apply equally
to the ‘fair use’ doctrine.
6 Fair dealing: the Committee’s proposal
This chapter sets out the Committee’s proposal for a revised fair
dealing provision that simplifies the current provisions and
application of fair dealing principles in the digital environment. The
limitations of the current provisions as they apply
to new technology are
examined, and several changes are proposed:
consolidation of the current fair
dealing provisions in a single provision;
expansion of fair dealing to an
open-ended model that specifically refers to, but is not limited to, the current
exclusive set of
application of the ‘fairness’ factors to all fair dealings.
The Committee makes a number of other recommendations aimed at
simplifying and extending the current fair dealing provisions. They
among other things,
changes to the provisions relating
to external students;
fair dealing for
the purposes of criticism or review, reporting news and giving professional
legal advice.
The chapter discusses the Committee’s
proposal for a new quantitative test and the relationship of both fair dealing
and the new quantitative test to the proposed right of communication
to the public recently announced by the Government.
An example of a prototype provision that could be used to implement the
Committee’s recommendations is also provided.
6.01 Paragraph 1(a) of the Committee’s terms of reference directs it to
report on simplifying the
, paying particular attention
to the exceptions to the exclusive rights comprising copyright. Much of the
present complexity in the
fair dealing provisions and the miscellany of other
provisions and schemes that provide for exceptions to copyright owners’
exclusive rights is due to the fact that they operate on the basis of a
particular technology or in relation to dealings with copyright
materials in a
particular material form. Technological developments will no doubt continue, and
they will probably affect copyright
owners and users in ways that are and will
remain unpredictable.
6.02 When considering simplification of the fair dealing provisions, the
Committee sought to achieve a model that is structurally
simpler than the
existing provisions and is also sufficiently flexible to accommodate the
challenges posed by technological developments.
The particular aspect of the
fair dealing provisions that caused the Committee the greatest difficulty is the
so-called quantitative
test in s. 40(3) of the Act and the associated
definition of a ‘reasonable portion’ in
Application of the existing
test to copyright materials in digital form has proved particularly
6.03 The Committee’s approach was to adapt the existing fair dealing
provisions to give them more general application so that
they can operate
effectively in the digital environment and in response to future technological
developments. As noted in paragraph
1.20, the Committee had regard to the
Government’s recent decision on the ‘Digital Agenda’ copyright
reforms, which
it announced on 30 April 1998. The centrepiece of the reforms is
a new broadly based, technology-neutral right of communication to
the public.
The new right will apply to works made available on the Internet and other
online services, as well as works transmitted
or broadcast to the public. It
will replace and extend the existing technology-specific transmission-type
rights; that is, the broadcast
right and the limited cable diffusion right.
6.04 Most importantly, for the purpose of this report, the Committee notes
the Government’s decision that the fair dealing limitation
should apply to
the new right of communication to the public and the digital environment
generally. In this regard the Committee
notes the Government’s decision
that the quantitative test should apply to literary, dramatic and musical works
form where they have been published in hard-copy form.
6.05 In relation to the quantitative test the Committee considered two
approaches. The first involved an examination of each type
of copyright subject
matter and the formulation of specific exceptions tailored to the particular
subject matter. The second approach—which
acknowledged the difficulties in
quantifying copyright materials in digital form as well as the inherent
limitations of the existing
concept of a ‘reasonable
portion’—was to leave dealings with copyright materials (other than
published literary,
dramatic and musical works, or adaptations of such works, in
printed form) to be determined in accordance with the general fair dealing
principles.
6.06 After careful consideration of the alternatives the Committee reached
the view that the latter approach is the most suitable.
Its strengths are that
it is technology neutral and avoids the use of specific provisions to deal with
specific cases. This should
lead to legislation that is simpler and less likely
to need constant revision to keep pace with technological developments.
6.07 The Committee is strongly of the view that an approach that seeks to
deal with each specific case is undesirable. First, it cannot
be comprehensive
in its coverage because it is not possible to predict new uses to which the
technological developments may give
rise (or how they will affect copyright
owners and users). Second, each new circumstance that needs to be dealt with
simply adds
to the complexity of the legislation. The Committee also considers
that the present quantitative test in s. 40(3) of the Act suffers
limitations that make it unsuitable for application to all copyright materials,
particularly, those in digital form. Once these
limitations are recognised, and
having regard to the Committee’s terms of reference (which direct it to
advise on simplifying
the Act), the only choice is to leave dealings with these
materials to be determined in accordance with the general principles of
6.08 Ultimately, the Committee adopted a model that is concise, sufficiently
flexible to accommodate new uses that may emerge with
future technological
developments, but also contains enough detail to provide valuable guidance to
both copyright owners and users.
The Committee’s model is not limited to
an exclusive set of purposes such as govern the extent of fair dealing as it
applies. The Committee considers that the removal of such a limitation
will provide greater flexibility by allowing courts to determine
the existence
of additional purposes that are regarded as falling within fair dealing. Only
this approach will enable fair dealing
to be adapted to changing technology and,
in particular, will move fair dealing into the digital environment. The
Committee’s
recommended model simplifies the existing plethora of fair
dealing provisions and addresses the real limitations of the current provisions,
which are that they are inflexibly linked to specific purposes and are difficult
to apply to new technologies.
6.09 In reaching its conclusion, the Committee took account of the open-ended
US ‘fair use’ doctrine. The Committee acknowledges
that the US model
has had some influence on the application in Australia of the extended fair
dealing doctrine that it is recommending.
It recognises, however, that the
applicability of the US fair use doctrine is qualified by factors germane to the
United States—for
further discussion, see Appendix C, paragraphs 10 to
13—and would expect Australian courts to develop law appropriate to
conditions. It is reasonable to surmise that with time an Australian
jurisprudence on the scope of the extended fair dealing doctrine
will develop.
6.10 The Committee’s recommendations in this chapter provide a means
for the current fair dealing provisions to be simplified
and extended into the
digital environment. The Committee proposes the following changes:
consolidation of the current fair
dealing provisions—ss. 40, 103C, 41, 103A, 42, 103B and
43(2)—in a single
expansion of fair dealing to
an open-ended model that specifically refers to the current exclusive set of
purposes— research
or study (ss. 40 and 103C), criticism or review
(ss. 41 and 103A), reporting news (ss. 42 and 103B) and professional
advice (s. 43(2))—but
is not confined to those purposes;
general application of the
non-exclusive set of factors provided for in s. 40(2) to all fair dealings.
The result of these three changes is to introduce a provision
akin to but more precise than the open-ended US ‘fair use’
(s. 107 of the US
The Committee further proposes the following:
creation of a new quantitative test
based on s. 40(3) of the Australian Act. As a result of the
Committee’s recommendations,
the new test
be specifically limited in
operation to published literary, dramatic and musical works or adaptations of
such works in printed form,
exhaustively through reference to a ‘prescribed portion’ that
reflects the limits currently described in relation
to a ‘reasonable
portion’ (s. 10(2)),
applied to all dealings for the purpose of research or study rather than
dealings by way of copying only (not supported by one
member of the
Committee),
be inserted in the Act as a
stand-alone provision separate from the new fair dealing
operate as a simple deemed
exception notwithstanding the factors described in s. 40(2)—as is
also the current position in relation
application of the
‘fairness’ requirements (s. 40(2)) to acts done for the purpose
of professional legal advice in relation
to subject matter other than works
through the absorption of ss. 104(b) and 104(c) into the fair dealing
removal of the fair dealing
provisions that apply specifically to external students (ss. 40(1A) and
removal of the provisions that
require sufficient acknowledgment in relation to fair dealings for the purpose
of reporting news (ss.
42(1)(a) and 103B(1)(a)) on the basis that the
proposed moral rights provisions will require such acknowledgment.
6.11 Paragraphs 6.143 and 6.144 describe an example of a
prototype provision that could be used to implement these changes.
Justification
6.12 The Committee considers it appropriate to extend the current fair
dealing provisions into the new digital environment. It is
attracted to the
recommended approach for the following reasons.
It continues to build on existing
jurisprudence that has developed around the current fair dealing
provisions.
It is consistent with
Australia’s current international obligations and with the spirit of the
yet-to-be-adopted WCT
It strikes a fair balance between
the competing interests of copyright owners and users and describes the limits
to copyright owners’
rights in a manner that maximises the public
It simplifies the Act.
It is not limited to an exclusive set
of purposes and therefore offers greater flexibility in allowing courts to
determine new circumstances
to which fair dealing can apply in response to
changing technology.
It provides
greater certainty in the determination of ‘fairness’ through the
general application of the non-exclusive
set of considerations (under
s. 40(2)) to all fair dealings.
preserves the valuable guidance provided by the quantitative test under
s. 40(3) through a new test expressly confined to dealings
for the purpose
of research or study of published literary, dramatic or musical works, or
adaptations of such works, in printed
on existing jurisprudence
6.13 An advantage of the recommended approach is that it does not abandon the
case law that has developed in relation to fair dealing.
The Committee considers
this will provide a solid basis on which the recommended fair dealing provision
can be interpreted and will
allow for greater certainty in the application of
the new provision.
International
obligations
6.14 The Committee considers its recommended approach is consistent with
art. 9(2) of the Berne Convention
art. 13 of TRIPS
Australia’s
other international obligations (see Appendix B). The
Committee also considers that, although the new fair dealing model is not
to an exclusive set of purposes, the model’s application is still
confined to
certain special cases
do not conflict with the normal
exploitation of the work
do not unreasonably prejudice the legitimate
interests of the right holder
view is supported by the fact that the similar US ‘fair use’
provision, which also is not limited to an exclusive
set of purposes, has not
been challenged by member countries of the Convention.
6.15 In the Committee’s view the recommended approach is in the spirit
of art. 10 of the yet-to-be-adopted WCT. Article 10 was
agreed to on the
basis of an understanding that
... the provisions of Article 10 permit Contracting Parties to carry forward
and appropriately extend into the digital environment
limitations and exceptions
in their national laws which have been considered acceptable under the Berne
Convention. Similarly, these
provisions should be understood to permit
Contracting Parties to devise new exceptions and limitations that are
appropriate in the
digital network
environment.
The Committee considers that the recommended new fair dealing model is one
such appropriate extension into the digital environment.
public interest
6.16 The prescription of fair dealing involves the broad balancing of
competing goals so as to maximise the public interest. Among
those competing
goals are rewarding creators, increasing investment by copyright industries, and
maintaining access to copyright
Although the public interest is an elusive concept and one on which agreement is
virtually impossible, the Committee considers that
a fundamental component of
maximising the public interest is allowing the free flow of knowledge, ideas and
information. As Sir Anthony
Mason, former Chief Justice of the High Court of
Australia, stated, such a free flow of information is vital to the political,
intellectual,
economic and social life, as well as the education, of all
Australians.
The Committee recognises,
however, that even where copying is being carried out in pursuit of a socially
desirable objective, it
does not follow that it should be permitted if it would
unreasonably prejudice the economic rights or other legitimate interests
6.17 In order to maximise the public interest, the Committee (with the
exception of one member) concluded that fair dealing should
not be limited in
its application to copyright material in ‘traditional’ forms. Such a
limitation would prevent the users
of copyright material taking full advantage
of developments in digital technology and would constrain the free flow of
information.
This majority approach is also consistent with the view stated at
the outset of the negotiations that led to the Berne Convention—that
‘limits to absolute protection are rightly set by the public
6.18 The Committee is aware that digital technology has the potential for
allowing copyright owners, through their respective collecting
societies, to
monitor and license the individual uses of copyright material in a way that was
not previously possible. It also is
aware that this has created new markets for
the use of copyright materials, both whole and in part.
6.19 The Committee majority does not consider, however, that this new
capacity to monitor and license uses of copyright materials
justifies a
limitation on the application of fair dealing so as not to apply to copyright
material in the digital environment. On
the contrary, the majority believes that
the fair dealing provisions are needed to ensure the free use of copyright
material in the
digital environment for purposes that are socially desirable,
especially given that digital technology has the potential to restrict
so as to enforce voluntary licensing agreements.
6.20 Changes in technology have led the Copyright Agency Limited (CAL) to
argue that copying that took place under the fair dealing
provisions and that
was previously regarded by rights holders and users as a subsidiary use is
now—through the advent of paper-based
reproduction and, more recently,
digital reproduction and dissemination of substantial portions of
works—perceived by authors
and publishers as a main
(Appendix D provides an analysis
of the submissions the Committee received on the question of fair dealing.) The
Committee considers
that this matter has been partly dealt with through the
introduction of statutory licence schemes (such as the scheme under Part
the Act, which was introduced in response to photocopying). Such schemes may
restrict the operation of fair dealing where there
is a potential overlap
between a statutory licence and a fair dealing limitation in relation to the
activity covered.
The extension of
statutory licence schemes to include digital reproduction and electronic
distribution would presumably have a similar
effect on the operation of fair
6.21 CAL argued that any digital copying, if allowed to take place without
remuneration, will substantially adversely affect the markets,
or potential
markets, of copyright owners.
It suggested
that a solution to the problems generated by changes in technology, which would
ensure fair remuneration, would be to
replace the fair dealing provisions
with voluntary licences. It argued further that if voluntary licences are not
used a statutory
licence scheme similar to that set out in Part VB of the Act
should be implemented. Finally, CAL submitted that if neither voluntary
statutory licences are used an alternative is the imposition of a levy on
copying equipment such as photocopiers, scanners and
personal computers.
6.22 With the exception of one member, the Committee rejects CAL’s
submission for a scheme of remuneration to replace the free
use of copyright
material under the fair dealing provisions. Although the Committee recognises
that the existence of a statutory
licence may affect the operation of fair
dealing, the majority of the Committee does not agree that such schemes should
be expanded
so as to substitute for fair dealing. The majority considers that
the replacement of fair dealing with remuneratory use would clearly
6.23 The Committee majority notes and agrees with the counter-argument put
forward by the Australian Vice-Chancellors Committee that
the basis for the fair
dealing principle is unaltered by the technology applying to the form of the
expression that is the object
protection.
Submissions from publishers
assert that in the digital environment all possible uses of copyright materials,

short extracts, will potentially be the subject of

constrained

Australians

and that the use
of short extracts be considered fair.
Committee notes, though, that uses of such extracts as are a substantial part of
the copyright material would have to fall within
fair dealing before they were
an exception to copyright.
6.24 The Committee notes CAL’s argument that a position often put
forward to justify the fair dealing provisions is that it
is impossible to
monitor or collect remuneration for copying and that because of changes in
technology this justification no longer
applies. In support of this argument,
CAL makes reference to the Franki report, which stated in relation to
photocopying under the

insuperable practical difficulties in obtaining permission from, and in
remuneration to, the copyright owners in these

Importantly, however,

immediately precedes it:
... we are satisfied that
as a matter of principle
a measure of

private study, to an extent which at least
falls within the present limits of
‘fair dealing’.
The majority of the Committee agrees with the Franki Committee on this
6.25 The Committee also notes comments made by the US Working Group on

(the US White Paper). The Working Group
considered that the development of technological measures for tracking the use
of copyright

6.26 This approach differs from to that taken by the majority of the

to take a position
that leans towards striking the balance in favour of
relation to the application of fair use to the National Information
