legitimate goal of “universal
6.27 In this regard the Committee agrees with Kurtz, who stated that there is
no ‘tax’ in issue and copyright owners have
never been entitled to
an unlimited scope of rent
The Working Group’s
comment also seems to be at odds with the understanding of the international
community, as reflected in
the relevant copyright conventions. The preamble to
the 1996 WCT sets out the international community’s recognition of the
need to ‘... maintain a balance between the rights of authors and the
larger public interest, particularly education, research
and access to
information, as reflected in the Berne Convention ...’
Simplification
6.28 In developing its new model for simplified fair dealing provisions, the
Committee was mindful of its terms of reference, which
direct the Committee to
report on simplifying the Act but do not give it a mandate to undertake a
wholesale review of the Act or
recommend significant changes to the policy
underpinning the law as it currently stands. In simplifying the Act, however,
the Committee
necessarily had to take account of a broad range of policy
considerations. It also had to have regard to the challenges posed by
development of digital technology. In doing so, the Committee sought to
preserve, as appropriate, the policy reflected in the
fair dealing provisions.
Accordingly, in formulating its revisions to the fair dealing provisions, it
sought to simplify the existing
framework and expression, maintain and
accommodate special policy considerations and, at the most basic level, ensure
that the provisions
continue to operate to balance the legitimate interests of
both creators and users of copyright materials.
Recommendation
6.29 The Committee recommends that the fair dealing provisions of the
be simplified by:
consolidating the current fair
dealing provisions
single section;
absorbing the
provisions relating to acts done for the purpose of professional advice in
relation to subject matter other than works
(ss. 104(b)
(c)) within
fair dealing;
removing the fair dealing
provisions that specifically apply to external students
(ss. 40(1A)
removing the provisions that
require sufficient acknowledgment in relation to fair dealings for the purpose
of reporting news
(ss. 42(1)(a)
adopting a modified
quantitative test
(s. 40(3)).
6.30 There was discussion about whether or not the details of the provisions
relating to fair dealing should be placed in the regulations
to the Act.
Although there was some support for this approach, the Committee considers that
access to the legal principles described
in the fair dealing provisions would
not be improved by requiring reference to both the Act and the regulations.
Recommendation
6.31 The Committee recommends that the proposed fair dealing provision, and
the details of the modified quantitative test, remain
in the Act.
6.32 The recommendation in paragraph 6.31 reflects the important role
that the Committee considers fair dealing plays within our system
of copyright.
This would not be recognised by placing the fair dealing provision, or its
detail, in subordinate legislation. The
Committee’s approach implements
the task described in paragraph 1(a) of its terms of reference in that it is
more likely that
users and owners of copyright material will be able to
understand the operation of fair dealing if they need to refer to only one
legislative instrument.
flexibility
6.33 The recommended model is not limited to an exclusive set of purposes
such as are regarded as currently falling within fair dealing.
Committee’s view the removal of such a limitation will provide greater
flexibility by allowing courts to determine the
existence of additional purposes
that are regarded as falling within fair dealing. The Committee considers that
this approach will
enable fair dealing to be adapted by the courts to changing
technology and will move the application of fair dealing into the digital
environment. The recommended approach would therefore answer the criticism that
the current fair dealing provisions are inflexibly
linked to specific purposes
and are difficult to apply to new
technologies.
6.34 The Committee notes that the adoption of an open ‘fair use’
approach was generally supported by copyright users and
criticised by copyright
owners. Although its recommendation would take the Act in the direction of the
US ‘fair use’
doctrine, by preserving the existing elements of fair
dealing it would still be a more precise and recognisable concept than
Recommendation
6.35 The Committee recommends the expansion of fair dealing to an
open-ended model that specifically refers to the current exclusive
purposes—such as research or study (ss. 40 and 103C), criticism or
review (ss. 41 and 103A), reporting news (ss. 42
and 103B) and
professional advice (s. 43(2))—but is not confined to those
application of considerations determining ‘fairness’
6.36 Under the Committee’s recommended model, the non-exclusive set of
factors that are currently considered in relation to
‘a dealing by way of
copying’ with a work for the purpose of research or study (s. 40(2)
of the Act) would be applied
generally to all fair dealings. The Committee notes
that this should not make a major change to the current operation of fair
since these factors have been derived primarily from the case law and
already apply broadly to all fair dealings
(see also paragraph 4.09). The Committee considers, however, that explicit
application of the factors described under s. 40(2) to
all fair dealings
will provide greater predictability in the determination of fairness, through
the provision of a non-exhaustive
set of considerations to which reference must
6.37 The Committee understands that the factors in both the US fair use and
Australian fair dealing provisions were introduced to
provide guidance to the
courts in their analysis of ‘fairness’. They were not designed to
provide a comprehensive framework
from which the fairness of a dealing could be
mechanically determined. On this point the Franki Committee stated,
... there are so many factors which have to be considered in deciding whether
a particular instance of copying is ‘fair dealing’,
that we think it
is quite impracticable to attempt to remove entirely from the Court the duty of
deciding the question whether or
not a particular instance constitutes
‘fair dealing’.
6.38 The Copyright Law Review Committee agrees with the Franki Committee on
this point. It also agrees with the comments made in the
US White Paper, where
it is stated that when applying fair dealing to the National Information
Infrastructure, the ‘... courts
will have to engage in the same type of
fact-intensive analysis which typifies fair use litigation and frustrates those
“bright lines” clearly separating the lawful from the
6.39 The US White paper also makes another important point:
The inability of our common law system to provide guidance covering every
possible permutation of behaviour is not necessarily a weakness.
By permitting
courts to reach decisions on a case-by-case basis, our system permits both
necessary gap-filling and jurisprudential
evolution without requiring repeated
pleas to Congress for additional
elaboration.
6.40 The Committee is at odds with the statement, made in relation to the
four factors of s. 107 of the US
, that ‘The
demonstrates the almost
infinite elasticity of each of the four factors and their concomitant inability
to resolve difficult
questions.’
6.41 It considers that the statement does not recognise the true nature of
fair use (and fair dealing) in that it is designed to be
flexible and cannot be
rigidly crystallised into a set of common law principles or a statutory
instrument. The Committee’s
understanding reflects the comments of Lord
Denning, when he said, among other things, ‘It is impossible to define
‘fair dealing’. It must be a question of
degree ...’
6.42 The Committee disagrees with the submissions of some broadcasters that
the introduction of such factors would be more likely
to increase the level of
confusion, by disturbing reasonably well established judicial principles. As
stated, the Committee considers
that the general application of the factors to
all fair dealings will provide greater predicability in the determination of
Further, the Committee considers that, rather than disturbing the
judicial principles, the proposed model will provide further statutory
recognition of them.
6.43 CAL submitted that the consideration of whether or not a dealing with a
work is for the purpose of including the work in an electronic
be specifically included as a factor in the determination of the
‘fairness’ of a dealing. Although the
Committee regards this factor
as important, it does not consider it necessary to include it as an additional
factor. To proceed down
this path would return the Act to the particularity for
which it has been justly criticised: the Committee’s recommendations
intended to overcome this deficiency. Rather, the Committee’s view is that
its consideration should fall either within
the currently specified factors,
such as ‘the purpose and character of the dealing’
(s. 40(2)(a)) and ‘the effect
of the dealing on the potential market
for, or value of, the copyright material’ (modified s. 40(2)(d)), or
within the court’s
residual discretion to take into account any other
relevant matter (that discretion arising by virtue of the non-exhaustive nature
of the specified factors).
Recommendation
6.44 The Committee recommends that the non-exclusive set of factors that
are currently considered in relation to ‘a dealing
copying’ with a work for the purpose of research or study (s. 40(2))
be applied generally to all fair dealings.
quantitative test
6.45 The current quantitative test (under s. 40(3) of the Act) applies
to dealings ‘by way of copying’ with published
literary, dramatic
and musical works for the purpose of research or study. It provides valuable
guidance in relation to the copying
of such works in printed form. As noted in
paragraph 4.12, the current test is open-ended: the definition of
‘reasonable portion’
(under s. 10(2)) to which the test refers
is not exhaustive.
6.46 As mentioned in paragraph 6.04, the Government has announced its
intention that the quantitative test be applied to literary,
dramatic and
musical works in digital form where they have been published in hard-copy form.
The Committee understands that a ‘reasonable
portion’ of the work in
digital form is to be determined by reference to the corresponding published
hard copy. Where a hard
copy is not ‘conveniently available’ the
user may copy a ‘reasonable portion’ of the digital work by running
a word count to determine what is 10 per cent of the work.
6.47 The Committee notes the submissions from various copyright users that
the quantitative test should be extended to all copyright
material, including
material in digital form. Particularly strong advocates of such an approach are
libraries and educational institutions,
which recognise the administrative
benefit that would be gained if the test were to be extended. In contrast,
interest groups representing
it currently stands and are therefore opposed to any extension of the test to
forms of copyright material and to formats other than print.
6.48 The Committee is not opposed in principle to extension of the
quantitative test to materials in digital form if this can be achieved
manner consistent with its terms of reference. The Committee gave detailed
consideration to how the present quantitative test
might be extended to the
digital environment. It recognised the value to users of there being an
ascertainable portion of a work
that might be copied without infringing
digital environment
a quantitative test could not work outside the print medium.
The reasons for this are set out in paragraphs 6.53 to 6.66.
6.49 The Government’s proposal appears to reflect the Comittee’s
conclusion. In effect, the proposal is a pragmatic attempt
to extend fair
dealing to works in digital form, in a manner that seeks to avoid the
difficulties inherent in this approach. This
has been done by limiting
permissible fair dealing to only those cases where reference can be had to a
published hard-copy version
of the work. The Committee understands that this
limitation has a further purpose, which is to prevent the quantitative test from
applying to digital databases that have not been published in hard-copy
6.50 Before turning to an analysis of the quantitative test’s
application to the digital environment, it is helpful to look
more closely at
the present form and effect of s. 40(3), which seeks to quantify, in
respect of literary, dramatic and musical works,
dealings that are
to be fair. Significantly, the scope of this provision is quite narrow. It
provides for dealings with works or adaptations, other
than those that comprise
an article in a periodical publication, to be fair only if the copying is of no
more than a ‘reasonable
portion’. A ‘reasonable portion’
is in turn defined in s. 10(2).
definition of ‘reasonable portion’ applies only to published
editions of literary, dramatic and musical works and
refers to various
quantities. The quantities used are ‘an edition of not less than 10
pages’, ‘10% of the pages
in that edition’ and ‘the
whole or part of a single chapter of the work’.
6.51 Unlike the limitations inherent in the quantitative test, fair dealing
permitted under s. 40(2) provides a much more adaptable
Section 40(2)(e) requires a fair dealing to be judged having regard to
‘the amount and substantiality of the part
copied taken in relation to the
whole work or adaptation’. This test is far more flexible in that it
requires a user and, in
the case of a dispute, the court, to weigh up not only
the quantity but also the quality of the material copied in determining whether
the dealing is fair. In contrast with the situation under s. 40(3), both
factors are considered together. The quantity alone of the
used is not determinative; indeed, the quantity cannot be specified if the
factor for consideration under s. 40(2)
is to work and have broad
application.
6.52 It is important to note that, where a dealing by way of copying with a
published literary, dramatic or musical work for the purpose
of research or
study falls outside the scope of the quantitative test, the dealing may still be
regarded as a fair dealing when assessed
against the non-exclusive factors set
out in s. 40(2).
associated with extension of the quantitative test
6.53 The Committee found that the nature of the copyright materials not
currently within the scope of s. 40(3) and the characteristics
current digital environment do not allow for the establishment of an extended
quantitative test that is simple and workable
and provides an appropriate level
of guidance and certainty. The Committee notes that the users of copyright
materials themselves
acknowledge the difficulty of applying the guidelines to
the digital environment.
6.54 The Committee identified the following problems in relation to extension
of the quantitative test:
an absence of discrete units of
measurement with which to apply a quantitative test to material in digital form;
the large numbers of separate
a lack of distinction that often
exists between different copyright works in digital
the difficulty of applying of any
quantitative test to subject matter other than published literary, musical or
dramatic works.
of measurement
6.55 As the test currently applies to published literary, dramatic and
musical works in printed form, a convenient unit of measurement
existed: the printed page. In the electronic environment, however, a similar
unit of measurement is difficult to define.
The traditional ways in which
information has been organised, such as pages, chapters, articles and books, are
in most cases irrelevant.
A new structure has evolved where information is
organised through files, folders, web pages, web sites and databases, and this
does not readily lend itself to a uniform unit of measurement
available to a user.
6.56 The Committee considered a number of different units for the measurement
of a literary, musical or dramatic work in electronic
form. It examined the use
of words, the size (as measured, for example, in bytes) of the file (or files)
used to store the work,
the time to download the file (or files) used to the
store the work, and the number of standard-sized pages of standard-sized text
used to describe the work as displayed by a computer. The Committee found that
reference to these entities could not provide an appropriate
measurement.
6.57 For example, the Committee found the use of words as a unit of
measurement to be problematic. First, musical works are not described
Second, much electronic information, such as low-level programming code and the
data associated with a computer program,
is not expressed in words. Third, there
are technological limitations relating to current software: Internet browsers
such as Internet
Explorer and Netscape, which are commonly used to access
it seem that they are likely to incorporate such a facility. The Committee
acknowledges that it would be possible, in some cases,
for text-based works to
be downloaded by a user and for a word count to be performed using another
application program (such as a
word-processing package). It notes, however, that
to download (reproduce) an entire work for the purpose of conducting a word
may not itself be a fair dealing.
6.58 In relation to the use of the size of a file used to store the work, the
Committee encountered two problems. First, the user
would need to have some
means of knowing the total size of the file that contained the work as well as
the size of the portion intended
for reproduction. Second, the size of the file
may not be directly proportional to the useful information contained within the
especially where the work consists of a number of different data types
(for example, where the work consists of text in which there
are embedded
digital images
6.59 Similar problems were identified when the Committee examined the time to
download a file used to store the work as a unit of
measurement. First, the user
would have to have some means of knowing the total time taken to download the
file that contained the
work and the time to download the material they wished
to reproduce. Second, the time to download a file is generally related to
size of the file; thus, the time to download the file may not be directly
proportional to the useful information contained within
the file, for the
reasons just explained. Further, the speed of data transfer across the period
taken to download a file could fluctuate
dramatically according to network
traffic. Finally, the time to download a file may not be a relevant
consideration as technology
improves and the downloading of information becomes
virtually instantaneous.
6.60 The Committee also encountered problems when considering a unit of
measurement based on the number of standard-sized pages of
standard-sized text
used to describe the work as displayed by a computer. The main problem was that
the test involved an estimation
of the total number of pages in the work and the
total number of pages in the portion intended for reproduction. The Committee
that this may not be a problem in relation to some software (used to
display, for example, literary works) such as word-processing
applications,
which provide the user with information on the number of pages in a digital
work. The Committee understands, however,
that different software applications
do paginate identical materials differently. The Committee also notes that the
most common language
currently used to display information on the Internet
(HTML, or hypertext mark-up language) does not divide the information displayed
into standard-sized pages but divides it into ‘web pages’ of
variable sizes.
6.61 Another problem that arises when applying the quantitative test to
large electronic
quantities (millions) of separate copyright
materials in digital form. Such
they possess a sufficient
degree of originality, in addition to satisfying the
other requirements of the Act.
6.62 Given therefore that an electronic database may contain millions of
separate items of copyright material and that a whole database
can itself be
classified as a literary work, a problem exists if the quantitative test is
applied to a database in the same way as
it would be applied to any other
literary work. Taken in the extreme, the application of the quantitative test to
a database would
include hundreds of thousands of separate copyright items
(10 per cent of a large database) incorporated in the database. The
notes that reproduction of such items would be possible only if the
test inoperative.
of distinction between works
6.63 A further problem concerns the lack of distinction that often exists
between separate works in digital form. For example, a single
web page may
contain a number of embedded works created by separate authors. The problem that
therefore arises is identifying what
should be regarded as the
‘work’ for the purpose of applying the quantitative test beyond its
current field of operation
under s. 40(3).
Application
to subject matter other than published literary, musical or dramatic
6.64 Finally, a problem exists in the general application of the quantitative
test to artistic works, sound recordings, cinematograph
films and broadcasts.
For example, application of a meaningful test to a non-linear cinematograph film
such as a computer game
, a multimedia
work, an artistic work, or a continuous broadcast would be very difficult. The
Committee considers that the level of
guidance and certainty found under the
current quantitative test could never be achieved in relation to such subject
6.65 The Committee notes the Government’s decision that the
quantitative test should be applied to literary, dramatic and musical
digital form where they have been published in hard-copy form (see paragraph
6.46). The Committee acknowledges the desirability
and clear public benefit of
an extension of the quantitative test to literary, dramatic and musical works in
digital format. On the
basis of the evidence, submissions and information
available to it, however, the Committee is unable to conclude that it is
practicable
to apply the test to works in digital form.
Recommendation
6.66 The Committee recommends that its analysis be taken into account in
the next stage of the Government’s consideration of
the Digital
test—‘prescribed portion’
6.67 As noted in paragraph 6.50, the current application of the quantitative
test is that of a simple deeming provision, providing
that a dealing that falls
within the parameters of s. 40(3) of the Act shall be taken to be a
‘fair dealing’. In the
Committee’s view it is important to
recognise that s. 40(3) and the definition in s. 10(2) operate
independently of the criteria
of fairness in s. 40(2). Section 40(3)
represents a pragmatic approach to dealing with the copying of a limited class
of materials
in limited circumstances. The provision is a legislative compromise
between the convenience of simple certainty and the need to avoid
prejudice to the legitimate interests of copyright owners in the limited
circumstances of the provision’s operation.
Indeed, it is the limited
circumstances of its operation—namely, research and study in combination
with a specified quantity—that,
in the Committee’s view, provide the
necessary elements for the provision’s operation to be considered fair.
Nonetheless,
the Committee strongly believes that it would be incorrect to
maintain the specific compromise effected by s. 40(3) within the proposed
open-ended fair dealing model because, as mentioned, s. 40(3) does not
require consideration of the fairness criteria in s. 40(2).
Recommendations
6.68 The Committee recommends that the quantitative test, and the concept
of a ‘reasonable portion’, as it is currently
called, be treated
separately from the concept of a ‘fair dealing’ since there is no
requirement for recourse to the
criteria of ‘fairness’. The
Committee therefore
the structure of the new fair
dealing provision reflect this distinction and that the quantitative test be
removed from ‘fair
dealing’ and drafted as a separate stand-alone
provision on permitted free copying;
‘reasonable portion’ be renamed a ‘prescribed portion’,
to distinguish it more clearly from the general
principle of fair
6.69 Additionally, the Committee recommends that the
provision just described expressly prohibit the serial copying of
10 per cent
6.70 As noted in paragraph 6.48, the Committee considered how the existing
concept of a ‘reasonable portion’ might be
extended to the digital
environment. It found that it was not possible to develop a test of broad
application in a manner consistent
with its terms of reference to advise on the
simplification of the Act. It is clear, however, that in the limited area where
present definition of a ‘reasonable portion’ operates it serves
a useful purpose.
Recommendations
6.71 In the light of the inherent difficulties that exist in the extension
of the quantitative test to all subject matter, including
material in the
digital environment, the Committee recommends that the operation of the
quantitative test be defined exhaustively
and be specifically limited to
published literary, dramatic and musical works in printed form.
6.72 The Committee therefore recommends the insertion of the word
‘printed’ before the terms ‘periodical publication’
‘published edition’ (see the discussion of the definition of
‘prescribed portion’ in paragraph 6.145).
6.73 The Committee recognises that the current quantitative test provides
valuable assistance to, and is regularly relied on by, copyright
Appendix D, paragraph 13). It notes that the recommended changes are not
designed in any way to limit the operation of
the current test as it applies to
published literary, dramatic and musical works in printed form.
6.74 The Committee considers that insertion of the word
‘printed’, as described in paragraph 6.72, would remove the
possibility
of an argument being raised that the term ‘periodical
publication’ or the term ‘published edition’, might
to include some literary, dramatic and musical works in digital form. Despite
the terms of s. 88, dealing with the nature
of copyright in published
editions of works, such an argument appears to be possible given the broad
wording of s. 92, dealing with
published editions of works in which
of a literary, dramatic
or musical work.
6.75 The Committee is of the view that the scope of application of the
quantitative test should be defined exhaustively so as to avoid
the possibility
of judicial interpretation extending the test beyond published literary,
dramatic and musical works in printed form.
If that were done, reference to a
‘reasonable portion’ would no longer be appropriate since under the
proposed test a
court should not have the discretion to determine what was
‘reasonable’. Any copying or other use that fell outside the
and that is argued to be a fair dealing would be assessed by reference to the
factors (modified s. 40(2)) used in the determination
‘fairness’.
6.76 The Committee notes that the term ‘reasonable portion’, as
defined in s. 10(2), is also referred to in other provisions,
copying by libraries and archives for users (s. 49(5)(b)), copying by
libraries and archives for other libraries or archives
(s. 50(7A)(b)(ii)),
and multiple copying of works by educational institutions (s. 135ZL(2)).
Paragraphs 7.73 to 7.82 and 7.96 to
7.99 provide further discussion of the
term ‘reasonable portion’ as it applies to copying by libraries and
Application
of the modified quantitative test to all dealings
Recommendations
6.77 With the exception of two members, the Committee recommends that the
current limitation on the application of the quantitative
test to a dealing
‘by way of copying’ be removed, thereby allowing the quantitative
test to apply to
all dealings
with printed published literary, dramatic
or musical works, or adaptations of such works, for the purpose of research or
6.78 In dissenting from the foregoing recommendation one Committee member
recommends the extension of the quantitative test to all
dealings except those
that involve the reproduction of copyright material from hard-copy form to
digital form. The other dissenting
Committee member recommends that the
quantitative test remain limited to dealings ‘by way of
6.79 In making the recommendation in paragraph 6.77, the Committee (with the
exception of one member) acknowledges that the proposed
test would have only
limited application to some of the exclusive rights, such as publication and
public performance, because of
the requirement that the dealing be for
‘research or study’.
6.80 The Committee notes the Government’s recent decision to implement
a broadly based, technology-neutral right of communication
to the public as a
result of its consideration of the proposals contained in the WCT and the WPPT.
With the exception of one member,
the Committee considers that extension of the
quantitative test to this new right would not unreasonably prejudice the
interests of copyright owners. It also considers that such an
approach is necessary to ensure effective use of new technology, including
sending of material over electronic communication networks such as the Internet.
6.81 With the exception of two members, the Committee considers that the
modified quantitative test should permit the scanning of
an article in a printed
periodical publication, or a ‘prescribed portion’, into digital form
and its subsequent transmission
to another person in the course of research or
study. To come within the exception, the transmission must be for the purpose of
or study by the person making the transmission. For instance, such a
dealing may arise when a researcher, for their own purpose of
research or study,
scans a printed chapter from a published book (being a ‘prescribed
portion’) and transmits it to colleagues
to discuss a theory expressed in
the chapter. Such a dealing would otherwise be an infringement of the copyright
owner’s right
of reproduction in the literary work, in addition to an
infringement of the proposed right of communication to the public (once such
right is introduced), unless it constituted a ‘fair dealing’ under
the proposed fair dealing model (see paragraph 6.143).
Unlike the new
quantitative test, the test currently in s. 40(3) is limited to instances
where a printed work is being ‘copied’.
For this reason it does not
apply in relation to other dealings.
6.82 Further, the majority of the Committee notes that a consequence of the
recommendation in paragraph 6.77 (that the quantitative
test apply to all
dealings) is that the proposed test would allow for the scanning of an article
in a printed periodical publication,
or a ‘prescribed portion’, into
digital form and the subsequent placing of that material in a database to be
made available
to specific users. To come within this exception, such a dealing
with the copyright material must be for the research or study purpose
person making the material available
For instance, such a dealing may
arise if a student, for the purpose of their own research or study, scans an
article in a printed
periodical publication and places it on a web site in
order to conduct a survey on a particular matter expressed in the article.
Again, such an activity would otherwise be an infringement of the copyright
owner’s right of reproduction and the Government’s
proposed right of
communication to the public, unless it constituted a ‘fair dealing’
under the proposed fair dealing
model (see paragraph 6.143).
6.83 The Committee notes that the majority recommendation is at odds with the
arguments raised by organisations representing copyright
owners. The Australian
non-digitised material must be
done with the permission of the copyright owner.
It argued that the creation of electronic databases without permission or
or both, was likely to conflict with the normal exploitation of the
work or at least prejudice the legitimate interests of the copyright
The ACC argued further for the
confinement of the quantitative test to dealings by way of copying when it
submitted that the term
‘copy’ should be interpreted as meaning a
limited subset of acts involving reproduction. It proposed that the term
did not include the creation, duplication or printing of
materials in digital form.
6.84 The exact distinction between the terms ‘copying’ and
‘reproduction’, as used in the Act, is unclear.
The Act does not
provide any real assistance other than suggesting under s. 10(1) that an
‘infringing copy’ of a work
means a reproduction of the
Professor Ricketson states that,
whilst it is clear that ‘reproducing’ a work means
‘copying’ it, the former
term is more neutral and potentially
In the case of
(Football) Ltd v William Hill (Football) Ltd,
Lord Reid (whilst analysing
the terms from a slightly different perspective) held that reproduction meant
More recently, in the case of
Skybase Nominees Pty Ltd and Another v Fortuity Pty Ltd,
Justice Hill
appeared to use the term ‘copied’ as a synonym for
‘reproduced’.
6.85 The precise scope of the terms ‘reproduction’ and
‘copying’ would, in relation to the application of
a quantitative
test, become an academic question if the test were to be expanded to include all
dealings. The Committee notes that
the exercise of the right of reproduction
under the new quantitative test should also include reproductions as specified
21 of the Act.
Recommendation
6.86 If the recommendation to apply the quantitative test to all dealings
is not adopted, the Committee (with the exception of the
two dissenting members)
recommends that the term ‘reproduction’ be used instead of the term
‘copying’, so
as to ensure application of the test to the widest
possible range of reproductions.

to the proposed rights is necessary if fair dealing

digital environment. For example, a fair dealing for the purpose of reporting
the news should be capable

the proposed copyright owner’s right of communication
to the public, which
will apply to works made available on the Internet and other online services. A
fair dealing for the purpose
of criticism or review should apply to extracts of
a recently published work that has been incorporated in an electronic article
and transmitted to the public via an electronic network. Such a dealing would
again otherwise infringe the copyright owner’s
proposed right of
communication to the public, which includes the transmission of works to the
6.91 The Committee majority therefore rejects CAL’s submission that

any proposed right of
communication to the public or to the proposed transmission
Recommendation

Computer Software Protection
report that

research or study.
6.93 The Committee notes that the fact that a dealing results in the use of a
work in digital form could be raised as a consideration
weighing against
regarding the dealing as fair. It recognises the greater potential for access
to, and therefore the value of, copyright
material held in digital form,
especially if such material is accessible via an electronic network. The fact
that the copyright material
has been transmitted to the public or made digitally
available would be specifically relevant to considerations such as the purpose
and character of the dealing and the effect of the dealing on the potential
market for the copyright material. The majority of the
acknowledges, however, that such dealing is capable of being considered fair, as
in the example provided in paragraph 6.90.
conducting dealings on behalf of others
6.94 The ability of a person to conduct a fair dealing on behalf of another
is not specifically dealt with in the Act. The closest
the Act comes to dealing
with this issue is in the provision of the exceptions relating to the copying of
of a library on behalf of users who require the
copy for the purpose of research or study (see paragraph 7.52). It must be noted
that such dealings fall only within the specific exceptions for libraries and
are not generally regarded as fair dealings. The Act
does not, however,
expressly limit librarians to copying under these library-specific exceptions.
The Committee notes the
case, which held that the relevant
purpose when determining whether a dealing is fair is that of the person
actually undertaking the
light of this decision the Committee considers that under the current fair
dealing provisions a librarian could copy on behalf
of a user only if the
librarian’s actual act of copying was regarded as being for one of the
specified purposes, as well as
fair. This is discussed further in paragraphs
7.61 to 7.72, and a recommendation to change this situation is set out at
6.95 The situation can be compared with that in the UK, where s. 29(3)
of the UK Act provides (in relation to fair dealings for the
purpose of research
and private study) that copying by a person on behalf of another is allowed
under certain conditions. The section
states that copying by a person other than
a researcher or student is not a fair dealing if,
in the case of a librarian, he or
she exceeds the copying limitations that apply to librarians (as set out in
ss. 38, 39 and 40 of
in any other case, the person
doing the copying knows or has reason to believe that such copying will result
in copies of substantially
the same material being provided to more than one
person at substantially the same time for substantially the same purpose.
6.96 The Australian case law also does not provide any clear
guidance as to the extent to which a person can act on behalf of another
reliance upon the fair dealing exception. As noted by the
, in the case of
Ltd v Haines
McLelland J
considered a hypothetical example of a teacher who appointed himself agent of
every member of the class and made multiple
copies for the purpose of classroom
study of more than a reasonable portion of the work. The judge took the view
that such a dealing
would be unlikely to be a fair dealing. This conclusion,
however, as noted by the ACC
primarily on the assessment of fairness having regard to the operation of a
statutory licence rather than any general rule
that would preclude a person
conducting a fair dealing on behalf of another.
6.97 In the New Zealand case of
Longman Group Ltd v Carrington Technical
Institute Board of Governors & Anor
it was held that the language of the
exception that authorised the reproduction of works in the course of educational
instruction
of the New Zealand
did not preclude the use of the exception by a person on behalf of
It must be noted, however, that
the exception was not a fair dealing.
6.98 The proposed fair dealing model is not limited to an exclusive set of
purposes and is therefore sufficiently flexible to allow
for the inclusion of
dealings for new purposes (see paragraph 6.33). The Committee considers that the
proposed open-ended model should
not exclude dealings whereby a person acts on
behalf of another, provided that such dealings are regarded as being fair when
against the non-exclusive set of factors described in
section (2) of the proposed fair dealing provision (see paragraph 6.143).
the Committee (with the exception of one member) is of the opinion that
such dealings should not exclude activities where a person
conducts a dealing on
behalf of another on a for-profit basis. The commercial nature of the dealing
would be examined by a court
in its consideration of the factor ‘the
purpose and character of the dealing’ (see section (2)(a) of the
proposed provision
in paragraph 6.143). The Committee notes that, in applying
the general fair dealing principles, the fact that the purpose of the
commercial would weigh against the finding of a fair dealing (see paragraphs
5.14 to 5.16). The dissenting member of the
Committee is of the view that a
person should be able to conduct a fair dealing on behalf of another only on a
cost-recovery basis.
6.99 The Committee majority considers that under the new open-ended fair
dealing model it would be open to a court, in assessing the
fairness of a
dealing where a person acted on behalf of another, to consider whether the
purpose of the person for whom the dealing
was conducted was recognised as being
fair, such as for the purpose of research or study. For example, a court could
take into consideration
the fact that a person who provided a commercial copying
service to students provided such a service on the understanding that the
material copied was for the purpose of each student’s own research or
study. The Committee majority notes that this approach
is consistent with the
principle stated in the
case (see paragraph 6.94) since that
decision was based on the current fair dealing model, which is limited to an
exclusive set of
purposes, whereas the suggested approach is based on an
open-ended model.
6.100 The Committee is of the view that a special exception should be made to
the principle stated in the
case in relation to copying by a
library or an archives on behalf of a user under the proposed fair dealing
provision (see paragraphs
7.61 to 7.65).
6.101 The Committee majority is of the view that extra conditions do not need
to be specified in relation to the conduct of a fair
dealing by a person on
behalf of another, as has been provided under s. 29(3) of the UK Act. The
Committee majority considers that
the non-exclusive set of factors set out in
section (2) of the proposed fair dealing provision (see paragraph 6.143) is
sufficiently
flexible to determine the fairness of the dealing.
6.102 Given that the conduct of a dealing on behalf of another must be
regarded as being fair before the fair dealing exception applies,
the Committee
considers that allowing such dealings will not unreasonably prejudice the
legitimate interests of copyright owners
or disturb the balance struck between
users and owners of copyright material.
Recommendation
6.103 The Committee recommends that, in principle, the dealing with
be excluded from
being a fair dealing if it can be found to be ‘fair’ having regard
to the relevant criteria.
Application of the
quantitative test
6.104 The Committee is of the view that, subject to the exception described
in paragraph 6.105, a person should not be able to conduct
a dealing under
the proposed quantitative test on behalf of another. The Committee reached this
conclusion because the proposed quantitative
test operates separately from the
proposed open-ended fair dealing model. Thus, applying the principle stated in
case (see paragraph 6.94), the purpose of the person
actually conducting the dealing on behalf of another would have to be that
in the test (that is, research or study). It is difficult to envisage
circumstances where a person, for their own purpose of research
or study, would
conduct a dealing under the quantitative test on behalf of another. The
Committee therefore concludes that where
a person conducts a dealing on behalf
of another with a literary, dramatic or musical work, and the amount dealt with
falls within
the scope of the quantitative test, the dealer would have to rely
on the general fair dealing provision.
6.105 The Committee considers that an exception to the foregoing should apply
in relation to a dealing by way of copying by a library
or an archives under the
proposed quantitative test (see paragraphs 7.67 to 7.72).

Restoration
of the word ‘private’ before ‘study or research’
6.112 The Committee notes that the
deleted the word ‘private’ from s. 40, in accordance with
the recommendation made by the Franki Committee. The Franki
Committee considered
that the limitation was intended to distinguish use of material for private
study from use for classroom instruction,
but it regarded the distinction as
artificial and difficult to maintain. It said,
It is clear that the photocopying of material is of considerable assistance
in enabling teachers and students to prepare material
for classroom use, and
that it is difficult to maintain a distinction between private study and other
educational purposes. So long
as the photocopying for educational use is
qualified, for the purposes of s. 40, by the requirement of fair dealing,
we think that
the removal of the limitation to private study will not prejudice
owners of copyright.
6.113 The Committee received a number of submissions that called for the
reinsertion of the word ‘private’ before study
because it was felt
that the operation of s. 40 is too broad (see Appendix D, paragraphs 29 to
36). CAL argued that it frequently
encounters corporations, institutions,
commercial organisations and statutory bodies claiming that they may rely on the
to copy material as part of their work activities. In CAL’s view
this is a use of the exception that was not contemplated when
the existing Act
was drafted or when it was amended in 1980.
6.114 The counter-argument was put forward by organisations representing
users of copyright material. The Council of Australian University
argued that limiting fair dealing in this manner would affect universities
engaged in joint-venture research, leading to
commercial exploitation of their
discoveries. Similarly, the AVCC stated that to attempt to qualify research as
in some other way introduces unnecessary
uncertainty and places an unworkable restriction on an activity that is
fundamental to universities:
researchers must have the flexibility to draw on
the information base and resources that underpin their research, regardless of
sources or ultimate use.
6.115 The Committee notes that the Australian courts are yet to decide a case
that determines whether research activities with a commercial
end in view are
included in fair dealing for the purpose of research or study. The Committee
understands that it is possible for
such activities to fall within the
equivalent fair dealing provisions of New Zealand and the United Kingdom (see
paragraph 4.18).
The Committee also notes, however, the advice by Dennis Rose
QC, as Chief General Counsel to the Attorney-General’s Department,
referred to in
Computer Software
In relation to the
expression ‘research and study’ used in s. 40, Mr Rose
[It is] likely that the courts would hold that ‘study’ is
confined to study by individuals for their own purposes, whether
in private or
in some institutional course or otherwise. Moreover, the courts could well
confine ‘research’ to research
activities such as those in
universities and the CSIRO, for the purpose of increasing knowledge in the
community as a whole—by
contrast, for instance, with research in a
Government Department for the purpose of advising Ministers on proposed
legislation, or
research by a manufacturing company for the purpose of improving
its products.
6.116 In the Committee’s view it is undesirable to reintroduce the word
‘private’ before ‘research or study’.
It notes that the
distinction between private and commercial activities undertaken for research or
study is often unclear, and it
considers that the public interest would be
maximised if fair dealing for the purpose of research or study did not
necessarily exclude
some commercial activities. The Committee notes, however,
that if the activity in question is for a commercial purpose and results
economic detriment to the copyright owner it should be raised as a factor
weighing against the finding of fair dealing. Consideration
of such a factor is
specifically required under the purpose and character of the dealing
(s. 40(2)(a)) and the effect of the dealing
on the potential market for, or
value of, the copyright material (modified s. 40(2)(d)) and would continue
to be required under the
proposed amendments.

6.117 The Committee recommends
that the word ‘private’
not be re-inserted before the phrase ‘study or research’ in relation
to fair dealing
for the purpose of ‘research or study’ (ss. 40
Limitations
on fair dealings
6.118 Under the current fair dealing provisions the following requirements
exist in relation to the operation of fair dealing:
(a) where the dealing is for the purpose of criticism or review, a sufficient
acknowledgment of the copyright material must be made
(ss. 41 and
(b) where the dealing is for the purpose of reporting news in a text form (a
newspaper, magazine or similar periodical), a sufficient
acknowledgment of the
(c) where the dealing is for the purpose of reporting news by means of
broadcasting or in a cinematograph film, a musical work played
in the course of
reporting the news must form part of the news being reported
(s. 42(2)).
6.119 The Committee considers that requirement (a) reflects Australia’s
international obligations under art. 10(3) of the Berne
Convention, which
requires that an indication of the source and author shall be provided when
quotations are used from works and
cinematograph
The Copyright Act complies with
this obligation and provides for ‘sufficient acknowledgment’ to be
made in relation to
the criticism or review of works (s. 41) and
‘audio-visual items’, which includes cinematograph films
(s. 103A). The
Committee notes, however, that the definition of
‘sufficient acknowledgment’ under s. 10(1) of the Act is
provided only
in relation to works and does not include cinematograph films.
This appears to be inconsistent with art. 14
(1) of the Berne
Convention which states, ‘The owner of copyright in a cinematographic work
shall enjoy the same rights as the
author of an original work.’
Recommendation
6.120 The Committee recommends
that the Act be amended so as to
include reference to cinematograph films in the definition of ‘sufficient
acknowledgment’
to achieve compliance with Australia’s international
obligations under arts. 10(3) and 14
(1) of the Berne
Convention.
6.121 The Committee considers it should anticipate that, at the time the
Government considers this report, the moral rights legislation
contained in the
will have come into operation. The new
be inserted by the Bill
provides for statutory recognition of
the moral rights of authors of works and directors and producers of films. The
Committee notes
that the new moral rights provisions will be insufficient to
effect compliance with art. 10(3) of the Berne Convention. As discussed
further in paragraph 6.125, the moral rights provisions require only
identification of the author (s. 193 of the new Part IX) and
do not require
that an indication of the source be given. As stated in paragraph 6.119,
art. 10(3) of the Berne Convention requires
an indication of both the
source and the author when quotations are used from works and cinematograph
Recommendation
6.122 The Committee recommends that a specific provision be maintained in
the Act (similar to the current ss. 41 and 103A) to comply
art. 10(3) of the Berne Convention or, alternatively, that the moral rights
provisions be appropriately amended in relation
to dealings conducted for the
purpose of criticism or review.
6.123 Unlike sufficient acknowledgment in relation to dealings for the
purpose of criticism or review, the introduction of the new
moral rights
legislation would remove the need for specific reference to the requirement for
sufficient acknowledgment in relation
to dealings for the purpose of reporting
the news in a text form (ss. 42(1)(a) and 103B(1)(a) of the Act—see
requirement (b)
in paragraph 6.118). The Committee notes that as requirement (b)
currently operates it does not satisfactorily implement Australia’s
international obligations under the Berne Convention. A fair dealing with a work
or cinematograph film for the purpose of reporting
the news is authorised under
the following articles that provide for the making of exceptions:
the general exception of art.
the specific exception of art.
(2), which applies in relation to the reporting of current events by
means of photography, cinematograph films, broadcasts and
transmissions;
the specific exception
(2), which applies in relation to the reporting of lectures,
addresses and other works of the same nature in the press.
6.124 Unlike art. 10(3), the articles just mentioned do not
specifically require the identification of the author. The above exceptions
operate subject to art. 6
, however, which applies in relation to
exceptions authorised by the Convention.
(1) therefore applies to all dealings for the purpose of
reporting the news and is not limited in any way to the reporting of news
text form. The article provides that an author has the right to have the
authorship of their works recognised in clear and unambiguous
The fact therefore that
requirement (b) specifies only sufficient acknowledgment when reporting the news
in a text form would appear
to fall short of the requirements of
6.125 The Committee notes that a fair dealing with a work or cinematograph
film for the purpose of reporting the news would be classified
‘attributable act’ under the new moral rights provisions and would
require the identification of the author (s.
193 of the new Part IX). The
Committee also notes that the current requirement for the acknowledgment of both
the source and the
author under ss. 42(1)(a) and 103B(1)(a) appears to be
unnecessary, given that art. 6
(1) provides only
with the right of attribution of authorship.
Recommendation
6.126 The Committee recommends that ss. 42(1)(a) and 103B(1)(a) be
removed from the Act and that reliance be placed on the new moral
provisions to effect compliance with art. 6
(1) of the Berne
Convention.
6.127 Given that art. 9(2) of the Berne Convention operates subject to
the requirements of art. 6
(1), it would appear that the author,
and the owner of copyright in a cinematograph film, have the right of
attribution of authorship
in relation to all fair dealings with works or
cinematograph films (art. 14
(1)). The Committee also notes that
the proposed s. 193 appears to attach moral rights to attributable acts,
even though they might
be a fair dealing. The use of works and films for
reporting the news would thus attract the operation of the proposed moral rights
provisions.
6.128 Section 42(2) of the Act provides that the playing of a musical work in
the course of reporting the news by means of broadcasting
or in a cinematograph
film is not a fair dealing with the work if the playing of the work does not
form part of the news being reported
(requirement (c), paragraph 6.118).
6.129 The Committee notes that s. 42(2) was introduced as a result of a
recommendation made by the Spicer Committee on the basis of
submissions received

Those organisations argued that

It shall also be a matter for legislation in the countries of the Union to

and cinematograph films. The Committee also understands that

permissible after the report has been made to embellish

Recommendation

dealing for the purpose of professional legal advice
6.135 Some organisations representing copyright owners have specifically
argued against the operation of the fair dealing exception
for the purpose of
professional advice by a legal practitioner or patent attorney. For example, CAL
submitted that the provisions
have no place in a modern copyright regime and
that it is not in the public interest that certain professional groups enjoy the
of making copies of copyright material without the need to pay (see
Appendix D, paragraphs 43 to 46).
6.136 With the exception of one member, the Committee notes that the purpose
of the fair dealing provisions relating to professional
advice is not to provide
legal practitioners or patent attorneys with benefits not enjoyed by other
professional groups or members
of society. The purpose of the provisions is to
facilitate access to the legal system and, indirectly, to lower legal costs. The
Committee majority therefore considers that the fair dealing provisions for the
purpose of professional advice are clearly justified
on the basis of the public
interest. The majority also notes that the free use of copyright materials under
these provisions is most
likely to be limited and that dealings for this purpose
are unlikely to have a major impact on the potential market for, or value
the copyright material being used. Although the dissenting Committee member
agrees in principle with the policy that access to
the legal system is in the
public interest, he does not consider that copyright owners, in particular,
should subsidise the access.
The dissenting member is opposed to allowing the
ability to electronically reproduce or transmit, or both, copyright material for
the purpose of professional legal advice without the permission of the copyright
6.137 Paragraphs 4.28 and 4.29 provide a general discussion of the operation
of ss. 43(2), 104(b) and 104(c). It should be noted that
at present a
number of inconsistencies exist in relation to the use of copyright materials
for the purpose of professional advice.
Section 43(2), which applies to works,
is in fact narrower in operation that ss. 104(b) and 104(c), which apply to
subject matter
other than works. Section 43(2) allows for the use of copyright
material for the purpose of the
of professional advice, whereas
ss. 104(b) and 104(c) allow for the use of copyright material for the
professional advice (s. 104(b)) and for the
purpose of,
or in the course of, the giving
of professional advice
(s. 104(c)). Another important difference is that s. 43(2) requires
the use to be a fair dealing, whereas
ss. 104(b) and 104(c) operate as
straight exceptions to copyright and are therefore not subject to any
‘fairness’ requirements.
In the Committee’s opinion there is
no basis for the distinctions that currently exist between works and subject
matter other
than works in relation to the operation of these exceptions. These
distinctions should be removed from the Act.

uses of copyright material for the purpose of professional
legal advice fall
within fair dealing and therefore be subject to the fairness requirements if
they are not to be infringements.
The dissenting Committee member does not agree
that ‘all uses’ should include the reproduction or supply of
electronically.

(2) In determining whether in any particular case a dealing is a fair
dealing, regard shall be had to the following:
(a) the purpose and character of the dealing;
(b) the nature of the copyright material;
the possibility of obtaining the copyright material within a
reasonable time at an ordinary commercial price;
(d) the effect of the dealing upon the potential market for, or value of, the
(e) in a case where part only of the copyright material is dealt

cinematograph film, in the course of reporting the news by means
of photography,
communication to the public
cinematograph film, shall be a fair dealing only if that material forms part of
the news being reported.

7.02 The Committee explored these issues with
interest groups at the public forum. The issues paper invited written
submissions on
the matters discussed. The Committee also made inquiries about
the practical application of these provisions and held with interest

on the Committee’s

to the issues paper, previous

during its consultations with interest groups.
7.03 Among other things, the issues paper prompted responses on temporary or
incidental copies created as a by-product of digital

publication of anonymous and pseudonymous works. The Committee considers that it
would be more appropriate to

Highways to Change
libraries and archives provisions needed at all?
7.04 The issues paper sought comment on whether separate provisions for
royalty-free copying by libraries and archives are needed
in the Act and, if so,
how these provisions could best be simplified.
7.05 In recommending changes to the existing library and archives provisions,
the Committee was mindful of the differing functions
of publishers and
libraries. Its recommendations are not intended to place libraries in direct
competition with publishers. Access
to copyright material provided by libraries
and archives is to remain limited so as to not interfere with the legitimate
of copyright owners.
7.06 Copyright owners have proposed the adoption of voluntary or statutory
licensing schemes in place of the present royalty-free
copying by libraries and
archives. The replacement of the existing libraries and archives provisions with
licensing schemes was opposed
by educational institutions, libraries, and the
Law Council of Australia (LCA) on the basis that it would result in restriction
access to information.
7.07 An alternative supported by copyright owners is to permit libraries and
archives to make copies only in instances where the copyright
material is not
available within a reasonable time at an ordinary commercial price. This
approach was strongly opposed by libraries
and educational institutions. Their
main objection was that to restrict all forms of copying by libraries and
archives to instances
where the copyright material is not available within a
reasonable time at an ordinary commercial price would unreasonably encroach
the ‘right to read’.
7.08 The Committee notes that this restriction is already a mandatory
requirement in relation to the following:
the copying of the whole of a work
(other than an article contained in a periodical publication) or parts greater
than a reasonable
portion of works for users
(s. 49(5));
the copying of the
whole of a work (other than an article contained in a periodical publication) or
parts greater than a reasonable
portion of works for other libraries and
archives (s. 50(7A));
(s. 51A(4)).
Applying this as a mandatory restriction to all
copying done by libraries and archives would extend its application to the
of articles contained in periodical publications, reasonable portions of
works, copying done by parliamentary libraries, and copying
by the Australian
Archives under s. 51AA. This would constitute a significant shift in the
existing balance between the interests
of copyright owners and the public
interest in access to information.
7.09 The Committee is of the view that, in the public interest, limited
royalty-free copying of copyright materials by libraries and
archives is an
important exception to the exclusive rights of copyright owners that should be
maintained. Provisions regulating royalty-free
copying of copyright materials by
libraries and archives should not be replaced by voluntary or statutory
licensing schemes or amended
in such a way as to constitute a substantial
reduction in the type of copying currently permitted in the Act.
7.10 The issues paper invited comment on whether the library and archives
provisions could be regarded as falling within a general
fair use or
‘dealing’ exception. Submissions from educational institutions,
libraries and archives gave support for consolidating
the library and archives
provisions with the fair dealing provisions in the Act. This amalgamation was
supported mainly because it
would allow libraries and archives to make copies of
7.11 The Committee notes, and discusses in full in paragraphs 7.52 to
7.58, the fact that some of the current provisions for royalty-free
libraries and archives regulate activities where the proposed fair dealing
provision, discussed in Chapter 6, has the
capacity to apply.
Recommendation
7.12 The Committee recommends that provisions regulating the making of
royalty-free copies of copyright material by libraries and
archives remain in
the Act, but in a simplified form.
division of library and archives provisions between Parts III and IV of the
7.13 Submissions received by the Committee suggested that one way to simplify
the Act would be to treat works and subject matter other
than works consistently
throughout the Act. The issues paper noted that, whilst libraries and archives
may make copies of published
works for users or other libraries and archives
under ss. 49 and 50 of the Act respectively, there are no corresponding
permitting the copying of published subject matter other than works.
‘Subject matter other than works’ comprises sound
recordings,
cinematograph films, television and sound broadcasts, and published editions of
works. That these copyright materials
cannot be copied by libraries and archives
for users or other libraries and archives appears to be historical rather than a
policy choice. Comments were sought as to whether the Act should be
amended so that it treats works and subject matter other than
works consistently
with respect to copying by libraries and archives.
7.14 In this context the Committee notes that, in connection with the copying
of unpublished copyright material and the copying of
purposes of preservation by libraries and archives, as well as all copying done
by parliamentary libraries,
works and subject matter other than works are
already treated consistently within the Act.

and archives of
the whole or parts of works and the whole or parts of subject matter other than
works were supported in the submissions
from libraries and the LCA.
7.16 The ACC opposed royalty-free copying of audiovisual items by libraries
and archives, at least with respect to copying for educational
institutions and

Act. These provisions apply only to educational institutions’ copying of

The ACC considers that the policy behind the
provisions in Part VA would be undermined if the library and archives provisions
the copying of non-broadcast material and the copying of broadcast

persuaded by this argument. Licensing agreements similar to those provided in
Part VA with respect to broadcasts are also
provided in Part VB of the Act with
respect to works. It is also noted that where a licensing agreement is in
operation the principle
enunciated in
would apply; that is, an existing licensing agreement would need to be taken
into account before any royalty-free copying under the

could be justified.
7.17 The issues paper noted that ss. 49 and 50 of the Act permit copying
of the whole, parts or reasonable portions of works and that
‘reasonable
portion’ is defined only with respect to literary, dramatic or musical
works. Comment was invited on whether
the term ‘reasonable portion’
should be extended to sound recordings, cinematograph films, sound broadcasts
and television
broadcasts, which are collectively defined as audiovisual items
under s. 100A of the Act.
7.18 Extending the definition of ‘reasonable portion’ to
audiovisual items for the purposes of the library and archives
provisions was
supported by educational institutions, libraries, archives and the ACC. The
Committee is not opposed in principle
to extending the definition of reasonable
portion in the Act, but it reached the conclusion that any approach that
attempts to define
a reasonable portion of audiovisual items is unworkable. The
Committee refers readers to its general discussion on reasonable portion
‘prescribed portion’, as proposed by the Committee) and the
quantitative test in Chapter 6.

s. 103C may make copies of audiovisual items but libraries and
acting at the request of their users cannot. This is especially so given that
libraries and archives may make copies for
users at their request
the copyright material requested to be copied is a work.
7.20 It is the view of the Committee that treating works and subject matter
other than works consistently will not only remove any
possible confusion to
users of the Act caused by different standards set for different types of
the structure of the Act and
unnecessary duplication. No matter what their views
on the extension of copying by libraries and archives to subject matter other
than works, submitters supported the amalgamation of the library and archives
provisions in Parts III and IV of the Act. The Committee
agrees that this would
significantly to simplifying the Act.
Recommendation
7.21 The Committee recommends that the library and archives provisions be
amended so that the copying of whole works or parts of works
and subject matter
other than works for users and other libraries and archives is treated
consistently (subject to a dealing with
a prescribed portion being confined to
printed published works).
Definitions
of ‘library’ and ‘archives’
7.22 The Committee’s issues paper noted that libraries generally
consider that it is not necessary for the term ‘library’
defined in the Act, subject to the proviso that a library to which the Act
applies not be conducted for profit. Section 18
of the Act provides, ‘For
the purposes of this Act, a library shall not be taken to be established or
conducted for profit
by reason only that the library is owned by a person
carrying on business for profit.’ It was further noted that, whilst
49 and 50 apply only to libraries that are not conducted for profit,
this limitation is not applied to copying under ss. 51, 51A,
110A and 110B;
that is, the provisions relating to copying unpublished copyright material and
copying for preservation. The Committee
sought comments on whether this is a
distinction that should be maintained.
7.23 The submissions the Committee received on this issue all stated that the
Act should be uniform in its provisions relating to
copying by libraries. It was
the view of these submitters that the provisions for copying by libraries should
apply only to libraries
that are not conducted for profit.
7.24 The Committee agrees that the provisions for copying by libraries should
be consistent throughout the Act. Contrary to the submissions,
however, the
Committee is of the view that all libraries, whether they be conducted for
profit or not, should be permitted to rely
on all the library provisions
regulating royalty-free copying. The Committee reached this conclusion for two
7.25 First, the Committee does not consider that permitting libraries that
are conducted for profit to perform royalty-free copying
in limited instances
will unreasonably prejudice the legitimate interests of copyright owners.
Profits accrued by libraries are also
likely to be derived from a range of
activities they engage in and not solely from the supply of requested
7.26 Second, determining whether or not a library is conducted for profit has
become an extremely difficult task. For example, if
a library is part of an
organisation that is conducted for profit, whether or not the library is
conducted for profit may depend
on the accounting practices of the organisation.
Any definition of what constitutes a library conducted for profit would need to
be long and detailed to cover every possible circumstance. This approach would
not contribute to the Committee’s aim of simplifying
It is the view of the Committee that libraries should continue to
be able to supply royalty-free copies of copyright material to users
libraries and archives, in response to specific requests and only in the limited
circumstances specified in paragraphs
7.57 and 7.58. The limited extent of
copying by libraries permitted by the Act stands in contrast to the exclusive
rights of publishers
as copyright owners or licensees. The Committee
does not expect that the provisions will encourage libraries to set up copying
as an alternative to publishers.
Recommendation
7.28 The Committee recommends that all provisions in the Act permitting
royalty-free copying by libraries (currently ss. 48A, 49,
50, 51, 51A,
104A, 110A and 110B) apply to all libraries, whether or not they are conducted
for profit.

must not be conducted for profit from the Act, s. 18 is

7.40 The submissions the Committee received from libraries and the LCA

where public records or other historical records are
