declaration system
7.42 When a user, not being a remote user, asks a library or archives to make
a copy of a published work for himself or herself, the
user must provide a
signed declaration stating that certain requirements are satisfied for the
copying to be non-infringing (s. 49).
When a library or archives makes for
a user or another library a copy of a published work
constitutes more than a reasonable portion of the work, or if the copying
done is for a remote user who has not provided a signed
request, or if the copy
is being supplied to a library that has previously received a copy of the work,
the librarian or archivist
responsible for the copying done must sign a similar
declaration (s. 50). Declarations are also required to be made and retained
when libraries and archives perform copying for the purposes of preservation
(ss. 51A and 110B). Declarations must be retained by
libraries and archives
for four years.
7.43 Inclusion of the declaration system was intended to act as a check
against excessive copying of copyright materials by libraries
and archives.
Requirements for the maintenance of these records are set out in ss. 203A
to 203F of the Act. Notations required to
be made on, or attached to, the copies
made are set out in ss. 203H(1) to 203H(5). No remuneration flows to
result of the making of these records. The principal
purpose of the declarations appears to be to remind libraries, archives and
their users of their copyright obligations and to enable copyright owners, if
they so choose, to monitor compliance with the copying
requirements.
7.44 Libraries and archives have identified deficiencies in the declaration
system. The main concern is the requirement that requests
made by users under
s. 49 be physically signed by the user. This prevents requests being made
digitally; for example, by email. Introduction
of a personal identification
number (PIN) registration system as a form of ‘electronic signature’
was suggested by libraries
and supported by educational institutions, the ACC,
and the LCA. If the declaration system were to be maintained, the Committee
support the introduction of a PIN registration system. But, for the
reasons given in the following paragraphs, it is the Committee’s
statutory provisions for a declaration system are no longer needed in the
7.45 During its consultations with libraries and archives the Committee heard
that a major deficiency in the declaration system is
that administration of the
records is labour intensive and diverts the financial resources of libraries and
archives away from areas
of more useful
expenditure, such as acquisition
of copyright materials. The Committee notes that an increase in the purchasing
of copyright materials
by libraries and archives would provide a direct
financial benefit to copyright owners, whereas the declaration system provides
direct benefit.
7.46 In its consultations with libraries, archives and copyright owners the
Committee sought comments on the reliance placed on, and
the rate of inspection
of, the records libraries and archives are required to retain under ss. 49,
50, 51A and 110B of the Act. Responses
to the Committee suggested that little or
no inspection of these records is undertaken by copyright owners or their
agents. It was
further stated by libraries that inspection of these records
would be unlikely to act as a deterrent against libraries and archives
infringing copies of copyright material since it is improbable that a librarian
or archivist would keep a record of their
own wrongdoing.
7.47 The Committee is of the view that any benefits for copyright owners that
may be derived from the declaration system in reducing
the amount of infringing
copying done by libraries and archives are greatly outweighed by the cost of the
system’s administration,
including the cost to copyright owners of
checking the records. The low rate of inspection of these declarations by
and their agents suggests that minimal reliance is placed on
the declarations. On the contrary, libraries and archives place greater
on the declaration system as a means of indemnification against possible suit
for copyright infringement.
7.48 The Committee notes that removing the declaration system will remove the
statutory immunity currently enjoyed by libraries.
A user, or library in
the case of inter-library copying, will still be required to make a request for
any copying done by a library
or archives for that user or library, and the
librarian or archivist responsible for the copying done must be satisfied that
copying is permitted under the Act. It will be for libraries and archives to
determine what procedures they require to be fulfilled
before making
royalty-free copies of copyright material. If challenged, they will need to
establish the basis for their copying,
be it a fair dealing or other statutory
In the absence of a statutory requirement for libraries and
archives to retain records, it would be up to the libraries and archives
choose whether to implement an alternative system of record keeping upon which
they could rely to defend any infringement action
that might be brought against
Recommendation
7.49 The Committee recommends that the requirement for declarations to be
made by both users and librarians and archivists when libraries
and archives
make copies of copyright material be removed from ss. 49, 50, 51A and 110B
of the Act.
7.50 Removal of the statutory requirement for libraries and archives to
administer a declaration system renders ss. 203A to 203F of
unnecessary. Similarly, ss. 203H(1) to 203H(5), which regulate the
notations to be made on, or attached to, copies made by
libraries and archives,
are no longer necessary.
Recommendation
7.51 Subject to acceptance of the recommendation that the requirement for
declarations be removed from the Act, the Committee recommends
ss. 203A to 203F and 203H(1) to 203H(5) be repealed.
by libraries and archives for users
Expansion of the
purposes for which libraries and archives can copy
7.52 Section 49 of the Act provides that users of libraries and archives may
request a copy of the whole, parts or reasonable portions
of works where the
copy is required by the user for the purpose of research or study. This
provision was recommended for inclusion
in the Act by the Spicer Committee as a
supplement to fair dealing for purposes of research or study provided under
s. 40. It was
considered that if a student were to be permitted to make a
copy of a work under fair dealing a librarian acting on the student’s
behalf should also be permitted to do
7.53 The issues paper sought comments on whether s. 49 should be
broadened to permit libraries and archives to make copies of works
not only for the purposes of research or study but also for all other purposes
recognised as being within the scope of
fair dealing. The current fair dealing
provisions with respect to works permit individuals to make copies of works for
for the additional purposes of criticism or review (s. 41),
reporting the news (s. 42) and giving professional legal advice
(s. 43(2)).
7.54 Submissions from libraries, educational institutions and the LCA
supported broadening the scope of s. 49 to permit libraries
and archives to
copy the whole, parts or reasonable portions of works for users for the same
purposes as under the current fair dealing
provisions. Libraries submitted that
it was ‘anomalous’ that individuals can make copies for
for certain purposes but a library or archives acting on
their behalf cannot.
7.55 The Committee is of the view that permitting libraries and archives to
perform copying for users for the purposes of research
or study but not for
other purposes permitted under fair dealing is a restriction in the Act that
should not be maintained. It considers
that the restriction should be removed,
and the Act further simplified, by repealing s. 49 and permitting the
copying currently conducted
by libraries and archives under this section to be
governed by the proposed fair dealing provision. Such an amendment to the Act
would permit libraries and archives to conduct dealings, by way of copying on
behalf of users, that would be assessed against the
‘fairness’ criteria (described in subsection (2) of the proposed
fair dealing provision—see paragraph
6.143) in order to determine whether
the dealing was ‘fair’. In determining the ‘fairness’ of
the dealing
the user’s purpose would be regarded as the relevant purpose
(see paragraphs 7.62 to 7.64).
7.56 The change just discussed would also permit a library or archives to
conduct a dealing by way of copying on behalf of a user
for purposes other than
research or study. As discussed in paragraph 6.143, the proposed fair dealing
model is open ended. A library
or archives would therefore be able to conduct a
dealing by way of copying for any purpose of a user, including those currently
in the Act, on the condition that such a dealing is regarded as
7.57 The Committee is of the view that a library or archives should be
permitted to conduct a dealing by way of copying under the
proposed fair dealing
provision only in response to a request made by the user and in instances where
the library or archives is
satisfied that the request constitutes a fair
dealing. With respect to libraries and archives copying subject matter other
for users, the Committee refers readers to the discussion and
recommendations in paragraphs 7.13 to 7.21.
7.58 Copying by libraries and archives on behalf of users should also be
permitted under the proposed quantitative test. Such copying
should, however, be
restricted to the user’s purpose of research or study (see paragraphs 7.67
Recommendations
7.59 The Committee recommends that s. 49 of the Act be repealed and
that copying by libraries and archives for users be governed by
the proposed
fair dealing provision and the proposed quantitative test.
7.60 The Committee recommends that libraries and archives be permitted to
perform copying for users in all instances where users would
be permitted to
make the copy for themselves under the proposed fair dealing provision or under
the proposed quantitative test.

7.61 The Committee notes the
case, which held that the
relevant purpose when determining whether a dealing is fair is that of the
person actually undertaking the
In the light of this decision the Committee considers that under the current

for one of the specified

7.62 In paragraph 6.99 the Committee notes that under the new open-ended fair
dealing model it would be open to a court, in assessing
the fairness of a

purpose of the person for whom the dealing
was conducted was recognised as being

that such an

7.63 With the exception of one member, the Committee is, however, of the view
that there should be a special exception to the principle
stated in the

Committee (see paragraph 7.52).
Recommendations
7.64 With the exception of two members, the Committee recommends that,
where a library or archives conducts a dealing by way of copying
on behalf of a
user under the proposed fair dealing provision, the user’s purpose be
regarded as the relevant purpose in ascertaining
the ‘fairness’ of
the dealing.
7.65 One dissenting Committee member supports the foregoing recommendation
on the condition that dealings by way of copying by libraries
and archives under
the proposed fair dealing provision do not include the electronic reproduction
of copyright material or dealings
conducted for profit.
7.66 The other dissenting member recommends that no exception to the
principle stated in the
case apply to a dealing by way of
copying by a library or archives on behalf of a user under the proposed fair
dealing provision.
7.67 In paragraph 6.104 the Committee notes that, subject to the exception
stated in paragraph 7.68, a person should not be able to

this view by applying the principle
stated in the
concluded that it is difficult to envisage circumstances in which a person, for
their own purpose of research or study, would
conduct a dealing under the
quantitative test on behalf of another.
7.68 With the exception of one member, the Committee is of the view that an
exception to the foregoing should apply in relation to
a dealing by way of
copying by a library or archives under the proposed quantitative test. As stated
in relation to fair dealing,
the Committee majority is of the view that such an
exception is necessary if libraries and archives are to be permitted to perform
copying for users in all instances where users would be permitted to make the
copy for themselves. The Committee majority therefore
considers that libraries
and archives should be permitted to make a copy on behalf of a user in response
to a verifiable request
by the user stating that the copy is for the purpose of
their own research or study and that they have not previously been supplied
a copy of the same article or other work or the same part of the article or
other work, as the case may be, by a library or
7.69 Such a dealing would operate as a special exception to the principle
stated in the
case because the user’s purpose would be the
relevant purpose.
7.70 The Committee majority also notes that such an exception is necessary if
libraries and archives are to be permitted to conduct
the same level of copying
under the proposed fair dealing provision and proposed quantitative test as they
currently do under s.
49 of the Act. Subject to s. 49(3) (discussed in
paragraphs 7.73 and 7.80 to 7.82), s. 49 operates as a deeming provision
and permits
a library or archive to copy works that fall within the scope of the
quantitative test where the material is for a user’s purpose
of research
or study. Permitting libraries and archives to copy under the quantitative test
will ensure that libraries and archives
can continue to copy articles and
smaller portions of works on behalf of their users.
Recommendations
7.71 With the exception of one member, the Committee recommends that a
library or archives be able to conduct a dealing by way of

user stating that the copy is for
the purpose of their own research or study and
that they have not previously been supplied with a copy of the same article or
work or the same part of the article or other work, as the case may be, by
a library or archives.

for profit.
restrictions to copying by libraries and archives under s. 49
7.73 The Committee notes that under s. 49 certain mandatory requirements
restrict a library’s or archives’s ability to
copy on behalf of a
user. The most important restrictions are as follows:
s. 49(3), which provides that,
where a charge is made by a library or archives for making and supplying a copy
of the whole, part
or a reasonable portion of a work to a user, the library or
archives may not rely on the immunity provided in ss. 49(2) and 49(2C)
this copying if the amount of the charge to the user exceeds the cost of making
and supplying the copy;
which provides that, where a library or archives is copying the whole of a work
(other than an article contained in a periodical
publication) or a part of such
a work that contains more than a reasonable portion, the work must form part of
the library’s
or archives’s collection. In addition, the librarian
or archivist must be satisfied that a copy of the work cannot be obtained
a reasonable time at an ordinary commercial
Application
of the restrictions to the proposed fair dealing provision
7.74 In relation to copying by libraries and archives under the proposed fair
dealing provision, the Committee (with the exception
of two members) is of the
view that these restrictions should not be specifically retained. The Committee
majority is of the view
that the matters raised in ss. 49(3) and 49(5) will
be considered in the determination of the ‘fairness’ of the dealing,
having regard to the relevant criteria (described in subsection (2) of the
proposed fair dealing provision—see paragraph 6.143).
Two members of the
Committee are of the view that the limitations should be retained in the Act and
included as part of the proposed
fair dealing provision.
7.75 In relation to s. 49(3), the majority of the Committee notes that
the proposed fair dealing provision is capable of applying
to copying done for
any purpose
and that this could include for the purpose of making a
profit. The commercial nature of the dealing would be examined by a court
consideration of ‘the purpose and character of the dealing’ (see
paragraph (2(a)) of the proposed provision in
paragraph 6.143). The Committee
notes that, in the application of the general fair dealing principles, the fact
that the purpose
of the dealing is commercial would weigh against the finding of
a fair dealing (see paragraphs 5.14 to 5.16). The majority of the
Committee is
of the view that libraries and archives should not be denied the opportunity to
supply requested copies for profit to
other bodies or individuals that request
the making of a fair dealing copy on their behalf.
7.76 A minority of the Committee considers that libraries and archives should
be restricted to supplying requested copies on a cost-recovery
basis because of
their unique position in being large depositories of copyright material. The
minority considers that libraries and
archives should not be permitted to charge
at more than a cost-recovery rate for copying and that no definition of cost
should be included in the Act. What is considered to be within cost
recovery would continue to be determined by the courts.
7.77 In relation to s. 49(5), the Committee notes that the possibility
of obtaining the work within a reasonable time at an ordinary
commercial price
is one of the factors listed for consideration in determining whether the
dealing is ‘fair’. The majority
of the Committee is of the view that
having this requirement as one of the factors for consideration in the fair
dealing provision
is sufficient to maintain the current balance between the
interests of copyright owners and the public interest of access to information.
7.78 Two members of the Committee are of the view that this requirement
should be mandatory, not just a factor for consideration.
They consider that a
section to the effect of the current s. 49(5) should be added to the
proposed fair dealing provision. Retaining
the requirement as mandatory would
mean that no more than a reasonable portion of a work of which the library or
archives owns a
copy would ever be able to be copied for a user unless the work
is no longer in print or in stock.
Recommendation
7.79 With the exception of two members, the Committee recommends that
sections that maintain the mandatory effect of the current ss.
49(5) of the Act not be included as part of the proposed fair dealing provision.
The dissenting Committee members recommend
that the effect of ss. 49(3) and
49(5) be retained in the Act.
Application of the
restrictions to the proposed quantitative test
7.80 Of the two restrictions mentioned in paragraph 7.79, s. 49(3)
is the only constraint that currently applies to libraries and
archives where an
article or portion of a work that falls within the scope of the quantitative
test is copied on behalf of a user
for the purpose of research or study. The
restriction provided in s. 49(5) applies only where an amount greater than
that prescribed
by the quantitative test is copied.
7.81 The Committee notes that the proposed quantitative test operates as a
simple deeming provision and functions separately from
the concept of
‘fair dealing’: there is no requirement for recourse to the criteria
of ‘fairness’ (see paragraph
6.67). The Committee is therefore of
the view that s. 49(3) should be specifically retained in relation to the
copying of an article
or part of a work by a library or archive under the
quantitative test on behalf of a user. The Committee considers that if a library
or archive wishes to copy on behalf of a user on a for-profit basis such a
dealing should be conducted under the proposed fair dealing
provision and
assessed against the relevant ‘fairness’ criteria (see paragraph
Recommendation
7.82 The Committee recommends that the mandatory effect of the current
s. 49(3) be included as part of the proposed quantitative test.
Committee further recommends that a library or archives be able to conduct a
dealing by way of copying under the quantitative
test on behalf of a user on a
cost-recovery basis only.
Specificity
of a user’s request and copying samples for users
7.83 The issues paper noted that libraries consider that remote users who do
not have access to bibliographic facilities are disadvantaged
by the requirement
in the Act that the user specify a particular work or article when requesting
copies from libraries and archives.
Comments were sought on the libraries’
proposal that these users be permitted to make requests for copies of
unspecified works
on a specified subject, rather than have to request
a particular work or article.
7.84 Libraries’ submissions to the Committee stressed that the needs of
people who cannot physically attend a library or archives
are the same as the
needs of those who can. The submissions from the Council of Australian
University Librarians and the Library
and Information Service WA pointed out
that permitting users in a remote location to specify a subject, rather than a
work, in their request to a library or archives is to provide them
with service equal to that of other library users. It was submitted
librarians commonly help users identify relevant material: permitting them to
select material on behalf of a user in a remote
location is, it was submitted,
similar to the assistance they provide to other library users.
7.85 Submissions to the Committee from libraries, educational institutions
and the LCA supported permitting users in remote locations
to make a request to
a library or archives for material by reference to subject only.
7.86 It was noted in the issues paper that copyright owners were not
supportive of remote users being able to make requests on a subject
rather than
a particular work or article. Copyright owners are of the view that copying for
this type of request cannot be considered
to be a fair dealing. They suggested
that a library or archives should provide users in a remote location with a
bibliographic list
from which a particular work or article could then be
requested. The issues paper sought comments on this approach.
7.87 Libraries and educational institutions were not supportive of supplying
bibliographic lists to remote users because of the necessary
increase in
administration this would entail, especially in the creation of the lists. ACLIS
would support the supply of bibliographical
lists provided that
‘unnecessarily high transaction costs’ could be
7.88 Libraries and educational institutions suggested that the administrative
burden of supplying bibliographic lists to remote users
could be reduced by
permitting libraries and archives to supply samples and content pages of works
to remote users. The main mode
of supply contemplated was electronic
transmission. Digital transmission of copyright material is discussed in
paragraphs 7.175 to
7.89 Libraries submitted that the supply of abstracts, previews or samples of
a work could better enable remote users to determine
if they required a copy of
the whole or a reasonable portion of a particular work. Submissions to the
Committee from the ACC and
the LCA did not support libraries and archives being
permitted to supply abstracts, samples, or contents pages of works to users.
is noted, however, as stated, that the LCA supported remote users being able to
request material on specified subjects, eliminating
the need for a library or
archives to supply bibliographic lists.
7.90 It is the Committee’s view that a special provision to permit
copying by libraries and archives for remote users on the
basis of a request on
a specified subject, rather than with respect to a particular work or subject
matter other than a work in the
library’s or archives’s collection,
is not warranted. The Committee recommends in paragraph 7.59 that s. 49 of
be repealed and the copying currently regulated by this section be
regulated instead by the proposed fair dealing provision and the
quantitative test. The Committee considers that the making and supplying of
copies of copyright material to a remote user
in response to a general request
on a specified subject should be assessed on a case-by-case basis to determine
whether the copying
constitutes a fair dealing. Some degree of specificity in
the request would be required, but this would depend on the subject being
researched by the remote user. The specificity required in the request would be
determined by what would be considered fair in the
circumstances.
7.91 The Committee notes that the degree of the user’s remoteness from
a library would be an important factor for determining
whether the copying done
by a library or archives in response to a request by subject only would be
‘fair’. It is the
view of the Committee that such copying is likely
to be considered a fair dealing only in instances where the user is in a
sufficiently
remote location from a library. In general, this would mean where
the user is geographically isolated and,
as a result of this
would have substantial difficulty in personally attending a
7.92 Permitting copying for remote users’ requests on a specified
subject in instances where this would constitute a fair dealing
means that it
will not be necessary to consider allowing libraries or archives to supply
copies of abstracts, contents pages or samples
from copyright material for the
purpose of enabling a user to request a specific title. It is noted, however,
that remote users may
prefer to make a more informed choice in their requests to
libraries and archives. The proposed fair dealing provision is not limited
specific set of instances that would constitute a fair dealing. The Committee is
of the view that a library or archives providing
an abstract or contents page to
a sufficiently remote user, as described in paragraph 7.91, for the purpose of
the user being able
to request a particular title could constitute a fair
dealing. The Committee considers that copying samples from the main body of
work for the purposes of enabling a remote user to determine whether they would
like to request a copy of the whole, a part or
a reasonable portion of the work
is also capable of falling within what constitutes a fair dealing. The Committee
considers, however,
that such circumstances would be limited.
Recommendation
7.93 The Committee recommends that no special provision be introduced into
the Act to permit a library or archives to give effect
to a remote user’s
request to copy unspecified works on a specified subject rather than with
respect to a particular work.
Copying by libraries and archives for remote users
should be regulated by the principles of fair dealing.
by libraries and archives for other libraries or archives
7.94 Section 50 of the Act permits a library or archives to provide a copy of
the whole, a part or a reasonable portion of a work
for a user of another
library or archives when the library or archives the user has attended does not
own a copy of the work (s.
50(1)(b)). Section 50 also permits a library or
archives to supply a single copy of the whole, a part or a reasonable portion of
a work to another library or archives for the purpose of including the copy in
the collection of the receiving library or archives
(s. 50(1)(a)). The
supply of a subsequent copy for this purpose is permitted only if the prior copy
sent has been lost, destroyed
or damaged (s. 50(7)). (Inter-library copying
for the purpose of assisting members of parliament in their duties
(s. 50(1)(aa)) is
discussed in paragraph 7.167.) The amount permitted to be
copied is the same under ss. 49 and 50 of the Act.
7.95 As with s. 49, it is the view of the Committee that the copying
currently permitted under s. 50 is copying to which the proposed
dealing provision and the proposed quantitative test could apply. The Committee
further considers that the purposes for copying
that are listed in the current
s. 50 are purposes that would be considered to be fair dealings. This
should be outlined in the explanatory
memorandum to the Bill that makes the
7.96 There are in s. 50 provisions equivalent to those in the current
s. 49 of the Act, prohibiting a library charging more than cost
for copies provided (s. 50(6)) and requiring that the work form part of the
collection of the library or archives and not
be available within a reasonable
time for a commercial price if the copy is of the whole or more than a
reasonable portion of a work
(other than an article contained in a periodical
publication) (s. 50(7A)). For the reasons given in paragraphs 7.74 to 7.78
respect to s. 49, two members of the Committee are of the view that
these requirements should be included as mandatory in the proposed
fair dealing
provision with respect to copying done by libraries and archives. For the
reasons given in paragraphs 7.80 and 7.81
with respect to s. 49, the
Committee is of the view that the restriction provided under s. 50(6)
should apply to the proposed quantitative
test with respect to dealings by way
of copying done by libraries and archives.
Recommendations
7.97 The Committee recommends that s. 50 of the Act be repealed and
that copying by libraries and archives that was previously done
under s. 50
be governed by the proposed fair dealing provision and the proposed quantitative
7.98 Two members of the Committee recommend that this be subject to the
condition that the proposed fair dealing provision be drafted
to restrict
copying by libraries and archives for other libraries and archives in ways
similar to that now provided for in ss. 50(6)
and 50(7A) of the Act.
7.99 The Committee recommends that the mandatory effect of the current
s. 50(6) of the Act be included as part of the proposed quantitative
of works, sound recordings and cinematograph films for preservation
7.100 Instances when a library or archives may make a royalty-free copy of a
work in its collection for the purpose of preservation
are listed in
s. 51A(1) of the Act. This form of royalty-free copying is permitted
whenever the work is in manuscript form
original artistic
work (s. 51A(1)(a)) or
the work has been damaged or has
deteriorated (s. 51A(1)(b)), or if it has been lost or stolen
(s. 51A(1)(c)).
7.101 The issues paper sought comments on proposals by libraries and archives
that royalty-free copying for preservation purposes
extend to all rare material
that is not available for purchase and be permitted
deteriorated or is damaged, lost or stolen. Support for broadening the scope of
s. 51A(1) in this manner was evident in
submissions from educational
institutions and reiterated in submissions from libraries and archives. The LCA
agreed that rare material
that is not available for purchase should be permitted
to be copied under s. 51A(1)(a), but it did not support preservation copies
being able to be made before copyright material has been damaged since this
‘would allow the copying of all material when temporarily
7.102 Through s. 51A(2), s. 51A also provides for the making of a single
royalty-free microform copy of a work for purposes not falling
s. 51A(1); that is, for purposes that are outside the preservation purposes
listed in the Act. By virtue of s. 51A(3), however,
when a copy of a work
is made under s. 51A(2) the work from which the copy is made must be
destroyed as soon as practicable. Submissions
from libraries, archives and
educational institutions expressed strong opposition to any requirement that a
work be destroyed after
being copied onto microform as against
decision being left to the discretion of the library.
7.103 As a general principle, the Committee takes the view that royalty-free
dealing with a copyright owner’s works should be
available only if that
dealing can be considered fair; that is, the dealing is consistent with an
equitable balance between the interests
of owners and users. The Committee
considers that this approach should govern the right of libraries and archives
to make preservation
copies of copyright material: the current limits on copying
preservation in s. 51A(1) are overly restrictive. Conversely,
the scope of s. 51A(2) is unjustifiably broad and this is reflected
restrictions in s. 51A(3). An approach preferable to these blanket
prescriptive
provisions would be for libraries and archives to assess
preservation requirements on a case-by-case basis, using the criteria set
the proposed fair dealing provision.
7.104 The current s. 51A provides that works held in published form may
be copied only if the librarian or archivist is satisfied
that a copy (not being
a second-hand copy) of the work cannot be obtained within a reasonable time at
an ordinary commercial price
(s. 51A(4)). Two members of the Committee are
of the view that, as with the corresponding provisions in ss. 49(5) and
50(7) of the
Act, the effect of s. 51A(4) should be retained as an
additional, mandatory requirement in the proposed fair dealing provision.
7.105 The two Committee members note that, although the current
s. 51A(4) prevents the copying of works that can be obtained within
reasonable time at an ordinary commercial price, the copying of less than a
reasonable portion of a work for preservation purposes
will still be permitted
by the Act, even if another copy is available within a reasonable time at an
ordinary commercial price. This
the effect of the current
s. 50 is that where less than a reasonable portion of the work is required
to be copied—for example,
to replace a damaged, deteriorated, lost or
stolen portion of a work—this portion may be copied and supplied by
another library
that owns a copy of the work. This amount of copying will be
maintained under the proposed fair dealing provision.
7.106 The current s. 51A limits copying for preservation of works to
works that form, or formed, part of the collection of the library
or archives
undertaking the preservation copying. The majority of the Committee considers
that this restriction should be maintained.
A section giving effect to this
requirement could be added to the proposed fair dealing provision.
Committee member is concerned, however, that in some instances the library
holding the material in need of preservation copying
may not have the facilities
to make a suitable copy; that member believes that in such circumstances another
library or archives
with the requisite facilities should be permitted to make
the preservation copy.
7.107 The Committee does not support the retention of s. 51A(2). It
considers that if a purpose for copying cannot be characterised
dealing no royalty-free copy should be able to be made. Repeal of s. 51A(2)
would also remove the need for any requirement
that a work be destroyed.
Recommendation
7.108 Subject to its recommendation in paragraph 7.136
in relation to copying for the preservation of unpublished works, the Committee
recommends that s. 51A of the Act be repealed. Two
members of the
Committee recommend that this be subject to the inclusion in the Act of a
section that maintains the mandatory effect
of the current s. 51A(4) as
part of the proposed fair dealing provision.
7.109 The issues paper noted that it is unclear whether the Act allows more
than one copy of a work to be made for preservation. Submissions
the Committee
received suggested that some copying formats (such as digitisation) necessitate
the creation of more than one copy
of the work and that if only one copy were
permitted to be made preservation of some works, or preservation in particular
could not be undertaken. The ACC submitted that, although the Act
should allow for more than one copy to be made without infringement
accommodate preservation methods that require a number of copies to be made, the
number of copies able to be made should be limited.
The Committee notes that
under the proposed fair dealing provision the number of copies made for
preservation purposes would be limited
to what would be fair in each
circumstance.
7.110 A majority of the Committee is of the view that the making of
preservation copies in digital format may be considered to be
a fair dealing. It
notes that, as part of the Digital Agenda reforms, the Government has decided to
extend s. 51A into the digital
environment. One member of the Committee
foresees problems with permitting the digitisation of material for preservation
if the original work was not in digital form. Digitisation of copyright
material is discussed further in paragraphs 7.169 to 7.174.
7.111 The Australian Society of Archivists (ASA) submitted that if the
provisions for copying for preservation were amended to permit
the making of more than one
preservation copy in whatever format is considered appropriate by the library or
archives making the copy,
the making of
copies before material is damaged,
retention of the original item after copying,
then s. 51AA,
which regulates the copying of works in the Australian Archives, would become
redundant. The Committee has already noted
that s. 51A does not require the

listed in s. 51A(1).
7.112 The Committee is of the view that the scope of copying described by the
ASA could be considered a fair dealing in some instances.
It considers
that, for reasons similar to those described in relation to s. 51A, the
provision for the copying of works in the Australian
Archives (s. 51AA)
should also be subsumed by the proposed fair dealing provision.
Recommendation
7.113 The Committee recommends that s. 51AA of the Act be repealed.
Copying currently regulated by this provision should instead be
regulated by the

7.117 Section 110B of the Act currently restricts preservation copying to
sound recordings and cinematograph films. The issues paper
sought comments on
the proposal by libraries and archives that copying for preservation be extended
to include preservation copying
of all subject matter other than works. This
would include the copying of sound and television broadcasts. Support for
s. 110B to apply to a wider class of copyright material than that
to which it currently applies was expressed in the submissions
from libraries
and educational institutions.
7.118 The ACC expressed the view that a library or archives making a copy of
a broadcast is making the copy not for preservation purposes
but for the purpose
of adding to its collection. This is because the ACC considers that the
preservation provisions relate only to
‘original’ physical
embodiments of material and that broadcasts are generally recorded and stored in
a broadcaster’s
own archives. The Committee’s consultations with
libraries suggest that this is not uniformly the case and that
researchers’
access to this material may be restricted if a library or
archives is not permitted to make a copy. The Committee notes that preservation
copying of works permitted in the Act extends beyond the copying of original
manuscripts.
7.119 A majority of the Committee agrees with the ACC’s submission. It
seems that the policy behind the preservation copying
provisions is to enable
libraries and archives to preserve articles in their holdings. The majority of
the Committee is uncertain
that either libraries or archives should have a role
in copying broadcasts for users. Where, however, a library or an archives has
its holdings a copy of a broadcast, it should be able to make a further copy,
for the purpose of preserving the original, without
infringing copyright in the
recorded broadcast or any of the other underlying copyright subject matter. One
member of the Committee
is of the view that an archives such as the National
Film and Sound Archive should be able to make copies of broadcasts with a view
to preserving the national heritage in sound and vision.
Recommendation
7.120 A majority of the Committee recommends that no change to the Act be
made to permit libraries or archives to copy broadcasts
without the permission
of the copyright owner. If a copy of an article embodying a broadcast needs to
be made for the purpose of
preservation of the copyright materials embodied in
that article (including the broadcast), copying by the library or archives
be permitted. One member of the Committee is of the view that an archives
such as the National Film and Sound Archive should be able
to make copies of
broadcasts with a view to preserving the national heritage in sound and
7.121 In paragraphs 7.13 to 7.21 the Committee proposes that, wherever
appropriate, works and subject matter other than works be treated
consistently
within the Act. The requirements for making a preservation copy under
s. 110B are substantially similar to those listed
for works under
s. 51A. As with preservation copies of works, the Committee is of the view
that in some instances copying should
be permitted to preserve a sound
recording, cinematograph film or any audiovisual item against damage, loss or
destruction. Whether
copying should be permitted in such circumstances is, in
the Committee’s view, best determined through reference to the proposed
fair dealing provision.
Recommendation
7.122 The Committee recommends that s. 110B of the Act be repealed.
Copying of audiovisual items for the purpose of preservation should
determined by the general principles of fair dealing.
7.123 Section 110B(3) provides that, before a preservation copy can be made,
the librarian or archivist must be satisfied that a copy
(not being a
second-hand copy) of the copyright material cannot be obtained within a

As with preservation copying of

with respect to copying by libraries and archives.

Unpublished
works and fair dealing
7.131 Section 51 of the Act defines limited circumstances in which a person,
or a library or archives acting in response to a request
from a user, may copy
an unpublished work for the purpose of research or study or with a view to
publication. Section 52 regulates
the publication of these unpublished works
As with other sections currently regulating copying done by libraries and
archives, the Committee has examined the feasibility of
repealing ss. 51
and 52 and having the copying currently permitted under those sections regulated
instead by the proposed fair dealing
7.132 The relationship between unpublished works and fair dealing is
discussed in paragraphs 5.18 to 5.22: the Committee expresses
conclusion that case law in Australia, the United Kingdom and the United States
suggests that it is unlikely that copying
or publication of an unpublished work
would be considered a fair dealing. The Committee notes in paragraph 5.18
that the US fair
use provision under s. 107 of the US
which lists factors for consideration that are similar to those
in the Committee’s proposed fair dealing provision, has been amended
to provide that ‘[t]he fact that a work is unpublished
shall not itself
bar a finding of fair use if such finding is made upon consideration of all the
above factors.’
The Committee considers that insertion of a similar statement in the
Australian Act would not clearly maintain the type of copying
and publication
currently permitted under the existing ss. 51 and 52. It considers that, if
libraries and archives are to continue to be able to copy and
publish unpublished works in special circumstances, and if libraries
archives are to continue to be able to copy such works for users, ss. 51
and 52 should be retained in the Act. Amendments to
simplify these provisions
are discussed in the following paragraphs.
Recommendation
7.133 The Committee recommends that ss. 51 and 52 remain in the Act,
but in a simplified form.
Preservation
of unpublished works
7.134 The Committee notes that the existing provisions for preservation
copying contemplate the copying of unpublished works for preservation
(s. 51A(5) of the Act). The Committee recommends in paragraph 7.108 and
7.113 that ss. 51A and 51AA be repealed and that
copying for preservation
be regulated by the proposed fair dealing provision. If this recommendation is
adopted, the ability of libraries
and archives to copy unpublished works for
preservation purposes may be placed in doubt by the general presumption case law
has developed
against finding the copying of unpublished copyright material to
be within copying permitted by fair dealing.
7.135 It is the Committee’s opinion that, rather than introduce a new
provision to retain the ability of a library or archives
to copy unpublished
works for preservation purposes, it would be better to amend the provisions
regulating the copying of unpublished
works. Section 51 currently permits
copying for the purpose of research or study or with a view to publication: the
purposes for
which copying is permitted under s. 51 could be extended to
permit copying for preservation.
Recommendation
7.136 The Committee recommends that copying for the purpose of preservation
be added to s. 51 of the Act.

Photographs
and engravings
7.149 Section 52(1) of the Act provides that, in certain limited
circumstances set out in the section,
published literary, dramatic or musical work incorporates the whole or a part of
an unpublished work
kept in a library or archives. Libraries have submitted that

photographs or engravings that are
incorporated in a literary,
dramatic or musical work. Such an extension would permit the publication of
photographs or engravings
as single works; for example, as a poster.
7.150 The Committee notes that s. 52 as it is currently drafted treats
all unpublished
works in a consistent manner. Just as unpublished
photographs or engravings that are not incorporated in a literary, dramatic or
work cannot be published, neither can the whole or parts of unpublished
works such as poems, diary instalments, letters, musical
scores or dramatic
texts that are not so incorporated. Adding the amendment suggested by libraries
would single out photographs and
engravings for special attention and will not
aid in simplifying the Act.

Recommendation

7.156 The submission from the State Library of New South Wales expressed
concern that there was inequity in the Act between the period
of protection
given to unpublished works and to unpublished sound recordings and cinematograph
films before they may be copied under
ss. 51 and 110A.
7.157 The only significant difference in the application of ss. 51 and
110A is the differing periods of protection before unpublished
material can be copied. Both ss. 51 and 110A specify a minimum period of
protection that accords with the amount of time
for which copyright would have
subsisted in the material had it been published; that is, the life of the author
and 50 years for
works and 50 years from the year of publication for sound
recordings and cinematograph films. The current s. 51, however,
an additional requirement that 75 years must also have passed since the work was
made. This may or may not extend the period of
protection beyond the length of
time for which copyright would have subsisted had the work been published.
7.158 The Committee recommends in paragraph
7.144 that the extra
period of protection required for unpublished works be removed. Removal of this
requirement means that unpublished
works and unpublished sound recordings and
cinematograph films are treated consistently within the Act.

protection given to unpublished sound recordings and cinematograph
s. 110A of the Act.

there is no

and cinematograph

by parliamentary libraries
7.162 The Committee’s issues paper sought comments on whether
ss. 48A and 104A of the Act need to state specifically that they
digital copying and whether their operation should be extended so that members
of any parliament—Federal or State
and Territory—have access to
copies made by any parliamentary library.
7.163 Submissions from libraries called for a clarification that these
provisions apply to digital copying. Since ss. 48A and 104A
are broadly
drafted to permit ‘anything done’, however, the Committee sees no
need to refer specifically to digital copying.
7.164 Libraries expressed support for provision being made for members of the
Federal Parliament to have the same access to copyright
State and Territory parliamentary libraries
they currently
enjoy at the Federal Parliamentary Library. Similarly, it was suggested that
State and Territory parliamentarians should
have the same
parliamentary libraries as they do in
their own. It was claimed that promoting the interests of informed
government requires such reciprocal
access. Submissions from copyright
owners and the LCA opposed any extension of the provisions for
parliamentary libraries.
7.165 The Committee is of the view that parliamentarians, whether Federal or
State and Territory, should have access to copyright
ss. 48A and 104A at any parliamentary library. It does not consider that
such an approach would adversely affect the interests
of copyright owners.
7.166 The Committee made inquiries about the practical operation of and
reliance placed on ss. 48A and 104A with a view to determining
these provisions could be subsumed by the revised fair dealing provision. The
responses the Committee received through written
submissions and during
consultations with interest groups described the volume of and speed with which
copies are required by members
of parliament in pursuit of their parliamentary
duties. The Committee is of the view that, given the special pressures
experienced
by parliamentary libraries, it is not appropriate to regulate
copying by parliamentary libraries through the proposed fair dealing
The Committee considers that the provisions relating to the use of copyright
material by parliamentary libraries should
be maintained, but in an amended
form. The provisions should be amended, in such a way as to not reduce the level
of certainty currently
provided in ss. 48A and 104A, to apply to both works
and subject matter other than works and to permit members of any parliament
make use of all parliamentary libraries.
7.167 In relation to inter-library copying for members of parliament
(s. 50(1)(aa)), the Committee notes its recommendation that s.
repealed and inter-library copying be regulated by the principles of fair
dealing. It is the Committee’s view that copying
non-parliamentary library for the purpose of supplying copyright material to a
parliamentary library that has requested
the material to assist a person who is
a member of a parliament in the performance of their duties as such a member
would be considered
to be a fair dealing.
Recommendation
7.168 The Committee recommends that ss. 48A and 104A of the Act be
amalgamated. The majority of the Committee recommends that this
section provide
that the copyright in works or subject matter other than works
infringed by anything done, for the sole purpose of assisting a person who is a
member of an Australian parliament in the performance
of their duties, by an
authorised officer of a library with the principal purpose of providing library
services for members of an
Australian parliament. A member of the Committee does
not agree that ‘anything done’ should include the reproduction
supply of material electronically.
digital environment
and archives making digital copies of materials held in their collections
7.169 The Committee’s issues paper invited comment on whether the Act
should make provision for libraries and archives to make
royalty-free digital
copies for the purpose of users accessing the copies electronically from within
or outside libraries and archives
and, if so, what limitations, if any, should
apply to these dealings.
7.170 The Committee recognises that, in providing users with access to
may need to exercise
rights other than the right of reproduction: they may be doing more than copying
the copyright material. The
Federal Government has stated that it intends to
introduce into the Act a new broadly based, technology-neutral right of
communication
to the public. This right will encompass acts of electronic
transmission such as email and making copyright material available to
through the Internet. The right of communication to the public will be subject
to statutory exceptions such as fair dealing,
as is the right of
reproduction.
7.171 The Committee notes that the effect of the recommendations it makes in
paragraphs 7.100 to 7.113 is to permit libraries and
archives to create digital
copies of materials held in their collections where this form of copying would
amount to a fair dealing.
For example, a preservation copy of a work in the
collection of a library or archives collection may be permitted to be made in a
digital format.
7.172 The remainder of this chapter deals with other instances when the
making of a digital copy by a library or archives could be
considered to be a
fair dealing. Apart from the example of copying for the purpose of preservation
just given, there are two other
obvious purposes for which libraries and
archives may wish to make digital copies of material held in their
collection:
to supply digital copies to users
and other libraries and archives in response to a request for the
to make digital copies
available for browsing by users.
7.173 One member of the
Committee does not agree that libraries and archives should be able to digitise
material that is not originally
in digital form without the prior permission of
the copyright owner. That member considers that libraries would be making a
transformative
use of the work, thereby exercising a right that in some cases
the copyright owner may have chosen not to exercise. Another Committee
considers that libraries and archives should be able to digitise copyright
materials that are not in digital form solely for
preservation purposes;
digitisation for any other purpose, in that member’s view, should occur
only with the permission of
the copyright owner.
7.174 Unless otherwise specified, the views expressed in paragraphs 7.175 to
7.188 are the views of the majority of the Committee.
of digital copies to users and other libraries and archives
7.175 In its
Computer Software Protection
report the Committee
recommended that libraries and archives be able to make royalty-free digital
copies (including electronic transmission
of a copy stored in digital form and
the loan of a digital copy of a work on a carrier such as floppy disk) for the
purpose of providing
copies to users and other libraries and archives under
ss. 49 and 50 of the Act.
Committee notes that in approving the introduction of a new right of
communication to the public the Government has also decided
that libraries
should, at the request of users, be able to make and supply a digital copy of a
work on the same terms as they can
now make a hard copy.
7.176 The Committee noted in its
Computer Software Protection
and reiterates here, that the effect of its recommendation is not to permit
systematic digitisation of a library’s
or archives’s collection.
Under the current ss. 49 and 50 of the Act a library or archives is
permitted to make a copy of the
part, or a reasonable portion of
a particular work only in response to a request made for that particular work.
Copying of more than
a reasonable portion of a work
(other than an
article contained in a periodical publication) is permitted only if the
authorised officer is, after reasonable investigation,
satisfied that a copy
(not being a second-hand copy) of the work cannot be obtained within a
reasonable time at an ordinary commercial
price. The format in which a copy may
be made is not specified. Under the proposed fair dealing provision, these
limitations will
continue to be factors for consideration in determining whether
the royalty-free copying done by a library or archives is permitted
by the Act.
7.177 Digital copies made by libraries and archives for transmission in
response to a request made by a user or another library or
archives should be
capable of being found to be a fair dealing. For example, a majority of the
Committee is of the view that
the supply of digital copies of copyright
by email should be treated as analogous to a library supplying
copies of the same material in print form by mail or by fax.
7.178 A minority of the Committee is of the view that if more than a
reasonable portion of a work (other than an article contained
in a periodical
publication) is sought, libraries and archives should be permitted to make
digital copies only if the librarian or
archivist is satisfied that a copy (not
being a second-hand copy) of the work cannot be obtained within a reasonable
time at an ordinary
commercial price. The minority considers that this
requirement should operate with respect to the availability of works in both
and digital form.
7.179 The Committee recommends in paragraph 7.21 that, wherever appropriate,
works and subject matter other than works be treated
consistently within the
Act. It is the Committee’s view that libraries and archives should be able
to make digital copies,
in response to a user’s request, of both works and
subject matter other than works in the circumstances currently contemplated
ss. 49 and 50 if such copying comes within the proposed new fair dealing
7.180 Digital copies made by libraries and archives in response to a request
should not be used for any purpose other than to supply
the copy to the person
who requested the copy. This means, for example, that a library or archives
would not be permitted to make
the digital copy available to the public by
placing it on a publicly accessible database maintained by the library or
archives. The
question of whether libraries and archives should be permitted to
make digital copies of materials held in their collections for
the purpose of
placing these digital copies on a database accessible by the public is discussed
in the following paragraphs.
digital copies available for browsing by users
7.181 Submissions from libraries and educational institutions supported
permitting libraries and archives to make digital copies available
for browsing
by users. Some educational institutions submitted that remuneration to copyright
owners should be payable when a digital
copy of their material is made.
Libraries submitted that, although the technology for gaining access to
the fundamental principles on which copyright
law is based have not. Libraries and archives, it was submitted, should not be
from using new technologies as they arise. The LCA submitted that
libraries and archives should be permitted to make digital copies
for digital
browsing only if a copyright owner has consented to their work being used in
this manner.
7.182 Copyright owners did not support libraries and archives being permitted
to make royalty-free digital copies of copyright material
for users to browse
digitally. The Australian Publishers Association submitted that recent changes
in technology have created a ‘new
paradigm’ that has substantially
altered normal practices for exploiting a work. CAL stated that viewing
from a computer terminal will soon become the main method of
exploiting a work. Whereas in the past the copying of a work would be
a subsidiary use, digital delivery of copyright material is developing into a
main use. Similarly, Screenrights stated
in its submission that permitting
digital copies to be browsed ‘is analogous to sales of many physical
Recommendation
7.183 The majority of the Committee recommends that no special provision be
included in the Act to permit libraries and archives to
digitise copyright
material held in their collections for the purpose of making digital copies
available for browsing by users.
7.184 The majority of the Committee notes that digitisation of copyright
material held in the collections of libraries and archives
may be permitted in
limited instances under the proposed fair dealing provision.
Recommendation
7.185 One member of the Committee recommends that libraries and archives be
given a separate right to digitise works and subject matter
other than works
held in their collections for the purpose of making digital copies available for
browsing by users, but only where
the copyright material being digitised is not
in digital form
within a reasonable time at an ordinary
commercial price.
7.186 This recommendation would operate as a statutory exception to the
proposed right of communication to the public.
7.187 The Federal Government has stated its intention to legislate that,
where a library or archives gives its users access to copyright
materials in its
holdings that have been published in digital form, this access must be provided
on computer terminals from which
it is impossible for users to copy, download or
print the copyright material being displayed. The consequence of this proposal
that the use of such digital copyright materials held by libraries and
archives will be physically confined to browsing. To the extent
that libraries
and archives are permitted to make such materials available without reference to
fair dealing principles, the Government’s
proposal is broader than that
recommended by the Committee.
7.188 The Committee notes that the use of a digital copy of copyright
material will be subject to the principles of fair dealing to
which use of a
hard copy of copyright material is subject. A user will not be permitted to
print or download a digital copy of a
work, just as they cannot photocopy from
the hard copy of a work, unless the reproduction constitutes a fair dealing.
This applies
to copying of the whole or substantial parts of copyright material.
The Committee considers that notices similar to those already
required to be
displayed close to photocopy machines in libraries and archives should be
required to be incorporated in the display
of database material. These notices
and their role in limiting the liability of libraries and archives with respect
to the authorisation
of infringement of copyright are discussed in the following
paragraphs.
copies made on machines installed in libraries and archives
7.189 Section 39A of the Act provides that, if an infringing copy is made on
a reprographic reproduction machine installed in a library
or archives, the
library or archives will not be taken to have authorised the making of the
infringing copy by reason only that the
copy was made on the machine if a
prescribed notice warning of the limits of the fair dealing exceptions is
displayed on or near
that machine. The display of this notice provides, to
libraries and archives that permit the use of reprographic reproduction
a limited form of immunity from liability for the authorisation of
Computer Software Protection
report the Committee
recommended that s. 39A of the Act be extended to apply where a work stored
on a library or archives database
is displayed via a computer terminal made
available to users. The report specified that, with respect to computer
terminals, the
notice would be required to be incorporated with the display of
the work; that is, the notice should appear within the screen of
the computer
The display of this notice
would provide the same form of immunity to libraries and archives in relation to
acts done on a computer
terminal as is currently provided for acts done on a
reprographic reproduction machine.
7.191 The Committee notes that, in addition to works, other copyright
material such as sound recordings and cinematograph films may
also be stored in
digital form. It is the Committee’s view that, where appropriate, works
and subject matter other than works
should be treated consistently in the Act.
The revised s. 39A notice should be technology neutral.
Recommendation
7.192 The Committee recommends that s. 39A of the Act be extended to
apply where any copyright material in the collection of a library
or an archives
is accessible via a computer terminal made available to users.
7.193 The recommendation in the
Computer Software Protection
proposed that the notice required to be incorporated within the display of the
work advise users of the computer terminal
of the copyright obligations incurred
by printing out the work. It is recognised that the printing out of a work may
not constitute
the only way in which the copyright in the work might be
infringed. In particular, the downloading of database material to computer
memory or onto a carrier such as a floppy disk and the transmission of the
material to another computer are other ways in which a
copy of a work or subject
matter other than a work may be obtained. To provide immunity from acts other
than copying, the notice
should refer to anything done by the user that cannot
be characterised as a fair dealing, as well as any other exception permitted
the Act to the exclusive rights of copyright owners.
Recommendation
7.194 The Committee recommends that the prescribed notice under s. 39A
advise the user of the copyright owner’s rights and the
possible legal
consequences arising from any unauthorised exercise of the copyright
owner’s rights.
8 Miscellaneous exceptions
This chapter examines 40 miscellaneous exceptions provisions in the
to see if they might be subsumed within the
Committee’s proposed fair dealing provision. The organisation of the
miscellaneous
exceptions provisions in the Act is considered first and
recommendations are made to simplify that organisation.
The chapter also considers substantive problems in respect of the
provisions. Repeal is recommended of only those provisions that
are clearly
capable of falling within the proposed revised fair dealing provision. A number
of the miscellaneous exceptions provisions
raise policy questions that go beyond
the Committee’s reference to simplify the Act. The Committee does not
recommend changes
to those provisions.
Introduction
8.01 In addition to the fair dealing provisions discussed in Chapters 4 and
contains 33 royalty-free exceptions to the exclusive rights
comprising copyright. As with the present fair dealing provisions, specificity
is the main feature of the miscellaneous exceptions provisions—each of
them allows for a specific act to be done in relation
to a specific subject
matter of copyright.
As already seen, the current fair dealing provisions
apply to all copyright subject matter and all acts of copyright but are confined
to permitting the doing of these acts for four specific purposes. Both sets of
provisions create exceptions to copyright and as such
are justified by the same
policy rationale: the need to allow, in certain circumstances, free use of
8.02 The emphasis of the miscellaneous exceptions provisions on specific acts
and works is reflected in the organisation of the exceptions
in the Act. Part
III of the Act deals with ‘Copyright in original literary, dramatic,
musical and artistic works’. Division
2 of Part III, ‘Infringement
of copyright in works’, contains provisions that specify what constitutes
an infringement
of a copyright work. Divisions 3, 4, 6 and 7 of Part III contain
the miscellaneous exceptions relating to copyright works. Division
(ss. 40 to 44B) is entitled ‘Acts not constituting infringements of
provisions in respect
of works, as well as four miscellaneous exceptions. Division 4 (ss. 45 to
47A) is entitled ‘Acts not
constituting infringements of copyright in
literary, dramatic and musical works’; it contains exceptions to the
exercise of
reproduction, performance and broadcast rights in respect of all
works except artistic works. Division 6 of Part III (ss. 54 to 64)
a statutory licence in respect of the recording of musical works.
Division 7 (ss. 65 to 73) is entitled ‘Acts not constituting
infringements of copyright in artistic works’; it contains
exceptions to
the exercise of all the exclusive rights of copyright in respect of artistic
8.03 Part IV of the Act deals with ‘Copyright in subject-matter other
than works’ which comprises sound recordings, cinematograph
films, sound
and television broadcasts and published editions of works. Division 6 of Part IV
(ss. 100A to 112B) concerns ‘Infringement
of copyright in
subject-matter other than works’. As well as containing provisions that
specify what constitutes an infringement
of copyright in the copyright subject
matter, Division 6 contains the four fair dealing provisions in respect of the
other subject
matter and 10 miscellaneous exceptions. These exceptions relate to
the exercise of the reproduction, performance and broadcast rights
in respect of
the subject matter referred to. Part VII of the Act deals with Crown copyright
and Crown use of copyright and contains
two relevant exceptions. Finally, Part X
of the Act is entitled ‘Miscellaneous’; it contains four
miscellaneous exceptions
relating to the transmission of broadcasts.
Organisational
problems with the miscellaneous exceptions provisions
8.04 It should be clear from the foregoing that the organisation of both the
fair dealing and the miscellaneous exceptions provisions
is confusing. This is
mainly because the exceptions are located in different Parts of the Act and,
within those Parts, in a number
of different divisions. A further problem is
that there is a lack of consistency in how the exceptions are arranged in
works and subject matter other than works. Thus, Part III of the Act
deals with the exceptions as they apply to works in three separate
while Part IV of the Act deals with the exceptions as well as more general
matters relating to infringement in one division
8.05 One way these organisational problems could be resolved is by locating
all the royalty-free exceptions in one part of the Act.
The key to such a reform
would be to eliminate, or at least minimise, the current distinction in these
exceptions between works and
subject matter other than
In the interests of simplifying
the Act and promoting a technology-neutral approach to it, the Committee adopts
this approach, subject
to the recommendations it makes for the substantive
reform of the particular exceptions provisions.
Recommendation
8.06 The Committee recommends that all miscellaneous exceptions provisions
be located in one part of the Act. It further recommends
that the provisions be
organised within the ‘Miscellaneous exceptions’ part in terms of the
exclusive rights of copyright
to which they relate.
Substantive
problems in respect of the miscellaneous exceptions provisions and the
Committee’s approach to these problems
8.07 More difficult than the organisation of the miscellaneous exceptions
provisions is the question of their substantive justification.
The provisions
cover a wide variety of situations and the question must be asked whether, given
the changes in the use of copyright
materials that have taken place since the
passing of the
in 1968, all the provisions are still relevant.
Simplification of the Act would be well served by a reduction in the number of
miscellaneous
exceptions, particularly since many of the provisions are wordy
and complicated.
8.08 In order to obtain feedback on the operation of the miscellaneous
exceptions provisions in practice, on 14 November 1997 the
Committee sent a
letter to copyright interests, seeking examples of reliance on the provisions
and expressing an interest in learning
whether any industry or business
practices have developed around the exceptions. The Committee received 29
submissions in response
to its letter. Where appropriate these submissions are
referred to in the discussion that follows.
8.09 The Committee’s approach to simplification of the miscellaneous
exceptions provisions has been to determine whether they
are capable of falling
within the revised fair dealing provision. The proposed expansion by the
Committee of the fair dealing concept
to include copyright dealings done for any
purpose allows a wide range of exceptions to copyright to be considered within
dealing context. For the reasons that follow, however, the Committee
recommends the repeal of only those miscellaneous exceptions
provisions that are
clearly capable of falling within its revised statement of fair dealing,
including the non-exclusive set of factors
developed from s. 40(2) of the
Act and under local and overseas case law and provisions of international
conventions such as art.
9(2) of the Berne Convention.
8.10 In approaching its task, the Committee has notionally divided the
miscellaneous exceptions into three categories:
those where in the Committee’s
view the exception is clearly capable of falling within the revised fair dealing
those where there is some
uncertainty whether the activity contained in the relevant provision would be
considered a fair dealing;
those where
the activity is excepted from what would otherwise constitute an infringement of
part of government.
8.11 The Committee considers that the provisions setting out the
exceptions falling within the first category should be repealed.
The Act will be
simplified if the ability to engage in these activities without infringement is
dealt with in the fair dealing context.
8.12 In relation to the exceptions falling under the other two categories,
the Committee proposes the retention in the Act of the
sections relating to
them. Problems that might arise from their operation are referred to but,
despite any shortcomings, the Committee
considers that these provisions must be
retained. In the case of the provisions falling within the second category, the
considers that their retention is warranted in the interests of
promoting certainty in the way the relevant exceptions operate. Simplification
of the provisions falling within the third category raises questions that, in
the Committee’s view, fall outside its terms
of reference.
8.13 Each of the 40 miscellaneous exceptions provisions is considered in this
chapter in the order shown in Table 1.

Reproductions
and other acts done for the purpose of judicial proceedings
8.14 Section 43(1) of the Act states that copyright in a literary, dramatic,
musical or artistic work is not infringed by anything
done for the purpose of a
judicial proceeding or of a report of a judicial proceeding. Section 104(a) is
in terms identical to those
of s. 43(1), except that it applies to subject
matter other than works. ‘Judicial proceeding’ is defined in
as ‘a proceeding before a court, tribunal or person having
by law power to hear, receive and examine evidence on oath’.
8.15 Submissions to the Committee evinced strong reliance on these two
provisions by the judiciary and the legal profession and by
libraries and
educational institutions. The Supreme Court of Western Australia, for example,
The two exceptions are both essential in order to facilitate the work of the
courts. The copying of works the subject of copyright,
or extracts from them,
whether they be extracts from reported or unreported judgments, legal or other
textbooks etc for the purposes
of evidence or submissions, is a well established
need. Acts with respect to subject matter other than works are also necessary
similar reasons including, in particular, copying, showing, playing,
exhibiting or otherwise dealing with the subject matter for
the purposes of
legal proceedings.
The LCA referred to the broader rationale of the two provisions by arguing
that they ‘play an extremely important role in the
administration of
justice and are fully consistent with the public interest’. The Committee
notes that the provisions were
recommended for inclusion in the Act by the
Franki Committee because ‘people are entitled to know the law and they are
to know their legal rights and
obligations’.
8.16 Strong policy considerations clearly underlie ss. 43(1) and 104(a).
The Committee regards review of these considerations as not
falling within its
terms of reference. It does, however, note that there is scope for criticising
the two provisions on the basis
that they are too broad, particularly in so far
as they are used by the private legal profession. As Professor Ricketson pointed
out, sound recordings, films and broadcasts may be copied under s. 104 by
any person or organisation without limitation, provided
they are copied for the
purposes prescribed by the provision.
In respect of s. 43(1), CAL submitted,
In CAL’s view, many users are confused as to what copying is permitted
under [s. 43(1)] and the meaning of the term ‘judicial
proceedings’. Many law firms believe they are entitled to rely on the
exception for copying related to intended or continuing
litigation. Due to the
existence of s. 43(2) [the fair dealing provision related to the giving of
professional advice], CAL’s
view is that this exception applies only to
copying within the court system itself in relation to matters that have already
8.17 One member of the Committee supports the claim that ss. 43(1) and
104 are too broad. The member agrees in principle with the
policy that access to
the legal system is in the public interest, but they do not believe that
subsidise that access. If the Committee
were of the view that the substantive reform of ss. 43(1) and 104(a) was
within its terms
of reference, this member of the Committee would make the
following recommendations in relation to those sections:
that s. 104(a) be limited to
applying to copying in respect of proceedings that have already
that ss. 43(1) and
104(a) be amended to make it clear that they do not allow the reproduction or
supply of material
electronically.
Recommendation
8.18 A majority of the Committee recommends the retention of ss. 43(1)
and 104(a) of the Act. It further recommends that the two provisions
copy of a computer program
8.19 Section 43A(1) allows the owner of a copy of a computer program to make
a copy of the program as protection against loss, destruction
or damage that
renders the copy unuseable. The back-up copy may not be made from an infringing
copy of the computer program or contrary
to an express prohibition by the owner
of the copyright in the computer program (s. 43A(2)).
8.20 The Committee received numerous submissions providing examples of
reliance on s. 43A. The ACC stated that it often receives inquiries
the section—for example, libraries wanting to lend the back-up copy rather
than the original; libraries wanting to rely
on the section to make back-up
copies of other material such as CDs, audiotapes and videos; owners of computer
programs (individuals,
companies, libraries, educational institutions, and so
on) wanting confirmation that they can make a back-up copy and what are the
conditions under which they can do it; commercial copying organisations wanting
to make back-up copies of clients’ software;
and application of the
exception to works in a software package that are not ‘computer
programs’. Seven educational institutions
and libraries informed the
Committee that they placed strong reliance on s. 43A.
8.21 Three of those seven submissions referred to computer programs’
special vulnerability to damage or destruction. Monash
University, for example,
submitted, ‘The exception offered by this section is essential protection
against accidental deletion
or corruption of the code which can easily occur
through no fault of the user. It should be
preserved.’
8.22 Strong policy considerations underlie s. 43A, a fact reflected in
the considerable public reliance on the section. In the Committee's
reliance warrants the retention of s. 43A. In its
Computer Software
report, the Committee made eight recommendations (in paragraphs
2.17 to 2.22) that effectively proposed the strengthening of s. 43A.
Committee notes that, as part of the Digital Agenda reforms already mentioned,
the Government announced that it has adopted many
of the recommendations in the
Computer Software Protection
report, including those relating to back-up
copying of computer programs.
Recommendation
8.23 The Committee reaffirms its recommendations in the
Software Protection
report concerning s. 43A of the Act.

of the Agvet Code of a State or of the Northern Territory;
or (b) Part 2 of the
Agvet Code of the participating Territories within the meaning of the

of, chemical products was affirmed (where the relevant

be an original compilation) by the English Court of Appeal in
