Specialists)
and the Full Court of

Skybase Nominees Pty Ltd v Fortuity Pty
The Committee understands that
ss. 44B and 112B were introduced in the mid-1990s as part of government
policy on the marketing of
generic agricultural and veterinary chemical products
in Australia. In the Committee’s opinion the exception provided by these
provisions may not be capable of falling within the revised fair dealing
8.26 Having regard to its terms of reference, the Committee considers that it

for purpose of broadcasting, reproduction for purpose of including work in

8.28 Section 47 provides that where the broadcasting by a person of a
literary, dramatic or musical work, or of an adaptation of such
a work, would
not—whether by reason of an assignment or licence or of the operation of a
provision of the Act—constitute
an infringement of the copyright in the

of the work
or adaptation would (apart from this section) constitute such an
infringement, the copyright in the work is not infringed by the
making such a recording or film solely for the purpose of broadcasting or
adaptation of the work (s. 47(1)). Sections
70 and 107 are identical to
s. 47(1) except for the following crucial differences: s. 70 applies
to the making of a copy of a broadcast
film that includes an artistic work and
s. 107 applies to the making of a copy of a sound broadcast that includes a
sound recording.
8.29 These sections allow broadcasters to make ephemeral reproductions or
reproductions that facilitate the broadcasting of a film
or sound recording. The
Spicer Committee justified its recommendation to include the provisions in the
Act as follows:
Reproductions of performances by artists and others are often made by
broadcasters for the purposes of subsequent broadcasting. In
a country such as
Australia, with its different time zones and limited number of co-axial cables,
we think that this practice is
necessary and
8.30 In the Committee’s view, it is uncertain whether the exceptions
provided by ss. 47, 70 and 107 are capable of falling within
the revised
fair dealing provision. The Committee notes that both the Berne Convention and
the Rome Convention specifically recognise
the making of ephemeral reproductions
as exceptions to copyright (arts. 11
(3) and 15 respectively).
Furthermore, the United Kingdom, the United States, Canada, Germany and the
Netherlands all specifically
allow for the making of ephemeral reproductions in
their copyright laws.
8.31 The Committee notes further that the three provisions were the subject
of consideration by the CCG in
Highways to Change
, where it recommended
that the provisions be extended to cover all broadcasting, regardless of whether
it involved wire or wireless
technology. In relation to other transmissions to
the public, the CCG suggested that the necessity of extending the ephemeral
provisions to cover these services should be given further consideration
in the ‘wider review of the Act, proposed by the Minister
Accordingly, the
Committee proposes to consider the possible extension of the ephemeral copying
provisions when it considers other
matters identified by the CCG as requiring
further review (see paragraph 8.73).

of the sections as a result of its consideration of matters

recommends the retention of
ss. 47, 70 and 107 of the Act as stand-alone provisions; it further
recommends that the three provisions

8.33 Section 65 provides that it is not an infringement of copyright in a
sculpture or work of artistic craftsmanship that is situated,
otherwise than
temporarily, in a public place or in premises open to the public, to make a
painting, drawing, engraving or photograph
of it or to include it in a film or
television broadcast. ‘Work of artistic craftsmanship’ is not
defined in the Act
but it is referred to in paragraph (c) of the definition of
‘artistic work’ in s. 10. The effect of this reference is
exclude paintings, drawings, engravings, photographs, buildings and models of
buildings from the operation of s. 65. Application
of the exception only to
sculptures and works of artistic craftsmanship has resulted in considerable
criticism of s. 65, which is
discussed in paragraph 8.36.
8.34 The exception in s. 66, which is identical in terms to s. 65,
applies to buildings and models of buildings. Section 67 provides
that, without
prejudice to ss. 65 and 66, the copyright in an
artistic work is not
infringed by the inclusion of the work in a cinematograph film or in a
television broadcast if its inclusion
in the film or broadcast is incidental to
the principal matters represented in the film or broadcast. The exception
provided for
in s. 67 is in addition to those contained in ss. 65 and
66. Unlike those sections, however, s. 67 applies to all artistic works
and, unlike s. 65, it is not limited in its operation to works in public
places or premises open to the public.
8.35 Section 68 states that the copyright in an artistic work is not
infringed by the publication of a painting, drawing, engraving,
photograph or
cinematograph film if, by virtue of s. 65, 66 or 67, the making of that
painting, drawing, engraving, photograph or
film did not constitute an
infringement of the copyright. Section 69 allows works that ss. 65, 66 and
67 permit to be included in
a television broadcast to be included also in a
television program that is transmitted to subscribers to a diffusion
8.36 A considerable level of community reliance appears to be placed on
ss. 65, 66, 67 and 68. The ACC stated that it often receives
rely on the section—for example, a creator
of public sculptures complained
of people reproducing images of the sculptures on postcards and other tourist
items for sale; a sculptor
designing a sculpture in connection with a major
public event was concerned about the sculpture’s image being reproduced on
postcards, advertising material, and so on; and a museum published pictures of
public art in a book.
8.37 The ACC noted that many of the inquiries it receives about s. 66
are from people who are upset that their house has appeared
in an advertisement.
Some inquiries are from students wanting to paint, draw or photograph buildings.
Others are from people who
want to photograph or film houses and public
buildings for inclusion in publications and in films. The State Library of New
Wales stated that it relies on ss. 65 and 66.
8.38 The ACC also noted that it often receives inquiries about
s. 67—for example, people making films and documentaries who
know if they need to get copyright clearances for artworks that are filmed;
advertising agencies filming in private homes;
and galleries wanting to know if
people can film in the gallery. The ACC noted that it also often receives
inquiries about s. 68
and that these arise in conjunction with queries
8.39 The Screen Producers’ Association of Australia stated that
s. 67 is much used in the film and television industries to
incidental filming of artistic works during shoots. SBS (the Special
Broadcasting Service) stated that it regularly relies
on ss. 65, 66 and 67
in relation to the filming of sculptures and buildings and incidental filming of
artistic works. It submitted
that the most common manner in which these
provisions would be applied would be when a reporter is conducting an interview
or a ‘stand-up’
in a public place and chooses to stand near a
sculpture, work of art or building as a backdrop.
8.40 In the Committee’s view incidental filming as covered by
s. 67 is clearly capable of falling within the revised fair dealing
8.41 The Committee understands that ss. 65 and 66 were included in the
Act to allow copying of works found in public parks or streets
and other places
where it is not practicable to control
The expectation was, it seems,
that the copying of the works would be occasional and would not constitute an
unfair use of the copyright
in the works. In practice, however, the intended
scope of ss. 65 and 66 has been exceeded by the commercial exploitation of
reproductions
allowed under the sections. As the ACC has pointed out, the
sections provide
nothing to stop commercial exploitation of [a] work and equally nothing which
grant[s] the artist any right to benefit from such exploitation
... The mere
display of, say, a tapestry in a gallery allow[s] a visitor to the gallery to
photograph that work and to reproduce
that photo onto items such as cards,
posters and postcards.
From the ACC’s submission to the Committee, it appears that there is a
considerable amount of commercial exploitation occurring
pursuant to ss. 65
and 66. The extended fair dealing concept is the appropriate context for
resolving the practical injustices to
which the present wording of ss. 65
and 66 gives rise.
8.42 In the Committee’s view it would be consistent with the intention
of the legislation as expressed by the Spicer
to allow the activities
covered by the provisions under consideration to be exempt from copyright
infringement as fair dealings,
if the claim can be made in the circumstances of
a particular case. On this basis, and on the basis that their deletion will
the Act, the Committee recommends as
Recommendation
8.43 The Committee recommends the repeal of ss. 65, 66 and 67 of the
Act and, as a necessary consequence, ss. 68 and 69.
Reproduction
of part of artistic work in later work
8.44 Section 72(1) provides that the copyright in an artistic work is not
infringed by the making of a later artistic work by the
same author if, in
making the later work, the author does not repeat or imitate the main design of
the earlier work. Section 72(2)
states that s. 72(1) has effect
notwithstanding that part of the earlier work is reproduced in the later work
and that, in reproducing
the later work, the author used a mould, cast, sketch,
plan, model or study made for the purposes of the earlier work.
8.45 Section 72 covers the situation where an artist has produced a
particular work, the copyright in which is owned by another person—for
example, where the work has been done pursuant to a contract of employment or a
contract of commission that provides for copyright
ownership by the employer or
commissioner—and the artist wishes to reproduce certain features or
aspects of this work in a
the absence of a special provision to the contrary, the artist would run the
risk of infringing the copyright in the first work
if he or she reproduced a
substantial part of it. The effect of s. 72(1) is that in such a case the
making of a later work will not
infringe the earlier work provided that the
artist does not ‘repeat or imitate the main design of the earlier
8.46 The operation of s. 72 has been criticised by some commentators as
being uncertain. Professor Ricketson, for instance, states
that the meaning of
the expression ‘main design’ is unclear and is not made any clearer
by s. 72(2). In his view s. 72
must mean that an artist is free to use
more than the ‘ideas’ of their earlier work or more than an
insubstantial part
thereof; otherwise the provision would be redundant.
Therefore ‘part’ in s. 72(2) must mean a substantial part; on
other hand, it must not be so substantial so as to constitute the
‘main design’ of the work, whatever that may
Professor Ricketson believes that
ultimately the section allows an artist to use a substantial part of an earlier
artistic work in
a later work provided that they do not take the whole design of
the earlier work.
8.47 In the Committee’s view it is not clear whether the exception
provided by s. 72 is capable of falling within the revised
fair dealing
exception, particularly in so far as it relates to commercial dealings with
works. The Committee acknowledges that there
is an argument to be made that
s. 72 is redundant. Professor Ricketson, for example, states,
Perhaps the real explanation of the section is that it is an attempt to put
into legal terms a convention normally exercised by artists
in their everyday
work and one that has never caused any particular trouble, being well understood
by all practising artists.
The Committee, however, is limited to making the following recommendation
with respect to s. 72.
Recommendation
8.48 The Committee recommends the retention of s. 72 of the Act.

Section 73(2) provides
that where a building has been constructed in accordance with architectural

later reconstruction of the building by reference to those drawings or
8.50 In the Committee’s view the exception given by s. 73 is

therefore makes the following recommendation.
Recommendation
8.51 The Committee
recommends the repeal of s. 73 of the
or recording of broadcasts for private and domestic use
8.52 Section 111(1) states that the copyright in a television broadcast, in
so far as it consists of visual images, is not infringed
by the making of a
cinematograph film of the broadcast or the making of a copy of such a film for
the private and domestic use of
the person by whom it is made. Section 111(2) is
identical to s. 111(1), except that it applies to the copyright in a sound
or in a television broadcast in so far as it consists of sounds.
8.53 The expression ‘private and domestic’ is not defined in the
Act. Section 111(3) effectively provides, however, that
the phrase does not
(a) selling a copy of the film or sound recording, letting it for hire, or by
way of trade offering or exposing it for sale or hire;
(b) distributing a copy
of the film or sound recording, whether for the purpose of trade or otherwise;
(c) by way of trade exhibiting
a copy of the film or sound recording in public;
(d) broadcasting the film or recording; or (e) causing the film or recording to
be seen or heard in public.
8.54 Section 111 applies only to the making of a recording of a television or
sound broadcast. It does not create an exception in
respect of the copying of
any work, film or sound recording included in such a broadcast. In practice,
therefore, the exception is
limited to the copying of live broadcasts such as a
sports event or concert.
8.55 Despite the ‘private and domestic use’ limitation contained
in this provision, the Committee does not consider that
the exception would come
within its revised fair dealing proposal.
8.56 The Committee notes the submission by ARIA (the Australian Recording
Industry Association), which called for clarification of
s. 111 in the
light of the High Court’s recent decision in
Telstra Corporation v
Australasian Performing Rights Association
This case considered the
meaning of the words ‘to the public’ as they appear in the
definition of the term ‘broadcast’
in s. 10(1) of the Act. A
majority of the court held that ‘public’ means the ‘copyright
owner’s public’.
ARIA submitted that the High Court’s
interpretation of ‘public’ is inconsistent with s. 111(3),
which, in ARIA’s
view, suggests that the words ‘private and domestic
use’ are to be understood in opposition to commercial or public use.
further submitted,
Section 111 needs to be clarified to ensure adequate copyright protection of
sound recordings in an increasingly converged communications
environment ...
Callers on hold are accessing material which has been broadcast to or
prepared in a recorded format for the point of transmission
of that material.
Internet users are in a comparable situation. With the advent of converged
communications services, in particular
Web TV and platforms which combine
broadcasting and interactivity, material can be accessed in private according to
subsection 111(3)
but in public, in the sense of being available to members of
the public should they choose to seek access, as found in
8.57 As discussed, on 30 April 1998 the Government announced its intention to
effect a number of copyright reforms, the most significant
introduction of a new broadly based, technology-neutral right of communication
to the public to replace the broadcast and
other transmission rights. The
Committee considers that the operation of s. 111 needs to be further
examined in the light of the
Government’s decision: it proposes to examine
the operation of this provision when it considers the simplification of the
rights of copyright owners.
Recommendation
8.58 Subject to any recommendations it makes when it examines s. 111
of the Act as part of its consideration of the simplification
of the exclusive
rights of copyright owners, the Committee recommends the retention of
s. 111 as a stand-alone provision.
Reproductions
of editions of works
8.59 Paragraph (a) of s. 112 provides that the copyright in a published
edition of a work is not infringed by the making of a reproduction
of the whole
or a part of that edition if that reproduction is made in the course of a
dealing that, by virtue of the fair dealing
defences in ss. 40 to 44, does
not infringe the copyright in that work, or the making of a copy (including a
handicapped reader’s
copy or an intellectually handicapped person’s
copy) of the whole or a part of that work, the making of which does not infringe
(ss. 49, 50 and 51A), copying by educational institutions
to 135ZT) and the copying of statutory instruments and judgments (s.182A).
Paragraph (b) of s. 112 extends the same exception
to a published edition
containing more than one work, in which case the relevant dealing or
reproduction may be with some or all
of the works.
8.60 The submissions the Committee received in respect of s. 112 showed
a low level of reliance on and awareness of the
The Committee infers that this
was because of the location of the provision outside the schemes of exceptions
to which it relates.
The provision is required because these other exceptions as
they are currently drafted do not apply to published editions.
Committee notes also that, in its view, it is not certain that s. 112 in

therefore makes the following recommendation and notes that, should the
Committee’s recommendations
on the other schemes of exceptions be
accepted, it will be necessary to make a number of changes to s. 112, such
as deletion of the
references to the fair dealing and library copying
exceptions, which the Committee has recommended should apply to all copyright
material, including published editions.
Recommendation
8.61 The Committee recommends the retention of s. 112 of the Act as a
stand-alone provision.
Reproduction
of statutory instruments and judgments
8.62 Section 182A of the Act states that the copyright, including any
prerogative right or privilege of the Crown in the nature of
prescribed work is not infringed by the making, by reprographic reproduction, of
one copy of the whole or of a part
of that work by or on behalf of a person and
for a particular purpose (s. 182A(1)). For the purposes of s. 182A, a
‘reprographic
reproduction’ is a facsimile copy of the whole or part
of the work in any size or form (s. 10(3)(g)).
8.63 The foregoing provision does not apply to the making, by reprographic
reproduction, of a copy of the whole or part of the work
where a charge is made
for making and supplying that copy, unless the amount of the charge does not
exceed the cost of making and
supplying that copy (s. 182A(2)).
8.64 Section 182A(3) defines ‘prescribed work’ thus:
a) an Act or State Act, an enactment of the legislature of a Territory or an
instrument (including an Ordinance or a rule, regulation
or by-law) made under
an Act, a State Act or such an enactment;
b) a judgment, order or award of a Federal court or of a court of a State or
c) a judgment, order or award of a Tribunal (not being a court) established
by or under an Act or other enactment of the Commonwealth,
a State or a
d) reasons for a decision of a court referred to in paragraph (b), or of a
Tribunal referred to in paragraph (c), given by the court
or by the Tribunal;
e) reasons given by a Justice, Judge or other member of a court referred to
in paragraph (b), or of a member of a Tribunal referred
to in paragraph (c), for
a decision given by him or her either as the sole member, or as one of the
members of the court or Tribunal.
8.65 By operation of s. 112, a published edition of any such work may be
copied where the copying of that work is not an infringement
of copyright under
8.66 The Committee received no representations relating to the content of
s. 182A. It notes, however, that Professor Ricketson criticised
drafting of the section as being confusing and unreasonably restricted in
8.67 The Committee considers that the copying permitted by s. 182A is
unlikely to be considered a fair dealing. Any change in the
policy of the
section lies outside the Committee’s terms of reference. Accordingly, it
is confined to making the following
recommendation with respect to s. 112,
but it does invite consideration of Professor Ricketson’s criticisms of
the drafting
of the section.
Recommendation
8.68 The Committee recommends that s. 182A of the Act be retained and
be amended to include published editions of prescribed works;
recommends that s. 112 be amended accordingly.

Performance
of a work at premises where persons reside or sleep, causing sound recording to
be heard at a guest house or club
8.75 Section 46 of the Act states that where a literary, dramatic or musical
work, or an adaptation of such a work, is performed in
public, by the operation
of wireless telegraphy apparatus or by the use of a record, at premises where
people reside or sleep, as
part of the amenities provided exclusively for
residents or inmates of the premises or for those residents or inmates and their
the performance does not constitute an infringement of the copyright in
8.76 In so far as it relates to premises where people reside or sleep,
s. 106 is identical to s. 46, except that it applies to sound
recordings. Section 106 effectively provides for two exceptions. Section 106(1)
states that where a sound recording is caused to
be heard in public at premises
where people reside or sleep, as part of the amenities provided exclusively for
residents or inmates
of the premises or their guests, this does not constitute
an infringement of copyright in the recording (s. 106(1)(a)). The exception
does not apply if a specific charge is made for admission to the part of the
premises where the recording is to be heard (s. 106(2)(a)).
‘specific charge’ may be made partly for admission and partly for
other purposes (s. 106(3)).
8.77 Section 106(1) also provides that where a sound recording is caused to
be heard in public as part of the activities of, or for
the benefit of, a club,
society or other organisation that is not established or conducted for profit
and the principal objects of
which are charitable or are otherwise concerned for
the advancement of religion, education or social welfare, the act of causing
record to be heard does not constitute an infringement of copyright in the
recording (s. 106(1)(b)). This exception does not
apply if a charge is made
for admission to the place where the recording is to be heard and any of the
proceeds are applied otherwise
than for the purposes of the organisation
(s. 106(2)(b)). The ‘charge’ may be made partly for admission
and partly for
other purposes ((s.106 (3)).
8.78 The ACC stated that it receives a small number of inquiries about
ss. 46 and 106. In respect of ss. 46 and 106(1)(a) it stated
inquiries have come from retirement villages, nursing homes, holiday camps and
resorts, boarding schools and prisons. In respect
of s. 106(1)(b) it stated
that inquiries have come from churches, schools and sporting clubs.
8.79 ARIA made a strong submission for the amendment of s. 106. It
argued as follows:
There is a growing incidence of resort-style enterprises which provide a
variety of entertainments including accommodation, sporting
activities as well
as areas where music is played, in particular night clubs. By amalgamating the
variety of amenities provided and
failing to charge a specific fee for entry to
the night club area, these clubs are able to claim a section 106 exemption.
These enterprises
are highly commercial and the playing of sound recordings
incontrovertibly adds to their appeal and the revenue of such enterprises.
nature of their business is no different from other businesses which derive
commercial gain from playing sound recordings in
public and recognise copyright
interests accordingly. Only the structure of the business is
ARIA suggested that s. 106 should perhaps be limited to those premises
that use sound recordings as background music
8.80 The Committee agrees with the fundamental proposition underlying
ARIA’s submission that in certain circumstances the operation
ss. 46 and 106 may be unfair. It notes that the Spicer Committee justified
its recommendation for inclusion of s. 46 in the Act
as follows:
In our opinion, a performance in a guest house or hotel to residents or their
guests should not be treated as a public performance
where there is a similarity
between the type of performance given in such an establishment and the
performance that a person might
receive in his own
In the Committee’s view, in the light of technological developments and
the High Court’s decision in the
Telstra v APRA
case, this is an
outdated characterisation of the public performance right in such circumstances.
The Committee sees merit in a re-examination
of ss. 46 and 106.
notes that on 1 June 1998 the House of Representatives Standing Committee on
Legal and Constitutional Affairs released a report
on the collection of
businesses. Although the report examined
the nature and scope of the public
performance right, it did not consider the exceptions provided by ss. 46
and 106 in any detail.
The Committee therefore proposes to consider the
operation of the two provisions when it looks at the outstanding matters
by the CCG.

and 106 of the Act as a result of its consideration of outstanding
identified by the CCG, the Committee recommends the retention of those sections

in sound recordings first published in Australia not infringed by causing the
recording to be heard in public or broadcast
8.87 Section 105 states that copyright subsisting in a sound recording by
virtue only of s. 89(3) is not infringed by the causing
of the recording to
be heard in public or by the broadcasting of the recording. Section 89(3) allows
sound recording without the need to show
that the maker of the sound recording was a qualified person or that the sound
was made in Australia. The only connecting factor that must to be
satisfied is that the sound recording was first published in Australia.
89(3) and 105 apply, in other words, to published sound recordings that have no
connection with Australia other than being
first published here.
8.88 Section 89(3) operates in conjunction with the Copyright (International
Protection) Regulations, which are made pursuant to s.
(‘Application of Act to countries other than Australia’). The
Regulations extend copyright protection to works and
other subject matter that
have a connection with a country that is a party to the relevant international
convention to which Australia
is also a party. The effect of the Regulations
together with ss. 89(3) and 105 is that the owners of copyright in
published sound
recordings that were made or first published in some countries
that are not signatories to the Rome Convention, or were made by the
or residents of such countries, do not have the exclusive right to perform or
broadcast the recording. The Committee understands
that denial of broadcasting
and public performance rights to the class of sound recordings specified in
s. 105 is consistent with
international obligations and has been on the
understanding that the countries with which the sound recordings have the
closest connection
do not give such rights to owners of copyright in sound
recordings.
8.89 ARIA submitted the following argument for the repeal of s. 105:
No rational basis is apparent to explain the distinction. Logically, where
it should exist in
respect of all rights. With the proposed expansion of the definition of
broadcast to include both wireless and
wired transmissions, this exception would
seem to inexplicably and unjustifiably prejudice the interests of copyright
owners of sound
recordings.
8.90 The Committee is of the view that this exception is not capable of
falling within the revised fair dealing provision. It does
not, however,
consider that it should recommend the repeal of s. 105, which would
constitute a major change in the policy underlying
this and other provisions in
the Act on which the Committee has received insufficient comment.
Recommendation
8.91 The Committee recommends the retention of s. 105 of the Act as a
stand-alone provision.
performance of old news film
8.92 Section 110(1) provides that where the visual images forming part of a

that, when they were

not an infringement of copyright

8.96 This provision appears to exist because copyright in a cinematograph

works. Section 33(2)
provides that copyright in all works except photographs subsists until the
expiration of 50 years after the
expiration of the calendar year in which the
author of the work died. Section 94(2) states that copyright in a cinematograph
subsists until the expiration of 50 years after the expiration of the
calendar year in which the film was first published. In the
Committee’s
view, the activity referred to in s. 110(2) would constitute a fair dealing
with the works used in the film.

Importation
of books and published editions
8.101 Section 44A allows the importation of books for the purposes of sale,
distribution and exhibition in three circumstances. The
first of these is
covered by s. 44A(1), which provides that the copyright in an overseas work
first published after 23 December 1991
is not infringed by a person who, without
the licence of the owner of the copyright, imports a non-infringing book into
for a purpose mentioned in s. 37. An ‘overseas work’
is ‘one which was first published in a country other than
Australia and
was not published in Australia within 30 days of its first publication in that
other country’ (s. 44A(9)). A
‘non-infringing book’ is a
book made (otherwise than under a compulsory licence) in a country specified in
the Copyright
Regulations, being a book whose making did not constitute an
infringement of any copyright subsisting in a work, or in a published
a work, under a law of that country. The effect of s. 37 is to prohibit the
unauthorised importation of the book for the
purpose of sale, distribution or
exhibition.
8.102 Section 44A(2) provides that the copyright in an overseas work
published before 23 December 1991 or a book first published in
whether before or after 23 December 1991, is not infringed by a person who,
without the licence of the owner of the copyright
imports a copy (the
‘imported copy’) of a hardback or paperback version of the book for
a purpose mentioned in s. 37
if the following conditions are satisfied. The
person must have ordered in writing from the copyright owner, or the
licensee or agent, one or more copies of that version of the book
(not being second-hand copies or more copies than were needed to
satisfy the
person’s reasonable requirements)(s. 44A(2)(c)). Furthermore, at
least seven days must have elapsed since the person
placed the order and the
the order would be filled
within 90 days after it was placed, or at least 90
days must have elapsed since the person placed the order and the copyright
licensee or agent had not filled the order (s. 44A(d)). Section
44A(2) does not apply to the importation of a copy of a hardback
version of a
non-infringing book into Australia if the copyright owner, or their licensee or
agent, is able to supply in Australia
enough copies of a paperback version of
the book to fill any reasonable order (s. 44A (7)).
8.103 Section 44A(3) states that the copyright in a published work (whether
first published before, on or after the commencing day)
is not infringed by a
person who, without the licence of the owner of the copyright, imports a single
copy of a non-infringing book
into Australia if the importation is for the
purpose of filling a written order, or a verifiable telephone order, by a
customer of
the person and by the order the customer makes a statement
(verifiable in the case of a telephone order) to the effect that they
intend to use the book for a purpose mentioned in s. 37. Section 44A(4)
extends this exception to libraries, for whom two
or more copies of a
non-infringing book may be ordered.
8.104 Section 112A is identical to s. 44A, except that it applies to
published editions.
8.105 In the Committee’s view, the importation of books in the three
circumstances described in ss. 44A and 112A is not capable
of falling with
the revised fair dealing provision. The Committee notes the submission from the
APA, which argued strongly that ss.

Act because they have had a positive effect on the book publishing industry,
especially on
its supply and printing practices. The Committee makes no comment
on the policy underlying ss. 44A and 112A but, as a matter of presentation
and for simplifying the Act, it makes the following recommendation.

STATUTORY LICENCES
9 Copying for educational purposes
This chapter briefly examines the background to the educational copying
provisions in the
. In the absence of a case being made
for a change in the general approach taken in the Act, the Committee does not
propose any substantial
change. Instead, it makes recommendations aimed at
simplifying the provisions.
The chapter examines the structure of the provisions, individual provisions
relating to educational copying, and Parts VA and VB of
the Act. The Committee
considers that the statutory licences should, as far as practicable, be
technology neutral; this approach
is reflected in the recommendations.
In relation to the Part VB licence, the Committee notes the
Government’s recent decision to extend the licence to cover some
digital form. It recommends that a new statutory licence be created whereby
educational institutions may make available
subject to payment of a royalty.
Introduction
9.01 Article 9(2) of the Berne Convention and art. 13 of the TRIPS
Agreement (see Appendix B) permit a member country to provide limited
to the exclusive rights of copyright owners. Any such exceptions must be a
special case, must not conflict with the normal
exploitation of the work, and
must not unreasonably prejudice the legitimate interests of rights owners.
Article 10(2) of the Berne
Convention permits the making of special provisions
for teaching.
9.02 In reliance on this express international authority, a number of
provisions have been included in the Act to deal with uses for
educational
purposes. The Committee summarised the effect of some of these provisions in its
issues paper entitled
Educational Institutions and Copying under the
. It received submissions in response to the paper from a
range of interests. In general, the comments from users reflected a desire
existing exceptions
to what would otherwise constitute infringement to be
extended or subjected to fewer constraints. Comments from owners tended to
the reverse approach—the right to copy, particularly without
remuneration or without a licence, should be limited. Neither
users nor owners,
however, proposed a radical recasting of the provisions of the Act that
currently relate to educational copying.
In particular, the majority of
submissions from both users and owners recommended retention of the present
statutory licensing schemes
set out in Parts VA and VB of the Act.
9.03 The Committee had invited comment on adoption of the scheme for
educational copying found in ss. 35 and 36 of the UK
and Patents Act 1988
, under which a free compulsory licence permits the
copying of a limited amount of copyright material in the absence of an agreed
voluntary licence. The principle underlying the scheme is to encourage the
parties to reach agreement on permissible copying. For
various reasons the
parties affected in Australia do not consider this a satisfactory way to deal
with educational copying. The Committee
considers that the position in Australia
is now well established and well known to all parties and no case has been made
for a change
in the general approach. The following discussion is set against
this conclusion. It is divided into three sections: the structure
of the Act;
individual provisions relating to copying by educational institutions; and the
content of the statutory licences.
structure of the Act
9.04 The Committee considers that all provisions relating to educational
copying should be included in one part of the Act. This view
was universally
supported in submissions.
9.05 The Committee also invited comment on whether it would be possible to
move some or all of the provisions relating to educational
copying to the
regulations. Submitters were generally opposed to this approach. The principal
argument against such action was that
it would mean that a person wishing to
find the law on the topic would have to look in two places. There was also a
feeling that
the greater ease with which regulations can be altered might result
in interest groups being able to secure, without adequate consultation,
alteration to the statutory schemes more easily than if the change had to be
passed through the Parliament.
9.06 The Committee does not consider the second reason to be valid: the very
point of regulations is to enable detailed provisions
of a technical nature to
be more readily updated. Some of the material that is contained in the statutory
licensing schemes is of
a kind that is appropriate for inclusion in regulations
rather than in an Act. It deals with the mechanical details of the licensing
scheme rather than the general question of whether such a scheme should apply to
educational copying practices. The Committee acknowledges,
however, that there
is a high level of sensitivity in this matter, even in relation to the details
of the licensing scheme itself.
Apart from the sections noted in Table 2,
it does not recommend the transfer of the provisions relating to the statutory
from the Act to the regulations.
9.07 The Committee does, however, consider that it would be possible to
reduce the number of provisions relating to the statutory
licences since there
is considerable duplication in the provisions relating to copying of broadcasts
in Part VA and copying of works
in Part VB. A study of the Act reveals that the
sections in Table 2 effectively duplicate one another and could be combined
less cumbersome form.
2 Sections to be combined
Sections under Part VA
Sections under Part VB
9.08 In addition, both Parts include common provisions relating to collecting
societies (see ss. 135P to 135S and 135ZZB to 135ZZE).
These provisions
could be combined. It would also be appropriate for them to be included in the
regulations since they relate to
a subject matter removed from educational
copying and are concerned with detail that it is not necessary to include in the
Recommendation
9.09 The Committee recommends that the Act be recast to
group together all provisions
relating to educational
institutions;
reduce the duplication in
Parts VA and VB by combining the provisions referred to in Table 2 with
appropriate modifications and including
them in a separate part or division of
transfer material relating to
collecting agencies to regulations.
of ‘educational institution’
9.10 The definition of ‘educational institution’ is
extraordinarily detailed. It is set out in s. 10(1) and further expanded
s. 10A and the proposed s. 153L in Schedule 10 of the
Amendment Bill 1997
. Overall, the definition occupies nearly four pages of the
Act. The definition works on the basis of specifying certain types of
institutions as ‘educational institutions’ and then allowing others
to bring themselves within the description by a process
of self-declaration,
thereby enabling them to take advantage of the special provisions in the Act
that relate to educational copying.
The self-declaration will, under the
, be able to be challenged by collecting societies by
application to the Copyright Tribunal (see proposed s. 153L).
9.11 The Committee considers that this attempt to produce an all-inclusive
definition of ‘educational institution’ has
resulted in unnecessary
complexity. It proposes that the effect of the definition can be achieved by an
alternative approach that
would allow the definition to be omitted from the
9.12 The provisions relating to educational institutions either except
copying by such institutions from what would otherwise constitute
infringement of copyright or permit the institution to take advantage of the
statutory licences. To omit the definition of ‘educational
institution’ from the Act would be to follow the approach taken in
relation to libraries. The Act does not define what is a
library but leaves it
to be given its ordinary meaning. If a body purports to use the library copying
provisions and a copyright
owner considers that the body is not a
‘library’, an infringement action can be brought and it will be
necessary for
the body sued to establish that it is indeed a
9.13 Adopting a similar approach, if there were no definition of
‘educational institution’ in the Act a copyright owner
challenge action being taken by a body using the educational copying provisions.
It would be up to the body concerned to demonstrate
that the exception or
licence provision applied to it because it was an ‘educational
institution’. This is in substance
a form of self-declaration similar to
that proposed to be included in the Act by the 1997 amendments. Instead of the
declaration
being done by
notice, it will be done through the
action of the body in seeking to rely on the exceptions in the Act. Indeed, the
giving of either
a records notice or a sampling notice under s. 135E
(copying of broadcasts) or under s. 135ZL (copying of works) would
conveniently
give the relevant collecting society notice of the self-declaration
of the body giving the notice and enable the society to consider
challenging the
status of the body at an early stage. This allows bodies that consider they are
engaging in activities that should
come within the exceptions in the Act to make
their case. It would remove from the Act cumbersome provisions that endeavour to
out in detail a concept that will probably change over time and require
updating and adding detail to the Act.
9.14 The Committee considers that, rather than attempting the task of trying
to identify the bodies that ought to be able to take
advantage of the
educational copying provisions, the Act should in effect transfer the onus of
establishing its credentials to the
body that is endeavoring to make use of
those provisions.
Recommendation
9.15 The Committee recommends that the Act be amended by omitting the
definition of ‘educational institution’ from s.
repealing s. 10A.
9.16 It may be thought that such an approach could lead to ambiguity about
whether centralised copying centres within educational
institutions or external
centres that undertake copying for such institutions would be able to use the
exceptions in the Act. But
the provisions of the Act that allow copying by
educational institutions in circumstances where that copying might be carried
by such a centre refer to copying by or
on behalf of
an educational
institution. It seems to the Committee that in these circumstances there is no
need to make special provision for
copying centres.
9.17 The Committee received a number of submissions referring to the
amendment to the definition of ‘educational institution’
made by the
so as to be able to rely on the reference to
institutions having to be ‘not for profit’ to attract the exceptions
the licensing provisions of the Act. It was said that profit-making
institutions should pay a higher level of remuneration for copying.
Committee considers that this is an appropriate matter for consideration by the
by regulations under
ss. 153A(3)(b) and 153C(3) as a matter that the Tribunal is required to
take into account.
9.18 Section 28 provides that the performance of works, sound recordings
and films in the course of educational instruction is not
to be regarded as a
public performance. The section is, however, limited by restricting the audience
allowed to be present at such
performances to teachers, students or other people
directly connected with the place where the instruction is given. A parent of
student is expressly excluded from the permissible audience. Educational
interests pressed for extension of the provision to permit
the attendance of
parents in the audience. Such an extension would bring school concerts and
performance recitals within the scope
of the section, thus eliminating the need
to obtain the permission of the copyright owner or a licence for the
performance. The Committee
is of the view that it would be inappropriate to
extend the operation of the section as suggested: when a work is being performed
for an audience that includes parents, this should be regarded as part of the
performance should be
treated as a public performance.
Recommendation
9.19 The Committee recommends that no change be made to the scope of
s. 28 of the Act.
40(1A) and 40(1B)
9.20 Sections 40(1A) and 40(1B)make special provision allowing a fair dealing
copy with a literary work for external students of an
educational institution.
This is discussed in Chapter 6.
9.21 Section 44 allows short extracts from works to be included in
collections to be used by a place of education. The right is heavily
circumscribed. It was included in the Act before the inclusion of the statutory
licence provisions to be found in Part VB. The Committee’s
revealed that, because of the limited circumstances in which it can be relied
upon, the section is seldom, if ever, used.
9.22 The fact that the section is seldom used would be sufficient basis to
justify a recommendation that it be repealed.The Committee
takes into account,
however, the fact that institutions are able to make copies of works for
inclusion in materials to be distributed
to students under s. 135ZG. The
copying permitted by s. 44 largely falls within the scope of s. 135ZG.
The Committee considers that
this approach to copying by educational
institutions is more appropriate than that contemplated by s. 44.
Recommendation
9.23 The Committee recommends that s. 44 of the Act be repealed.
9.24 Section 200 permits the use of works and broadcasts for educational
purposes in certain limited circumstances. The ACC suggested
that the section
could be repealed. It pointed out that the section predated the inclusion in the
Act of the licensing schemes in
Parts VA and VB and that the activities covered
by s. 200 should be seen to fall within those schemes.
9.25 Section 200(1)(a) is concerned with the reproduction of copyright
material in the course of educational instruction ‘otherwise
than by the
use of an appliance adapted for the production of multiple copies or an
appliance capable of producing a copy or copies
by a process of reprographic
reproduction’. Most obviously, it permits the reproduction of copyright
material on a blackboard
and the making by hand and use of an overhead
projection of a work. The ACC expressed concern that ‘the section could be
interpreted
as allowing more than one copy to be made, and that its application
to activities such as reproducing on a computer disk is not clear’.
Committee does not see that on its face the section can lead to the result
referred to by the ACC. In the absence of evidence
of the section being so
employed, the Committee does not think it appropriate to recommend any change to
a section that is obviously
intended to allow limited but useful copying for
teaching purposes.
9.26 Section 200(1)(b) permits educational institutions to reproduce works as
part of the questions to be answered in an examination
or in an answer to an
examination question. The view is put by copyright owner representatives that
reproduction of works in these
circumstances should be regarded as falling
within the statutory licence provided by Part VB. Educational institutions
by suggesting that to include copying for examination questions as
part of the statutory licence could have the effect of risking
confidentiality of the examination.
9.27 The Committee considers that educational interests can properly be
concerned that the revelation of what material has been copied
for examination
purposes in the records that are kept as part of the statutory licensing scheme
could jeopardise the confidentiality
of the examination process. It is also the
case that in some circumstances examination papers are not made public after an
examination
is completed.
9.28 Representations to the Committee from owners did not provide evidence of
widespread copying of works in the context of examinations.
It did not appear to
the Committee that copying under s. 200(1)(b) had a significant impact on
9.29 The ACC suggested that the copying of sound broadcasts, which is
permitted by ss. 200(2) and 200(2A), is covered by the statutory
included in Part VA. The Committee notes that the provision was retained when
the statutory scheme was enacted, s. 200(2A)
being added to permit a record
to be made of a sound broadcast without remuneration.
9.30 Educational institutions said that they relied on these provisions for
making records of broadcasts. The Committee considers
it inappropriate to repeal
ss. 200(2) and 200(2A). It notes, however, that s. 200(2A) is of
limited effect since it relates only
to making a record of a sound broadcast and
does not extend to works or sound recordings included in the broadcast.
Recommendation
9.31 The Committee recommends that no change be made to s. 200 of the
9.32 There was general agreement in the submissions that the statutory

The Committee
