Representations
to the Committee
9.38 The consensus among interests seen in relation to the Part VA licence
does not extend to the licence to copy works contained
in Part VB. While still
asserting the principle that the copying licence should not be affected by
differences in technology, the
parties disagreed about the implications of this
for the licensing scheme contained in that Part. Owners would confine the
to print materials. Works in electronic form should be viewed
differently. Copying of these should require express permission and
a negotiated
payment. Conversion of works to digital format and electronic distribution of
works should not be permitted under the
9.39 As might be expected, educators wish to be able to treat copyright
material in digital form in the same way as they do print
works and also to
exploit electronic distribution of works whether they have been digitised from
print or are originally in digital
form. They called on the Committee to
recommend the expansion of Part VB to permit them to do this.
Government’s proposals
9.40 The Committee’s consideration of these matters was overtaken by
the Government’s announcement that the Part VB licence
is to be extended
to cover some digital copying of works. The Committee understands that it has
been decided that educational institutions
should be able to make and network
digital copies of works that have been published in print form on terms similar
to the right given
to copy print works. The detail of the extension of the
educational copying licence was not announced at the time of the statement
9.41 The Committee had reached a point in its deliberations where it had
concluded that the statutory licence permitting educational
institutions to make
multiple photocopies of works in print form should be extended to include all
works in digital form, no matter
what their original published format might have
been. The original version of the Part VB licence was adopted in 1980 to enable
educational
institutions to take advantage of the then new technology of
photocopying. It recognised the principle that educational institutions
not be prevented from using developments in technology to update teaching
methods. The Part VB scheme also recognised, however,
that copying by
educational institutions should require payment to copyright owners. The
Committee is of the view that the same principle
applies to the use of works in
digital form by educational institutions: the institutions should be permitted
to make certain use
of this technology without having to seek the permission of
the copyright owner but in return should be obliged to pay an appropriate
9.42 To the extent that the Government’s decision is limited to digital
copying of print material, the Committee considers that
it does not go far
enough. The Committee can see no reason for limiting the digital use of material
to works in print form. The principle
on which the Part VB scheme is based seems
not to require that such a constraint be adopted.
Recommendation
9.43 The Committee recommends that educational institutions be permitted to
use all copyright material in digital form pursuant to
a statutory licence
requiring the payment of a royalty.
9.44 One member of the Committee disagrees in principle with the copying of
works digitally (including the transfer from print to
digital) without prior
permission and negotiated payment. That member understands, however, that the
practice is widespread and accordingly
supports the Committee’s
recommendation that this copying be permitted subject to the payment of a
9.45 The Committee’s consideration of this recommendation’s
implementation led it to conclude that such an approach could
not be adopted by
a simple extension of Part VB of the Act for two reasons. First, the Part VB
licence is based on copying of pages
or identifiable parts of works; as
discussed in relation to fair dealing (see Chapter 6), this approach is not
compatible with work
in a digital form. Second, the licence provided for by Part
VB permits the multiple copying of copyright material. The Committee
understands
that the principal means of distribution by educational institutions of teaching
materials in digital form will not be
through the institution making multiple
copies; rather, the institution will make a digital reproduction of a work
available to students.
The students will then access the work and choose whether
to make their own electronic copy or print out the work in hard-copy form.
institution will thus not be the copier of the work. It will make the work
available for students to deal with in such manner
as they wish. In the
Committee’s view this is a different concept from the licence provided for
by Part VB and it is not possible
to extend the statutory provisions relating to
that licence to embrace the copying of works in digital form. The question will
to be dealt with by a separate licence that recognises the nature of the
dealing with digital copyright materials. That licence will
educational institution to make a copyright work available in digital form for
copying by students. The limits on such
copying will need to be spelt out in the
Act in same way as the limits on multiple copying of print works are spelt out
in Part VB.
9.46 As noted in paragraph 9.40, the Committee understands that the matters
alluded to in paragraph 9.45 have been recognised by the
Government in its
proposal to extend the educational copying licence. The question of making
available works in digital form is one
that arises where a work is converted to
digital format from print as well as where the work is originally in digital
form. It will
thus have to be dealt with to give effect to the
Government’s decision. The Committee does not consider that it is
appropriate
for it to attempt to set out in this report the detail of a
compulsory licence for digital copying when the Government is going through
same process at the same time. It seems to the Committee that the more efficient
procedure is for it to bring its views informally
to the attention of the
officers of the Attorney-General’s Department who are working on the
preparation of instructions for
the Office of Parliamentary Counsel. This
approach also accords with the direction to the Committee in its terms of
reference that
it should have regard to any amendments to the Act that the
Government announces are being considered for introduction into the
Parliament.
9.47 When the Government’s legislation is available the Committee will
examine it to see whether there are matters to which
it gives rise that fall
within the Committee’s terms of reference and on which it considers it
should comment. In this report
the Committee confines its consideration to Part
VB as it now stands; that is, the operation of the licence in relation to
copies of print materials.
for copying
9.48 The question of the amount that can be charged for copying of works was
raised in submissions to the Committee. Section 135ZZH(1)(a)
has the effect that
if a copy is sold or otherwise supplied for a financial profit it falls outside
the Part VB licence and becomes
an infringement. The Full Federal Court, in
, determined that an
educational institution that charged for materials supplied to students for use
in courses on a user-pays basis,
with no intention of selling the materials for
a financial profit, did not come within the proscription in the section. The
stated that an institution could build an amount for overheads into the
costs charged for the materials. The Committee does not consider
that any change
needs to be made to the present provision of the Act.
135ZM: copying of artistic works
9.49 Section 135ZM permits the copying of artistic works that are
illustrative of an article or other work that is being legitimately
pursuant to the Part VB scheme. Owners of copyright in artistic works claim that
the provision unfairly denies them a right
to remuneration for
copying. If the artistic work occupies a full page of the item that is being
copied, it seems to the Committee that the copying
of that work is covered by
the copying scheme in s. 135ZL and remuneration is payable for a page in
the totality of that which is
copied. The Committee acknowledges, however, that,
while it is the whole of the artistic work that is being copied, remuneration
will be paid only at the rate for a single page of the text copied. Where the
artistic work takes a form, such as a diagram, not
occupying a whole page, it
seems that remuneration is not payable for the copying of the diagram separately
from and in addition
to the amount that is payable for the copying of the page
on which the artistic work appears.
9.50 Viscopy and CAL argued strongly the injustice of this position and
called for the repeal of s. 135ZM. In its report on the
the Senate Legal and Constitutional Legislation Committee recommended,
‘that the Attorney-General, on the basis of consultation
conducted from
his Department, consider whether it is appropriate to repeal section 135ZM by an
amendment to the Copyright Bill 1997’.
The Government is considering the
recommendation.
9.51 If s. 135ZM were repealed it would be necessary for educational
institutions to pay remuneration under s. 135ZL of the Act, to
for copying the page on which the artistic
work and the text appear. Separate
records would have to be kept of the copying of the literary, dramatic or
musical work being copied
on one hand and the artistic work on the other.
Separate payment arrangements would have to be negotiated. Since it is probable
it would be the whole of the artistic work that was being copied, it would
also be necessary for educational institutions to satisfy
themselves that the
artistic work had not been separately published. Viscopy contemplates
administering such a licence. It points
out that the
Review of Australian
(the Simpson report) recommended the repeal of
The education users
rejected the notion of there being put into operation a second licence scheme
with its own additional record-keeping
or negotiation requirements.
9.52 In practice, as with the present copying of literary works, it could be
expected that a voluntary
licensing scheme building on the provisions of
Part VB would be put into operation.
9.53 The Committee considers that the inability of owners of artistic works
to be remunerated for copying in educational institutions
in the circumstances
contemplated by s. 135ZM seems unfair. Inclusion of the section represents
a pragmatic view that it is only
by allowing free copying of an incidental
artistic work that the licensing scheme can be made to work. The AVCC and TAFE
stated that they would be happy with the repeal of the section if
adequate administrative arrangements to allow remuneration existed.
Queensland suggested that CAL should collect the amount due and pass it to
9.54 The Committee recognises the difficulty of the present situation, but it
does not see an easy way of resolving the problem without
establishing an even
more complex system for recording copying than already exists.
9.55 The Committee considers that the only practical course is for
s. 135ZM to continue to apply to copying unless there exists an
between the educational institutions and Viscopy approved by the Copyright
Tribunal or, failing agreement, a determination
made by the Tribunal that
relates to compensation and the method of collecting of that compensation for
the owners.
9.56 CAL should be involved in these negotiations. It may well be that CAL
could design its returns in such a way that, with minimal
effort, the copying of
artistic works could be noted by institutions when completing the CAL
Recommendation
9.57 The Committee recommends that no change be made to s. 135ZM of
the Act. The operation of the section should, however, be kept
under review. The
section should be repealed if appropriate arrangements are made between the
parties to provide compensation to
owners of artistic copyright in works.
135ZH: copying of published editions
9.58 Section 135ZH provides that the copyright in a published edition of a
work being a work in which copyright does not subsist is
not infringed by the
making of one or more reproductions if the reproduction is made in the course of
making a copy of the whole
or a part of the work by, or on behalf of, a body
administering an educational institution for the educational purposes of that
institution
or of another educational institution. The Australian Mechanical
the basis that
it allowed the copying of expensive high-quality editions of music. The
Committee recognises the justification for
this claim; it can also be made in
relation to the copying of some editions of artistic works. But it is difficult
to see how the
section can be amended to exclude copying of these types of
editions while permitting the copying of more mundane editions as part
educational copying scheme. It seems to the Committee that s. 135ZH must
apply to all published editions or be repealed. If
it were repealed, it would be
necessary to construct a statutory licensing scheme that permits the copying of
published editions
for remuneration; failure to do this could render the present
statutory licence provided by Part VB of the Act ineffective.
9.59 The Committee considers that the exception in s. 135ZH should be
recognised as simply a further restriction on the already limited
that is provided for published editions of works.
Recommendation
9.60 The Committee recommends that no change be made to s. 135ZH of
technical amendments to the licensing scheme
9.61 Interest groups made other representations for amendment of various
technical aspects of the licensing scheme. In the view of
the proponents of
these changes, adoption of such amendments would simplify the operation of the
Part VB licence and so fall within
the Committee’s remit. There is,
however, no agreement between the parties as to the desirability of making the
changes sought.
The scheme represents a compromise between the competing
interests of education users and copyright owners,
Committee is not inclined to make recommendations for any change of a
technical nature in the absence of submissions, from all parties
affected, on
the implications for them of the changes proposed.
10 Copying for people with disabilities
This chapter examines four main issues relating to copying for people with
disabilities. First, the issue of broadcasts by print–handicapped
licensees under s. 47A of the
is examined. The
Committee considers that the section should be extended to apply to sound
broadcasts and other transmissions and
the making available to the public of
programs and materials, but only where the sole purpose of the communication is
to serve people
with a print disability.
The second matter examined is copying of works for print-handicapped
readers and copying of copyright material for intellectually
handicapped people.
It is proposed that the relevant schemes be combined but that the single scheme
remain separate from, rather
than be subsumed by, a general fair dealing
The third matter concerns captioning, which it is proposed should be
recognised under the combined scheme through a statutory licence
preparation of captioned works to assist those with hearing disabilities.
Finally, the chapter proposes that the use of broadcasts by institutions
assisting intellectually handicapped people be included under
the proposed
combined scheme.
10.01 In its issues paper the Committee sought comments on the simplification
of the provisions dealing with copying for people with
disabilities under four
separate headings:
broadcasts by
print–handicapped radio
copying of works for
handicapped readers;
captioning.
Committee explored each of these topics with interested parties at the public
forum conducted in Sydney on 28 April 1997. At the
forum the Committee invited
further submissions on the topics discussed; this resulted in 10 submissions.
The following recommendations
are based on a consideration of all the
submissions received and the comments made at the public forum.
by print–handicapped radio licensees
10.02 Section 47A of the Act provides a statutory licence for the making of
sound broadcasts of published literary or dramatic works,
or adaptations of such
works, by radio stations for print-handicapped people. Operation of the licence
is conditional on the making
and retention of records relating to the broadcast
(ss. 47A(2) and 47A(3)) and allowing the copyright owner access to those
(s. 47A(6)). The copyright owner has the right to claim equitable
remuneration for the broadcast (s. 47A(8)). In the absence of an
the amount of equitable remuneration is determined by the Copyright Tribunal
(s. 47A(8)).
10.03 The Committee’s issues paper asked whether the licence to
print–handicapped radio licensees, provided for under
s. 47A of the
Act, should be extended to encompass other broadcasts and broadcasters.
10.04 Submissions received by the Committee following the public forum also
identified a problem with the operation of the section
resulting from changes
brought about in the
Broadcasting Services Act 1992
. At present the
provisions of s. 47A apply to the holder of a
‘print–handicapped radio licence’. This is defined
s. 47A(11) by reference to licences in force under the
Broadcasting
Services Act 1992
Radiocommunications Act 1992
licence that was granted for the purpose of authorising the making of sound
broadcasts to people unable to handle, read
or comprehend written material. The
Australian Council for Radio for the Print Handicapped (ACRPH) noted that the
distinction between
print–handicapped radio licensees and other
broadcasters no longer exists under the
Broadcasting Services Act
. The Committee
understands that this resulted from the merging of all categories of public
radio licences that existed at the time
of commencement of the
Broadcasting
Services Act
(and which included print–handicapped radio licences) and
their allocation as community licences under
Broadcasting
Services Act
Broadcasting Services (Transitional Provisions and
Consequential Amendments) Act 1992
s. 5(1)(c). As a consequence,
s. 47A clearly applies only to licences granted under the
Broadcasting
, which applied before the
Broadcasting Services Act 1992
came into force
It would be reasonable to assume, however, that
s. 47A also applies to community broadcasting service licences granted
Broadcasting Services Act 1992
Radiocommunications
provided they are for the purpose specified in
s. 47A(11)(b).
10.05 The Committee understands that the primary concern of copyright owners
in relation to the possible extension of s. 47A to include
a wider range of
broadcasters is that other broadcasters reach a much wider audience (of which
print-handicapped people form only
a minority). The Committee also understands
that much of the material broadcast for people with print disabilities is made
selected readings from newspapers. Newspaper publishers are understandably
concerned that if this material were widely distributed
by broadcasters there
could be a significant impact on the circulation of their newspapers. There is
particular concern in connection
with the broadcast of local newspaper reports
by local radio stations.
10.06 The Committee notes the submission by the ACRPH that radio for the
print-handicapped remains a readily identifiable form of
broadcasting. To the
extent that this is the case, it seems unlikely that what the Committee
understands to be the present format
of print–handicapped radio delivery
of newspaper articles would be appealing to many listeners other than
print-handicapped
listeners. It is not clear that the impact on local newspapers
would be any more significant than that on regional or national papers.
10.07 The Committee does not, however, favour allowing the broadcast,
pursuant to an amended s. 47A, of materials by broadcasters
simply on the
basis that some of the broadcasters’ audience includes people with a print
disability. The Committee considers
that the provision should be restricted to
applying in respect of a service or program whose sole purpose is to broadcast
who meet the criteria in s. 47A(11) and that this should be the
case regardless of what type of licence a broadcaster holds. It was
that expansion of the scheme to broadcasts made by other broadcasters would be
difficult to administer; for example, determining
what the equitable rate of
remuneration should be where there is a mixed audience. The Committee notes,
however, that s. 47A currently
provides for the relevant parties to agree
on an amount by way of equitable remuneration and that in the absence of
agreement this
is to be determined by the Copyright Tribunal. It would seem that
this mechanism is sufficiently flexible to enable an amount of
remuneration to be determined, even in circumstances where there is a broadcast
to a mixed audience.
10.08 The Committee notes that it is possible for services for people with a
print disability to be transmitted online—for example,
Internet—as well as broadcast as radio programs. These services may also
attract a wider audience than just those
people with a print disability. In this
situation, the Committee considers that the ‘sole purpose’ test
should also apply
to such transmissions.
Recommendation
10.09 The Committee recommends that s. 47A of the Act apply in respect
of sound broadcasts and other sound
transmissions and the making
available to the public of programs and materials, but only if the sole purpose
of the service or program
is to communicate with people described in
s. 47A(11)(b). The provisions relating to record keeping, inspection and
licence-fee determination
should be retained.
of works for handicapped readers and copying of copyright material for
intellectually handicapped people
10.10 Under the Act two separate schemes exist that permit the copying of
works for handicapped readers and the copying of copyright
material for the
intellectually handicapped. The schemes are set out in detail in ss. 135ZN
to 135ZQ and ss. 135ZR to 135ZT respectively.
The former scheme permits
certain copying of literary and dramatic works by institutions assisting
handicapped readers. To fall within
the scheme an institution must be an
educational institution or be declared to be an eligible institution. The
institution is limited
to making copies of such works in various prescribed
forms provided that the copy is made solely for use by a handicapped reader
the purpose of research, study or self-instruction.
10.11 The latter scheme is broader in its operation: it permits the copying
of published literary, dramatic, musical or artistic works,
sound recordings,
cinematograph films or television broadcasts by institutions assisting
intellectually handicapped people. Use of
the copy, by the institution or
otherwise, must be solely for the purpose of assisting intellectually
handicapped people.
10.12 Under the former scheme copying is permitted only if the person making
the copy is satisfied that it is not possible to obtain
a new copy of the work
in the required format in a reasonable time at an ordinary commercial price.
Similarly, copying under the
latter scheme is permitted only if there is no copy
available commercially within a reasonable time. Both schemes require notice
the copying to be given to the relevant collecting society and the payment of
equitable remuneration on request.
10.13 The former scheme permits the making of a master copy of a literary or
dramatic work for use in the making of copies of the
work for handicapped
readers on the condition that the master copy be destroyed within three months.
Similarly, under the latter
scheme, a copy made for the purpose of making other
copies for intellectually handicapped people must be destroyed within three
10.14 In its issues paper the Committee asked whether the schemes could be
regarded as falling within a general fair use provision.
Although the Committee
did not receive many submissions on this subject, the majority of comments did
not favour the schemes being
subsumed by a general fair dealing/fair use
provision. The Committee is of the view that a case has not been made for
inclusion of
the disability copying provisions in its proposed revised fair
dealing provision.
Recommendation
10.15 The Committee recommends that the provisions for copying for
handicapped readers and intellectually handicapped people remain
identifiable in
the Act and not be merged with the fair dealing provisions.
10.16 The Committee also sought comments on whether the two copying
schemes—for handicapped readers and for intellectually handicapped
people—could be combined. The Committee asked whether, having regard to
the somewhat similar provisions in both schemes, they
might be simplified by
combining them. The overwhelming response from representatives of both copyright
owners and users was that
the two schemes should be combined. CAL submitted that
the provisions concerning equitable remuneration, commercial availability,
record keeping and marking requirements should be retained, while restrictions
on the purpose for which copies could be made should
be relaxed.
10.17 The Committee is of the view that, in the light of the broad-based
support for the combination of the two schemes, this would
be a worthwhile
simplification of the Act.
Recommendation
10.18 The Committee recommends that the current schemes for copying for
handicapped readers and intellectually handicapped people
be combined into a
provision that deals with copying for people with print and intellectual
disabilities.
10.19 The Committee notes that if this recommendation is adopted amendments
to ss. 135ZW(5) and 135ZZH(2) would be necessary.
10.20 Subject to the Committee’s following comments, and taking into
account changes resulting from the combination of the two
schemes, the revised
structure of the provisions could be built on the streamlining of the two
schemes already proposed in Schedule
10.21 The Committee sought comments on a number of specific matters arising
from the proposals in Schedule 6. The following discussion
takes into account
the Committee’s recommendation that the two schemes be combined.
10.22 The release of the Committee’s issues paper and the public forum
occurred before the
was referred for consideration to
the Senate Legal and Constitutional Affairs Committee, which published its
findings on 27 October
1997. The Senate
Committee has accepted the
proposals in Schedule 6 of the Bill. In relation to one matter, however, it has
recommended that the Attorney-General
consider whether the proposal in the Bill to limit the formats covered by
licence to audio, braille and large print: will disadvantage
people with a print
disability by denying them access to electronic copies; and consider whether the
Bill should be amended.
The Committee’s recommendation in paragraph 10.33 deals with this.
matters relating to the copying of works for people with a print disability and
people with an intellectual disability
of a handicapped reader
10.23 Section 10(2) of the Act defines a ‘handicapped reader’
(a) a blind person;
(b) a person suffering from severe impairment of his or her sight;
(c) a person unable to hold or manipulate books or to focus or move his or
her eyes; or
(d) a person suffering from a perceptual handicap;
10.24 The Committee sought comments on whether this definition should be
recast and extended to refer to a person who has ‘perceptual
disabilities which limit their ability to follow a line of print or which affect
concentration’, as suggested by the Round Table on Reading
Materials for People with Print Disabilities Inc. An extension of
the definition
in this form was supported in five of the 10 submissions received (four
submissions did not discuss this question
and one submission queried its need).
The use of the term ‘disability’ to replace the term
‘handicap’ was
also supported in a number of submissions. The
Committee notes that the
implements such a change
in terminology but does not extend the definition beyond its current scope.
10.25 It was also suggested by LISWA that the words ‘or cognitive
disabilities’ be added to the definition to include
intellectually
disabled people. CAL queried the need for the words ‘or other disabilities
which limit their ability to follow
a line of print or which affect their
concentration’ and submitted
that reference to a person with
‘perceptual’ disabilities should be sufficient. CAL also noted that
s. 69(4)(e) of the
New Zealand
, which refers to a person who
‘suffers a handicap with respect to visual perception’ might provide
an appropriate model.
The Committee disagrees. It is of the view that it is
unclear whether the words used are sufficient to include a person with a
disability. Specific reference to such people should therefore be
included in the definition.
Recommendation
10.26 The Committee recommends the recasting and extension of the present
definition of a handicapped reader to include people who
have ‘perceptual
or other disabilities that limit their ability to follow a line of text or that
affect their
concentration’. The Committee also recommends that a
specific reference to people with ‘cognitive disabilities’
10.27 The Committee notes that, under the current definition of a
‘print–handicapped radio licence’ in s. 47A(11)(b),
reference is made to a licence granted for the purpose of making sound
broadcasts ‘to persons who by reason of old age, disability
or literacy
problems are unable to handle books or newspapers or to read or comprehend
written material’. This definition,
in effect, extends the prescribed
audience well beyond that which would be covered by the extended definition
proposed in paragraph
10.26. In particular, s. 47A(11)(b) includes
people unable to read or comprehend due to ‘literacy problems’. A
problem is probably not commonly regarded as a ‘perceptual or
other disability’ that limits a person’s capacity
to follow a line
of text or that affects their
concentration. The Committee notes,
however, that in today’s society a person with literacy problems suffers
from a significant
form of disability.
10.28 The Committee does not believe that the licensing schemes that operate
in relation to the broadcasting and copying of copyright
materials for the
disabled vary to the extent necessary to justify the significant differences in
their permitted audience or users.
The Committee understands that the schemes
are designed to aid people with disabilities in the comprehension of written
Accordingly, it considers that application of the schemes should be
harmonised and that a single definition should be used.
Recommendation
10.29 The Committee recommends the further extension of its proposal for a
recast definition to people who, ‘because of insufficient
literacy or
language skills, cannot comprehend written material’.
10.30 The Committee does not consider that specific reference is needed to
people of ‘old age’, as provided under s. 47A(11)(b):
people who suffer from a disability would be included in the extended
definition. The Committee notes that, in addition to
harmonising the
broadcasting and copying licensing schemes for the disabled, a single definition
would help to simplify the Act.
Recommendation
10.31 The Committee recommends that for the purposes of the Act a
‘person with a print disability’ be defined as
a person without
a person whose vision is
severely impaired;
a person who is
unable to hold or manipulate books or to focus or move their eyes;
a person who has perceptual, cognitive
or other disabilities that limit their ability to follow a line of text or that
affect their
concentration;
who because of insufficient literacy or language skills cannot comprehend
written material.
of formats of works covered by licence

B International obligations
B.1 The rights accorded copyright owners under
Australia’s
derive from a number of
international agreements for the protection of intellectual property to which
Australia is a party. The permissible
scope of any statutory exceptions to those
rights must also be determined by reference to the exceptions allowed for in
those international
agreements.
B.2 For the purpose of considering permissible exceptions to the exclusive
rights of copyright owners, the relevant international
agreements are the Berne
Convention, the Rome Convention and the TRIPS Agreement.
B.3 In December 1996 two new treaties—the WIPO Copyright Treaty (WCT)
and the WIPO Performances and Phonograms Treaty (WPPT)—were
agreed to at
the WIPO Diplomatic Conference on Certain Copyright and Neighbouring Rights
Questions held in Geneva. Neither treaty
is yet in force since in each case 30
countries must have deposited instruments of ratification or accession for this
to occur. Australia
is yet to ratify them, so the obligations they contain do
not apply to Australia. The Committee understands, however, that the Government
will soon be considering whether Australia should become a party to the two new
treaties. For this reason, and having regard to the
Committee’s terms of
reference, which require it to have regard to ‘ongoing developments in the
relevant copyright forums’,
the potential impact of their provisions is
also considered here.
Berne Convention
B.4 The Berne Convention provides for protection of the rights of authors of
literary and artistic works. The works that are protected
are set out in
art. 2 of the Convention. In summary, the Convention requires members to
protect literary works, dramatic works, musical
works, artistic works and
cinematographic works. It does not require protection of sound recordings,
performances or broadcasts.
Permissible
exceptions to the rights of authors under the Berne Convention
B.5 Exceptions to the rights of authors are permitted under arts. 2(8),
, 9.2, 10 and 10
and by compulsory licences provided for in
(3) and 13. Article 9(2) is perhaps the most significant
exception: it applies to the reproduction right of all works protected under
Convention. The remaining exceptions are confined to certain special categories
of works in certain special cases.
B.6 Article 9.2 provides,
It shall be a matter for the legislation in the countries of the Union to
permit the reproduction of such works in certain special
cases, provided that
such reproduction does not conflict with a normal exploitation of a work and
does not unreasonably prejudice
the legitimate interests of the author.
Accordingly, exceptions to or limitations of the reproduction right of
authors are permitted only if three conditions are satisfied.
The Committee
notes that the scope of this provision is still the subject of dispute and that
each of the conditions it contains
raises its own problems of interpretation.
Further, the conditions are cumulative: each must be satisfied before an
exception can
be justified. The first condition is that the reproduction is
allowed only ‘in certain special cases’. This means that
circumstances justifying the making of an exception must be special or
exceptional. A broad kind of exemption would not appear
to be allowed and the
special case must be justified on the basis of clear public policy or other
exceptional circumstances. In his
book on the Berne Convention, Professor
Ricketson notes that the provision is not intended to exclude exceptions that
national legislation at the time of the 1967 Stockholm Revision
Conference, at which art. 9(2) was
introduced.
B.7 Second, the exceptions or limitations are permitted only where they
‘do not conflict with the normal exploitation of a work’.
Ricketson comments that no guidance is given about the meaning of the expression
‘normal exploitation of a work’.
In his view, however, the
expression probably refers simply to the ways in which authors might reasonably
be expected to exploit
their works. The determination of what is normal
exploitation would seem to depend on industry practice and the particular kind
B.8 Third, the exceptions or limitations to the reproduction right should
‘not unreasonably prejudice the legitimate interests
of the author’.
Professor Ricketson notes that any exception to the reproduction right
fundamentally prejudices the interests
of the author and for this reason the
qualification ‘unreasonable’ is added. Professor Ricketson considers
that unreasonable
prejudice can be avoided by the payment of
remuneration—for example, under a compulsory licence. But this does not
a use that conflicts with the normal use of a work can be justified by
the payment of royalties under a compulsory licence: such
a use could not be
regarded as part of the author’s normal exploitation of their work.
B.9 Article 2(8) provides that the protection of the Berne Convention does
not apply to the news of the day or to miscellaneous facts
having the character
of mere items of press information. Article 2
also provides for
possible limitations on the protection of certain works, including political
speeches and speeches given in the
course of legal proceedings, as well as
certain uses of lectures and addresses for informatory purposes.
B.10 Articles 10 and 10
allow certain free uses of works such as
quotations, illustrations for teaching (subject to the requirement that mention
of the source and the name of the author if it appears on the work),
reproduction by the press and the broadcast of news articles,
and the use of
works seen or heard in connection with current events. Article 11
allows member states to determine the regulation of ephemeral recordings made by
broadcasting organisations for their own broadcasts.
Article 13 provides for the
possible limitation of the right of recording musical works and any associated
Rome Convention
B.11 The exceptions provided for in art. 15 of the Rome Convention are
exceptions to the rights of performers (art. 7), phonogram
(art. 10) and broadcasting organisations (art. 13). These exceptions
are facultative. They apply only if provided for by
the national legislation of
the country in which the protection is claimed.
B.12 Article 15 provides,
1. Any Contracting State may, in its domestic laws and regulations, provide
for exceptions to the protection guaranteed by this Convention
as regards:
(a) private use;
(b) use of short excerpts in connection with the reporting of current
(c) ephemeral fixation by a broadcasting organisation by means of its own
facilities and for its own broadcasts;
(d) use solely for the purposes of teaching or scientific research.
2. Irrespective of paragraph 1 of this Article, any contracting state may, in
its domestic laws and regulations, provide for the same
kinds of limitations
with regards to the protection of performers, producers of phonograms and
broadcasting organisations, as it
provides for, in its domestic laws and
regulations, in connection with the protection of copyright in literary and
artistic works.
However, compulsory licences may be provided for only to the
extent to which they are compatible with this Convention.
B.13 The four purposes for which art. 15(1) allows member states, without
qualification, to make exceptions to the rights of performers,
producers of
phonograms and broadcasting organisations are discussed in the following
paragraphs.
B.14 The type of use contemplated by this provision is any use that is other
than public use or use for profit. In relation to performers,
the WIPO guide to
the Rome Convention notes that the exception arises only if their performances
are recorded. With the development
of recording technology since the date of the
Convention, private use (reproduction) of sound recordings and the fixation and
reproduction
of broadcasts has become widely available to the public.

B.15 This provision is modelled on art. 10
of the Berne
Convention. The use must be in connection with reporting current events and it
must be short.
fixations made by broadcasters for their broadcasting
B.16 The purpose of this exception is to meet the needs of broadcasting
organisations for time-delayed broadcasts.
solely for the purpose of teaching or scientific research
B.17 This exception allows member states to refuse protection against uses
that are solely for the purpose of teaching and research.
B.18 In addition, art. 15(2) allows members to provide the same kinds of
limitations on protection under the Rome Convention as they
do with regard to
are provided for in the Convention—see
art. 7.2(2) (‘The use by
broadcasting organisations of fixations made for broadcasting purposes’),
art. 12 (‘The
payment of equitable remuneration to performers and/or
producers of phonograms for the use of phonograms for broadcasting or any
communication to the public’) and art. 13(d) (‘Communication to
the public of television broadcasts if this is done in
places accessible to the
public on payment of an entry fee’).
Phonograms Convention
B.19 The Phonograms Convention is designed to resolve the problem of piracy
of sound recordings. Specifically, it requires member
states to protect
phonogram producers against the making of unauthorised or illicit duplicates of
their phonograms for distribution
to the public. Producers of phonograms are
protected against the making of illicit duplicates, their importation and their
distribution.
B.20 Article 6 of the Convention allows member states to provide the
exceptions that are allowed under art. 15(2) of the Rome Convention.
Compulsory licences are permitted, however, only if the three following
conditions are met.
The duplication is solely for
teaching or scientific research.
licence is valid only for that territory and does not extend to
Equitable remuneration is
Article 7(1) provides a safeguard for copyright and
neighbouring rights protected under any domestic law or international
TRIPS Agreement
B.21 Article 9 of the TRIPS Agreement sets out its relationship with the
Berne Convention. It requires members to comply with arts.
1 to 21 of the
Berne Convention and the appendix, with the exception of art. 6
(‘Moral rights’).
B.22 Article 13 of the TRIPS Agreement provides,
Members shall confine limitations or exceptions to exclusive rights to
certain special cases which do not conflict with a normal exploitation
work and do not unreasonably prejudice the legitimate interests of the right
In effect, the wording of this article extends the application of the
three-step test in art. 9(2) of the Berne Convention so as to
apply to all
the rights of authors of literary and artistic works, not just the reproduction
B.23 Paragraphs 1, 2 and 3 of art. 14 specify the rights to be enjoyed
by performers, producers of phonograms and broadcasting organisations.
14(6) provides,
Any member may, in relation to the rights conferred under paragraphs 1, 2,
and 3, provide for conditions, limitations, exceptions
and reservations to the
extent permitted by the Rome Convention. However, the provisions of Article 18
[‘Works existing on
the Convention’s entry into force’] of the
Berne Convention (1971) shall also apply,
mutatis mutandis
, to the rights
of performers and producers of phonograms in phonograms.
Accordingly, under the TRIPS Agreement Australia may still provide the
exceptions and limitations to the rights of authors of literary
and artistic
works recognised under the Berne Convention and to the rights of performers and
producers of phonograms recognised under
the Rome Convention.
December 1996 WIPO treaties
B.24 The WCT is both a protocol to the Berne Convention (between Berne member
countries that adhere to the WCT) and a free-standing
treaty between Berne
members and non-members and between non-member countries. Article 1(4) of
the WCT also requires non-member countries
to comply with arts. 1 to 21 of
the Berne Convention.
WIPO Copyright Treaty: limitations and exceptions
B.25 Article 10 of the WCT allows contracting parties to enact
‘limitations of’ or ‘exceptions to’ the rights
provides. It does so in the same manner as the three-step test in art. 9(2)
of the Berne Convention (see paragraph B.6). Article
10(1) of the WCT
(1) Contracting Parties may, in their national legislation, provide for
exceptions to the rights granted to authors of literary and
artistic works under
this Treaty in certain special cases that do not conflict with a normal
exploitation of the work and do not
unreasonably prejudice the legitimate
interests of the author.
B.26 The Committee understands that art. 10(1) was the subject of some
debate at the Conference. The debate was a result of concern
by some delegations
that art. 10(1) would introduce inconsistency between exceptions and
limitations under the WCT and those allowed
under the Berne Convention. The
article was, however, agreed to on the basis of an understanding, adopted by the
Conference, that
... the provisions of Article 10 permit Contracting Parties to carry forward
and appropriately extend into the digital environment
limitations and exceptions
in their national laws which have been considered acceptable under the Berne
Convention. Similarly, these
provisions should be understood to permit
Contracting Parties to devise new exceptions and limitations that are
appropriate in the
digital network environment.
Article 10(2) provides,
(2) Contracting Parties shall, when applying the Berne Convention, confine
any limitation of or exceptions to rights provided for
therein to certain
special cases that do not conflict with a normal exploitation of the work and do
not unreasonably prejudice the
legitimate interests of the author.
Article 10(2) requires the three-step test set out in art. 10(1) to be
applied to Berne limitations and exceptions. The effect of
art. 10(2) is
expressed in an agreed statement, adopted by the Conference, that it
‘neither reduces nor extends the scope of
applicability of the limitations
and exceptions permitted by the Berne Convention’.
WIPO Performances and Phonograms Treaty: limitations and exceptions
B.27 The WPPT is a free-standing treaty; it is intended to provide performers
and producers of phonograms with the same protection
as that provided under the
Rome Convention. The Rome Convention and TRIPS of course remain the operative
international instruments
in relation to the rights of broadcasters.
B.28 Article 16(1) of the WPPT carries the substance of art. 15(2) of
the Rome Convention over to the Treaty:
(1) Contracting Parties may, in their national legislation, provide for the
same kinds of limitations or exceptions with regard to
the protection of
performers and producers of phonograms as they provide for, in their national
legislation, in connection with the
protection of copyright in literary and
artistic works.
Article 16(2) provides,
(2) Contracting Parties shall confine any limitations or exceptions to rights
provided for in this Treaty to certain special cases
which do not conflict with
the normal exploitation of the performance or phonogram and do not unreasonably
prejudice the legitimate
interests of the performer or of the producer of the
Unlike art. 15(1) of the Rome Convention, art. 16(2) of the WPPT
does not expand the possibilities for exceptions to, and limitations
rights of performers and producers of phonograms. It simply imposes the
three-step test on the provision of ‘any limitations
of or exceptions to
rights provided by this Treaty’.
B.29 As discussed, Australia’s obligations to authors of literary and
artistic works are set out in the Berne Convention and
the TRIPS Agreement. It
is art. 13 of TRIPS that defines the scope of permissible exceptions to the
exclusive rights of authors.
The Committee notes, however, that art. 2(1)
of TRIPS provides that nothing therein ‘shall derogate from existing
obligations
that Members may have to each other under ... the Berne
Convention ...’ This suggests that authors from Berne Convention
countries remain entitled to the same minimum level of protection, in any
Berne Convention member country, as applied at the time
that country assumed its
TRIPS obligations.
B.30 The rights accorded performers, producers of phonograms and broadcasting
organisations under the Rome Convention, the TRIPS Agreement
and the Phonograms
Convention are all expressed to be subject to the exceptions allowed under the
Rome Convention and it is those
limitations that currently apply. If, however,
the Government were to decide that Australia should become a party to the WPPT
appears that it would no longer be open to Australia to provide for the
specific classes of exceptions listed in art. 15(1) of the
Rome Convention.
As noted in paragraph B.28, art. 16 of the WPPT confines the permissible
exceptions to those allowed under the three-step
C International comparisons
C.1 Free use of copyright material is called
‘fair dealing’ in the United Kingdom and UK-derived legislation
Australian and New Zealand legislation) and ‘fair use’ in
US law. In other legislation it is described as ‘restrictions’
‘exceptions’.
C.2 According to Stewart the special cases of free use are of two kinds:
(i) free use for all classes of works for specific purposes such as reporting
news, research or study, etc.; and
(ii) free use for particular classes of works such as public speeches,
newspaper articles, broadcast commentaries,
C.3 Stewart notes that the various exceptional free uses of copyright
material found under international copyright legislation fall
within five
categories:
(i) exceptions for public speeches, lectures, speeches in legal
proceedings;
(ii) exceptions justified by the desirability of freedom of information in
press and broadcasts;
(iii) quotations (e.g. for the purpose of criticism);
(iv) research or study; and
(v) reproduction for personal or private use.
C.4 Stewart acknowledges that the exceptions were conceived mainly with
manuscript or typewritten copies in mind and that modern technology
has provided
new tools that create major challenges to the reproduction right.
C.5 In analysing the foreign legislation, this Committee report describes
only exceptions to the exclusive rights comprising copyright
that may fall under
a general ‘fair use’ exception. It does not attempt to describe
exceptions covered under remunerated
statutory licensing schemes.
legislation
C.6 Section 107 of the US
contains a non-exclusive
list of factors to be taken into account in determining fair use; this is
similar to the guidelines in s.
40(2) of the Australian
The notable difference between the US and Australian sets of factors is the
existence of s. 40(2)(c) in the Australian Act, which
has no counterpart in
the US legislation. Section s. 40(2)(c) provides for consideration by a
court of ‘the possibility of
obtaining the work or adaptation within a
reasonable time at an ordinary commercial price’.
C.7 Section 107 of the US Act provides
Notwithstanding the provisions of sections 106 and 106A, the fair use of a
or phonorecords or
by any other means specified by that section, for purposes such as criticism,
comment, news reporting, teaching
(including multiple copies for classroom use),
scholarship, or research, is not an infringement of copyright. In determining
the use made of a work in any particular case is a fair use the factors
to be considered shall include—
(1) the purpose and character of the use, including whether such use is of a
commercial nature or is for non-profit educational purposes;
(2) the nature of the copyrighted work;
(3) the amount and substantiality of the portion used in relation to the
(4) the effect of the use upon the potential market for, or value of, the
The fact that a work is unpublished shall not itself bar a finding of fair
use if such finding is made upon consideration of all the
above factors.
C.8 In the United States, the fair use doctrine is a general limitation or
‘equitable rule of reason’
and is not limited, as it is in Australia, to certain specific purposes. Nor
does the US law include the overall quantitative test
for fairness found in
s. 40(3) of the Australian Act, which applies notwithstanding the factors
to be taken into account in determining
fairness in s. 40(2).
C.9 The US provision specifically provides that the fact that a work is
unpublished shall not of itself be a bar to the finding of
a fair use in
relation to the work. This is similar to the Australian position, where no
specific bar is placed on the application
of the fair dealing provisions to an
unpublished work. The fact that a work is unpublished may be considered by a
court under s.
40(2)(b), ‘The nature of the work or
adaptation’, in determining whether a dealing with the work is a fair
dealing. It
should be noted that the quantitative test found in s. 40(3)
applies to published works. The first arm of the quantitative test,
s. 40(3)(a), refers to a ‘periodical publication’; the second
arm of the test, s. 40(3)(b), refers to a ‘reasonable
which is defined as being a reasonable portion of ‘a published
edition’ under s. 10(2). For further discussion
of the current
operation of s. 40(3) see paragraphs 4.11 to 4.14.
C.10 The Committee notes that differences between the US and Australian legal
systems are of particular significance in relation to
the operation of a
‘fair use’-style provision in Australia. According to
, the US law is interpreted by US
courts in a policy context that differs from the one in which the fair dealing
provisions are interpreted
in Australia. Lahore states that in the US the courts
are of the view that ‘Congress has thus far provided scant guidance for
resolving fair use issues’.
courts therefore play a more active role in providing solutions to major
technological innovations, such as reprographic reproduction,
which alter the
market for copyright materials. This must be contrasted with the Australian
position where the Copyright Act was
amended following the Franki report to
introduce a detailed system of copying under fair dealing provisions and
statutory licences
for the specific purpose of implementing the
Government’s policy on reprographic reproduction.
C.11 The Committee notes that one reason the US courts play a more active
role in providing solutions to technological innovation
may be that they are
more likely to adopt what has been called the ‘legal realist’
approach. This has been crucial, for
example, to the rapid development of US
jurisprudence on the scope of copyright protection of computer software. In
contrast, the
Australian courts have been more restrained, the judges being
concerned largely with the interpretation of statutory provisions and
parliament for explicit direction regarding the application of intellectual
property protection to new technologies. The
Committee considers that this
approach by the Australian courts is likely to continue.
C.12 The Committee notes a further difference between the US and Australian
legal systems that is of particular significance in relation
to the operation of
a ‘fair use’-style provision in Australia. The US doctrine of fair
use, and US copyright law generally,
operate against the background of
s. 8, art. 1 of the US
Constitution
, which sets out the purpose of
laws relating to intellectual property as ‘to promote the progress of
science and useful arts,
by securing for limited times to authors and inventors
the exclusive right to their respective writings and discoveries’. In
Australia there is no equivalent statement in the
Constitution
Australia’s copyright legislation must, however, be consistent with the
relevant international copyright conventions to which
Australia is a party.
C.13 The submission received from Mr Brian Fitzgerald of Griffith University
(submission 2) stated that s. 107 of the US Copyright
Act embodies the
essence of US copyright law and in a more general sense the American psyche. It
noted that there are at least three
American principles that are fundamental to
the understanding of s. 107: wealth maximisation, free speech and use, and
private immunity.
legislation
C.14 Under the UK
there is no
general limitation of fair use. Instead, as in the Australian Act, there are a
number of specific purposes relating to
‘fair dealing’. The specific
exceptions defined as a fair dealing are ‘research and private
study’ (s. 29)
and ‘criticism, review and news reporting’
(s. 30). In addition, although not actually defined as a fair dealing,
in works is not infringed by their incidental inclusion in an artistic
work, sound recording, film, broadcast or cable program. Similarly,
there is no
infringement for anything done for the purpose of parliamentary or judicial
proceedings.
C.15 Unlike the Australian Act, no statutory guidance is provided about what
factors are to be taken into account when determining
whether a dealing with a
particular work is in fact a fair dealing. Fair dealing is regarded by the
courts as a matter of fact and
Hubbard v Vosper
Lord Denning
It is impossible to define what is a fair dealing. It must be a question of
degree. You must first consider the number and extent
of quotations and extracts
... Next you must consider the proportions ... Other considerations may come to
mind also. But, after
all is said and done, it must be a matter of
impression.

C.17 It should be
noted that the UK Act provides for fair dealing for the purpose of
‘research and
study’ (s. 29) [emphasis added],

40). The Committee received a number of submissions

C.18 There are other differences between the UK and Australian Acts in
relation to fair dealing. Under the UK Act fair dealing for
the purpose of
research and private study (s. 29) does not apply to the use of a film,
sound recording, broadcast or cable program.
This must be contrasted with the
Australian position, which applies the fair dealing exception of ‘research
under s. 103C to an ‘audio-visual item’, which
is defined in s. 100A as ‘a sound recording, a cinematograph film,
sound broadcast or a television broadcast’.
C.19 In addition, under the UK Act fair dealing for the purpose of reporting
current events does not apply to the use of a photograph
(s. 30(2)). This
restriction is not provided under the related Australian provision (s. 42).
C.20 Finally, the Committee notes that the fair dealing provisions of the UK
Act do not refer to a quantitative test.
C.21 Under the UK Act it is specifically stated that it is not a fair dealing
to convert a computer program expressed in a low-level
language into a higher
level language (s. 29(4)(a)). Special provisions have been introduced for
back-up copying (s. 50A), decompilation
for interoperability (s. 50B),
and other acts permitted to lawful users (s. 50C).
C.22 The exceptions to the exclusive rights comprising copyright (including
the fair dealing provisions) under the UK Act have been
conveniently confined to
one area of the legislation (Chapter III—‘Acts permitted in relation
to copyright works’).
This must be contrasted with the location of the
exceptions under the Australian Act, which are scattered throughout the
legislation.
Zealand legislation
C.23 The fair dealing provisions of the New Zealand
are based on a combination of the fair dealing provisions that exist under the
UK and Australian legislation.
C.24 The general structure of the New Zealand exceptions to the exclusive
rights comprising copyright, including the fair dealing
provisions, is based on
the UK Act. The specific purposes defined as fair dealing under the New Zealand
Act are ‘criticism,
review and news reporting’(s. 42) and
‘research or private study’(s. 43), as also defined under the
C.25 Although worded differently, s. 42 is identical in effect to the
corresponding section of the UK Act (s. 30). Section 43 is also
the related UK provision (s. 29), in that it applies to
study. But s. 43 is broader than the UK provision, which applies only to
literary, dramatic, musical and artistic works and published
editions. In
contrast, s. 43 of the New Zealand Act, and the related Australian
provision (s. 40), apply to all forms of copyright
C.26 Section 43(3) of the New Zealand Act incorporates a list of factors to
be considered in determining whether a dealing for the
purpose of research and
private study is a fair dealing. The factors themselves are very similar to the
factors found in s. 40(2)
of the Australian Act. There is, however, a
notable difference in that under the New Zealand Act the list appears to be
an exclusive set of factors; the Australian factors are drafted as a
non-exclusive list.
C.27 The New Zealand legislation does not include a quantitative test in
relation to the fair dealing provisions.

further exceptions, including ‘incidental copying of
(s. 41) and use of a work in ‘parliamentary and judicial

C.29 Under the New Zealand Act it is not an infringement of copyright to make
a back-up copy of a computer program (s. 80). The Act
does not appear to
provide an exception allowing for decompilation for the purpose of
interoperability.

C.39 Canada’s

purposes relating to ‘fair dealing’ in addition to a set of further
exceptions.
C.40 The purposes described as fair dealing are research or private study
(s. 29), criticism or review (s. 29.1) and news reporting
(s. 29.2). It should be noted that a dealing for the purpose of criticism
or review and news reporting will be regarded as fair only
if the source and the
name of the author, performer, maker or broadcaster are mentioned.
C.41 The Canadian Act does not specify any factors to be considered in
determining whether a dealing is a fair dealing: the factors
are therefore left
to the common law. The Canadian Courts have narrowly construed fair dealing and
consider such factors as the amount
of the original work taken, whether the work
is published, and whether the new work competes with the
C.42 It appears that the Canadian Act does not provide for any quantitative
test in relation to fair dealing.

D Outline of Submissions on fair dealing
Introduction
D.1 In formulating its recommendations the Committee considered the
submissions it received in response to the issues paper entitled
Simplification of the Fair Dealing Provisions of the
as well as submissions that followed the public forum. It also took account of
submissions previously received in response to its
report entitled
Simplification of the
D.2 This appendix outlines the main submissions made to the Committee.
Chapter 6 provides an analysis of the issues raised in these
submissions
and presents the Committee’s response to them.
Simplification
of the fair dealing provisions
D.3 Submissions from users of copyright materials, such as libraries and
educational institutions, suggested the adoption of a US-style
use’ provision that would combine all possible fair dealing exceptions
into a single provision and subject these
exceptions to the same set of
statutory factors for consideration. This notion was commended as a direct means
of simplifying the
Act by reducing its length and applying a consistent approach
to the fair dealing exceptions.
D.4 With the exception of the submission from Publishing and Broadcasting Ltd
(PBL), submissions from broadcasters strongly opposed
adoption of a US-style
fair use provision. The majority submitted that introduction of such a provision
would mean that the traditional
distinction between research or study, criticism
or review and reporting the news would be removed from the Act. It was further
that introduction of a US-style fair use provision would substantially
limit the scope of the current operation of the criticism
or review and
reporting of news fair dealing exceptions. These objections are set out in
paragraphs D.37 to D.42.
D.5 Some submissions from libraries and educational institutions supported
broadening the fair dealing exception by drafting a non-exhaustive
circumstances in which fair dealing may arise. This is the approach used in
s. 107 of the US
. ACLIS stated, ‘Existing fair dealing
categories are inflexible and do not allow certain appropriations of materials
be consistent with the spirit of the fair dealing
[exception].’
D.6 The ACC and the LCA, however, were highly critical of an approach that
would provide a non-exhaustive list of fair dealing exceptions.
considered that the Act’s current approach of exhaustively listing the
specific purposes regarded as falling within
fair dealing ‘promotes much
greater certainty than a US-style ‘fair use’ system ever
D.7 The ACC further questioned whether a broadening of the fair dealing
provision would be in breach of Australia’s international
obligations, as
set out in art. 9(2) of the Berne Convention and art. 13 of the TRIPS Agreement.
These articles require that exceptions
to exclusive rights of copyright be
limited to certain special cases.
D.8 Chapter 6 provides the Committee’s conclusions about the US-style
fair use provision and whether permitting fair dealing
to apply to a
non-exclusive set of purposes will aid in simplifying the Act.
D.9 Other ways of simplifying the Act were put to the Committee. The LCA
submitted that all the fair dealing provisions for all copyright
material could
be collected in one part of the Act and apply to all types of rights.
Submissions from libraries, educational institutions
and the Communications Law
Centre proposed the use of supplementary regulations or guidelines for ancillary
matters such as the operation
of the quantitative test.
D.10 The inclusion of supplementary guidelines or regulations, especially for
a quantitative test, was commended by the NLA as ‘a
that would help simplify
the Act and provide the law with the flexibility to adapt to changes brought by
new technologies. LISWA
noted that ‘guidelines to address the needs of new
technologies would be easier to formulate and amend if not specifically
contained in the Act’ since this would allow ‘issues raised by
rapidly changing technologies to be addressed more
D.11 SBS and the LCA opposed the use of supplementary regulations or
guidelines to accompany the Act. SBS saw the use of such instruments
of merely shifting complexity from one legal document to another or, worse, as
‘an additional layer of
complexity’.
The LCA remarked,
‘The need to make reference to multiple documents (which are often of very
limited availability) will obscure
access, make comprehension more difficult and
increase costs.’
D.12 Paragraphs 6.31 to 6.33 provide the Committee’s assessment of the
advisability in using regulations and guidelines for
varying purposes.
dealing for research or study
quantitative test
D.13 The submissions claimed that the quantitative test provided in
s. 40(3) of the Act and the associated definition of a ‘reasonable
portion’ in s. 10(2) are extremely useful tools that copyright users
regularly rely on to determine what amount of copying
is permitted. The test was
described by libraries and educational institutions as
‘invaluable’
useful practical guide’
widely acknowledged limitation’
and an ‘administrative
LISWA stated that
‘it cannot be emphasised enough that libraries are constantly under
pressure to provide copying guidelines
to users’ and warned that
‘removal of quantitative guidelines altogether could create a chaotic
situation’.
There is strong
support among these bodies for retention of the quantitative test.
D.14 On the other hand, the Australia Council referred in its submission to
the ‘danger’ that the quantitative test poses
by permitting
without reference to the broader principles
of fair dealing provided under
CAL stated that the
quantitative test ‘cannot be reconciled’ with the common law
principles of fair dealing, which emphasise
a ‘case-by-case’
approach. CAL is concerned that the effect of the quantitative test ‘is to
encourage users to
systematically copy the substance of
D.15 Paragraphs 6.53 to 6.66 set out the Committee’s views on the
general application of the quantitative test to copyright
materials in digital
form. The distinction between the quantitative test and the general principles
of fair dealing is discussed
in paragraphs 6.67 and 6.68.
D.16 The quantitative test currently applies only to published literary,
dramatic and musical works. Submissions from libraries and
educational
institutions recognised that the usefulness of this test in its present form is
limited to printed texts such as books
and journals.
D.17 The difficulty of extending the quantitative test to artistic works,
audiovisual items and the digital environment was raised
in many submissions
from by libraries, archives and educational institutions as well as from the
International Map Trade Association,
the Australia New Zealand Land Information
Council, and the LCA.
D.18 In general, copyright users are keen to have comparable tests for other
form, added to the
Act, without interfering with the conventional practices that have developed for
fair dealing with printed texts.
Different materials were suggested as requiring
different quantitative tests.
D.19 Paragraphs 6.53 to 6.66 set out the Committee’s analysis of ways
of extending the quantitative test to copyright material
other than published
literary, dramatic and musical works.
quantitative test and artistic works
D.20 Submissions from users and owners of artistic works protected by
quantitative
test for artistic works, especially maps.
D.21 Concerns raised about the difficulty of estimating 10 per cent
of a mapping product related mainly to instances where maps are
presented in
digital form. The difficulties outlined—such as the potential to
manipulate the measurement of data through rescaling
or compression and the
problem of determining percentages of material stored on databases—are not
unique to maps but apply
to all material reproduced digitally.
D.22 Unlike printed texts such as books and journals, artistic works are not
divided into multiple units (such as pages). Artistic
works are usually
stand-alone works that do not lend themselves to obvious subdivision. The
Committee received no suggestions about
how the quantitative test could apply
generally to artistic works.
quantitative test and audiovisual items
D.23 Section 100A of the Act defines ‘audio-visual item’ as
‘a sound recording, a cinematograph film, a sound broadcast
television broadcast’. The National Film and Sound Archive expressed
reservations about the ease with which audiovisual
items would lend themselves
to a quantitative test. No submitters made suggestions about how a quantitative
test could apply to audiovisual
quantitative test and the digital environment
D.24 The AVCC submitted, ‘The single most difficult issue in relation
to fair dealing is its extension into the digital
environment.’
In its submission,
the APA described the recent development of digital technologies as having
created a ‘new digital paradigm’,
being ‘more in the nature of
a revolution in information dissemination than an
evolution’.
D.25 The AVCC and PBL submitted that there is no theoretical justification
for extending the quantitative test to the digital environment.
The AVCC stated,
‘There can be no doubt that the fundamental proposition underlying fair
dealing is unaltered by the technology
applying to the form of expression which
is the object of protection.’
Similarly, PBL submitted, ‘In an age of proliferation of sources of
communication, the fair dealing defences should apply whatever
technology is
D.26 Submissions from libraries and educational institutions supported
appropriate extension of the quantitative test to the digital
environment. The
Open Learning Institute of TAFE Queensland submitted, ‘Not to extend [the
quantitative test] to digitally
generated text is against the public interest,
punishes bona fide users and in effect quarantines copyright owners from an
of technological
D.27 Submissions acknowledged that the 10 per cent deeming
provision cannot be directly translated to the digital environment because
its fundamental differences with other presentation formats. The LCA noted,
‘The quantitative limits currently contained
in the Act are appropriate,
but require reworking to make them relevant in the digital
environment.’
The Australia
Council submitted, ‘In the digital environment the 10% deeming provision
is redundant.’
D.28 Submissions that dealt with extending the quantitative test to the
digital environment pointed out the difficulty of applying
a quantitative test
to digitally stored material, but none offered suggestions for quantifying this
Restoration
of the word ‘private’ before ‘study’
D.29 Restoration of the word ‘private’ before ‘study’
in ss. 40(1) and 103C was strongly opposed in the submissions
libraries and educational institutions, the LCA and the CLC. Libraries and
educational institutions were opposed to any alteration
to the Act that would
limit the present scope of the research or study fair dealing exception in this
D.30 Copyright owners and organisations representing the interests of
ss. 40(1) and 103C. The Australia Council was of the view that ‘there
is already concern in the arts industry that copyright
owners are unwillingly
subsidising the education system, because of the wide interpretation of
s. 40(1)’ and that ‘reintroduction
of the word
“private” before “study” may adequately tighten this
provision’.
D.31 Submissions suggested that the term ‘private’ is often
understood as distinguishing non-profit from commercial use.
University of Technology objected to the re-insertion of the word
‘private’ in ss. 40(1) and 103C because
‘difficulty of determining a borderline between commercial and
non-commercial research and
In its submission, the
AVCC explained,
It is the nature of research that the outcomes and the future application of
the results are in general unpredictable. To attempt
to qualify research as
‘private’ or otherwise introduces unnecessary uncertainty and places
an unworkable restriction
on an activity which is fundamental to
universities.’
ACLIS noted that the term ‘private study’, if used to denote a
non-commercial dealing, may give rise to confusion since
“private” itself is often used in such contexts as the
“private sector”, rendering the term
“private study”
ambiguous in this sense.’
D.32 The ACC made a suggestion for clearly defining a commercial dealing; it
submitted ‘that research conducted in the course
of employment, or in
return for valuable consideration, be deemed to be not fair
D.33 It also submitted,
... fair dealing for research or study should only be available to a person
making use of copyright material for his or her own research
or study, and ...
should not be available to a person making use of copyright material for another
person or entity (such as a
corporation).
This qualification of the present provision for fair dealing for research or
study is supported by other bodies representing the rights
of copyright owners.
D.34 CAL submitted that it does not support continuance of the fair dealing
exceptions. It contended that, if the research or study
exception were to remain
a recognised exemption from royalty-paid copying, this exception should not
include copying for any commercial
or government use. It proposed qualifying the
provision with not only the word ‘private’ but also the word
‘individual’.
AMPAL submitted, ‘The concept for the purposes of research and study
should not include the ability to make material
available to the
D.35 Further ambiguity in ss. 40(1) and 103C was noted in submissions.

amenable to any clear
distinction’.

terms are interchangeable, it was submitted, little effect would be produced by

dealing for criticism or review and for reporting news
D.37 The submissions the Committee received from copyright users who rely on
the fair dealing exception for criticism or review and
for reporting news were
almost uniformly opposed to any alteration of ss. 41, 42, 103A or 103B.
Only one submitter, PBL, favoured
the introduction of a US-style fair use
D.38 Broadcasters were generally opposed to a US-style fair use provision
chiefly because of concern that well-established industry
practices would be
negated if the fair dealing provision for criticism or review is altered to
include statutory factors such as
those that currently apply to research or
study. Such action, it was claimed, would have an adverse and disruptive effect
industry practice. PBL submitted, however,
Users do generally take the [US] section 107 factors into account in deciding
what is fair. As a result, it is unlikely that the proposed
amendment would have
a dramatic outcome, although it would have the advantage of being simpler to
read, and easier to follow.’
D.39 PBL noted, though, that the terminology used in the current reporting of
news provision refers to ‘for the purpose of,
or associated with, the
reporting of news’. It submitted that, if the words ‘or associated
with’ are not maintained
in the Act, then the scope of fair dealing for
the purpose of reporting news may be limited.
D.40 Paragraphs 6.118 to 6.126 put forward the Committee’s views on
whether the provisions in the Act for the purposes of criticism
or review and
for reporting of news should be subsumed into a single fair dealing
D.41 In its submission to the Committee the ACC referred to the distinction
made in s. 42(1) between reporting of news in a newspaper,
magazine or
similar periodical and reporting of news by means of broadcasting or in a
cinematograph film. When reporting news in
a print medium, sufficient
acknowledgment of an embedded work must be made. This requirement does not apply
when news is reported
by means of broadcasting or in a cinematograph film. The
ACC submitted that there is no justification for the distinction.
D.42 Submissions from broadcasters were unanimous in rejecting a quantitative
test for the purposes of criticism or review and reporting
dealing for the purpose of giving professional advice

Hubbard v Vosper
[1972] 2 QB 84
Under the US Act no distinction is made
between works and subject matter other than works. Section 102(a) of the Act
provides that
a ‘work’ takes in the following categories:
1. literary works;
2. musical works, including any accompanying words;
3. dramatic works, including any accompanying music;
4. pantomimes and choreographic works;
5. pictorial, graphic and sculptural works;
6. motion pictures and other audio visual works;
7. sound recordings;
8. architectural works.

Hubbard v Vosper
[1972] 2 QB
On this point the Committee agrees
with the comments made in the submission from Publishing and Broadcasting Ltd
(PBL) to the effect
that in practice the media make reference to the current
statutory factors listed in s. 40(2) and that to include these factors in
relation to criticism or review will not substantially change current practices
in the media industry (see Appendix D, paragraph
Section 10(2) provides,
‘Without limiting the meaning of the expression “reasonable
portion” in this Act, where
a literary, dramatic or musical work is
contained in a published edition of that work, being an edition of not less than
a copy of part of that work, as it appears in that edition, shall be
taken to contain only a reasonable portion of that work if the
pages that are
copied in the edition:
(a) do not exceed in aggregate, 10% of the number of pages in that edition;
(b) in the case where the work is divided into chapters exceed, in the
aggregate, 10% of the number of pages in that edition but contain
only the whole
or part of a single chapter of the work.’
See, for example, fair dealing
submission from ACLIS, 20.02.
Suppose a digital literary work
consisted of 20 standard-sized pages of text within which was embedded an
illustrative digital image
of average resolution the size of a page. The amount
of information needed to display the graphic (for example, a jpg file of 160K)
would be approximately equivalent to the amount of information needed to display
the text (a text file of 160K)). If the 10 per cent
test were applied
to this work in printed form, only two pages could be copied, regardless of
whether or not the pages consisted
of the text, the image, or both. If, however,
the 10 per cent test were based on 10 per cent of the amount
of digital information
used to display the work, more than two pages containing
text could be reproduced, or only a portion of the image. From this simple
example it can be seen that a test based on the amount of information used to
display a digital work, especially where the work consists
of a number of
different data types, is not effective.
It was recently held, in the case of
Galaxy Electronics Pty Limited
Sega Enterprises Limited and Avel Pty

computer game stored in an integrated circuit fell within the meaning of
‘cinematograph

of copyright owners.

This is the approach taken by the
Committee in connection with the organisation of the fair dealing provisions
(paragraph 6.29),
the libraries and archives provisions (paragraph 7.21) and the
educational copying provisions (paragraph 9.09). The Committee notes
approach to the organisation of copyright exceptions has been taken by the
United Kingdom in the
‘copyright work’ is used in the UK Act to refer to all subject
matter of copyright protection. While these
are all treated together in the UK
Act, the old distinction between works and other subject matter continues to be
relevant. Thus,
all the exceptions to copyright appear in Chapter III of the UK
Act, which is entitled ‘Acts permitted in relation to copyright
works’. Chapter III contains a number of titled but unnumbered divisions,
including the following: ‘General’ (ss.
dealing provisions), ‘Education’ (ss. 32–36),
‘Libraries and archives’ (ss.
‘Miscellaneous: literary, dramatic, musical and artistic works’
(ss. 57–65), ‘Miscellaneous:
sound recordings, films and
computer programs’ (ss. 66–67) and ‘Miscellaneous:
broadcasts and cable programmes’
(ss. 68–75).
